thanked for her review of this report. ii   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  iii Executive summary 
The purpose of this project is to gain a better understanding of the magmatic history of the East 
Kimberley, and to allow for a better evaluation of its prospectivity for magmatic Ni-C u-(PGE) as 
well as reef-style V-T i-Fe-PGE deposits. In order to do so, a multidisciplinary approach was taken, 
focusing on two of the major mineralised systems already known of in the area: the Speewah 
Dome and the Savannah magmatic systems. Thanks to the collaboration of researchers with a 
wide scientific expertise, this project combined results from: geochemistry, mineralogy, 
mineral chemistry, traditional geological mapping and logging, isotopic studies (S, O), 
geochronology, and magnetic (paleomagnetism and magnetic anisotropy) studies. The key 
results obtained are summarised below: 
•Lack of any detectable ∆33S anomaly in the Savannah and Speewah systems, which is not  
only in agreement with the lack of exposed Archean sedimentary rocks in the vicinity of the
deposits, but also points to a more fundamental lack of interaction between the magmas
emplaced at 1700-1800 Ma and the inferred presence of an unexposed Archean core in the
Kimberley Block.
•Results of a detailed study of the isotopic and geochemical composition of the mafic-
ultramafic intrusions of the Savannah magmatic system indicate that the entire intrusive
suite, with the exception of the early mafic granulite dikes, was derived from a similar parent
magma composition of basaltic composition.
•Trace element geochemical signatures of the various intrusions imply that this parent
magma probably had a supra-subduction zone origin, most likely in a back-arc setting. There
is no evidence of a mantle plume origin. A limited amount of crustal contamination is
indicated, but the main contaminant does not appear to have been the immediately adjacent
Tickalara metamorphics. The episodic nature of the mafic magmatism may be related to
mantle upwelling associated with slab break off events.
•Palaeomagnetic-AMS-logging, geological mapping and high precision dating gave us a
better  understanding of the geometry, spatial and temporal relationship between the
Savannah  and Savannah north mineralised intrusions. High precision TIMS dating
highlights a small  difference in age, with the Savannah intrusion being dated at 1835.6
+-0.5, 0.9 to 3.1 Ma older than Savannah north (1833.6+-0.6). Paleomag and AMS logging
also highlight the fact that Savannah and Savannah North have undergone distinctly
different 
degrees of post-emplacement deformation. Savannah shows evidence for a
northerly rotation event not recorded by Savannah North, despite their close proximity and
similarity in age. This is partly  explained by a difference in the intrusion’s shapes influencing
their response to strain, but it  can also be explained by the small age difference between the
two intrusion, as well as being a consequence of syn-tectonic emplacement in a
transpressive fault system in the early  stages of the Halls Creek orogeny.
•The mineralised Copernicus intrusion was identified as being part of a much younger
magmatic event by U-Pb dating (1823 Ma).iv   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Mineralogical or geochemical indicators of prospectivity of individual intrusions are limited to 
moderate degrees of Ni depletion in olivines from the lower portion of the Savannah North 
intrusion. This signal is not evident in the (so far apparently) unmineralised Dave Hill and Wilson’s 
intrusions, but this absence should not be taken to downgrade the potential of these intrusions. 
This project concludes that Paleoproterozoic arc and collisional environments should be recognised 
as prospective and conducive to the formation of intrusion-hosted orthomagmatic Ni-Cu deposits, 
and that the major driver behind the prospectivity of these intrusions appears to be their large-scale 
tectono-magmatic setting, opening up the entire Central Zone as a prospective area for Savannah 
and Savannah North –style systems. 
Prospective targets are small conduit-style intrusions containing poikilitic olivine and/or olivine-
plagioclase cumulates, in close proximity to tectonic discontinuities not necessarily recognisable as 
brittle faults, with ages anywhere in the 1845-1820 Ma range. 
The identification of Copernicus as much younger than Savannah at 1823 Ma appears to indicate 
that a whole new generation of intrusions, intruding in a post-collisional setting synchronous with 
the Halls Creek Orogen, can now be considered as potentially prospective for intrusion-hosted Ni-
Cu-(PGE) mineralisation. 
In order to further our understanding of the magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East 
Kimberley, the following research alleys are recommended:  
•TIMS dating and further geochemical/mineralogical characterisation on more intrusions in 
the vicinity of Savannah.
•Better characterisation of the Tickalara metamorphics (lithostratigraphy, geochem, S 
isotopes)
•O isotopic work on other intrusions in the vicinity of Savannah.
•Thermochronology –  identification of tectonic domains based on pressure-temperature-
time paths.
•Palaeomagnetic work on the Speewah dome.
•Further work on 6-element PGE concentrations in massive and disseminated ores from 
Savannah and Savannah North, and S isotopes on trace disseminated sulfides in Dave Hill  
and Wilson’s, to further elucidate the sulfide incorporation and segregation history in the 
mineral system.
•General magnetics work on the remanent intrusions in the area, e.g., MacIntosh Layered  
Intrusion.  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  1 Part I Introduction 2   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  1 Project Description  
The East Kimberley region contains two large mafic magmatic provinces, both of which contain 
magmatic mineralization. The first magmatic province in t he Halls Creek Orogeny is composed of  an 
extensive suite of mid -crustal intrusions ranging from chromite - and PGE -bearing dunite -
anorthosite complexes (e.g. the Panton Sill) to peridotite -gabbronorite layered intrusions, two of 
which (Savannah, Copernicus) host economic Ni -Cu-PGE d eposits. In both magmatic suites, the key 
exploration question is in identifying potentially ore hosting intrusions from a large number of 
potential targets. The second magmatic province, which is t he Hart Dolerite suite , is located in the 
adjacent Kimberl ey Basin  and is a typical large igneous province consisting primarily of extensive 
dolerite sills. One of these, the Speewah Complex, hosts currently sub economic stratiform V -Ti 
mineralization with potential for PGE sulfide reef style deposits.  
Magmatic Ni-Cu-PGE sulfide deposits are a major source of the world’s resources of these 
commodities, and as a class include some of the world’s most valuable individual deposits and 
camps. Some of the larger deposits in the Noril’sk (Siberia) and Sudbury (Canada) camps contain 
many hundreds of billions of dollars’ worth of metals. They are extremely attractive but notoriously 
difficult exploration targets.  
Exploration for this style of deposit in Proterozoic mobile belts has recently received a major boost 
in Austr alia following the discovery of the Nova deposit in greenfields terrane in Western Australia, 
and also with exciting new discoveries and deposit extensions in the Musgrave province also in WA. 
However, detailed camp -scale targeting and exploration for this  deposits remains extremely 
challenging and new approaches are required.  
Globally, mafic intrusion -hosted nickel accounts for around 80 -85 % of hard -rock nickel sulfide 
resource, the rest being hosted in komatiites. Of the major (>1 million tonnes Ni) indi vidual deposits 
in the world, almost all are mafic intrusion hosted (Perseverance, Mt Keith and Thompson being the 
only exceptions). A tiny proportion of the global mafic intrusive -hosted resource lies within 
Australia, and the major part of this share is in the Nebo -Babel deposit, which along with the Nova 
discovery in the Fraser Range is one of the last major truly greenfields nickel exploration successes 
in Australia. The Savannah (formerly Sally Malay) deposit in the East Kimberley also falls in this 
category.  
Nebo -Babel is an instructive example. It was discovered by WMC following an entirely conceptual 
targeting model: location of deposits within large igneous provinces at Archaean craton boundaries. 
It is almost blind, and was picked up on the basis o f a single soil sample. Had it been under two 
metres of transported cover it would not have been found. It is almost unique among Ni sulfide 
deposits in showing no signal of crustal sulfur assimilation, being in a very small intrusion, and 
showing essentia lly no distal footprint. There are almost certainly more of these deposits to be 
found elsewhere in Australia. What we currently lack is a predictive model, at anything other than 
gross regional scale, to target them.  
Many exploration programs in Australi a have intersected mafic intrusions, but finding intrusions is 
only part of the battle. We have to find the right ones. A significant advance in recent years is the Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  3 recognition that ore deposits are associated with very particular kinds of intrusion types such a s 
chonoliths, bladed dykes, or funnels that formed at high-throughput magma conduits within much 
larger magmatic provinces developed in particular structural settings (Barnes et al., 2015 ; Lightfoot 
and Evans-Lamswood, 2015 ; Saumur et al., 2015). Nebo-Babel is an excellent example of a 
chonolith, whereas Savannah is thought to be an example of a bladed dyke, and they both represent 
areas of focussed high magma throughput within a large magmatic province. 
The critical decisions to be made are the standard issues in greenfields exploration to do with 
extrapolating geological intelligence from sparse data. In this case, the questions are: how to 
distinguish a potentially mineralized intrusion (or suite of intrusions) from a barren one; how to 
recognise the edge of a significant orebody from a subtle indication; how to recognise the 
potentially fertile parts of a magmatic plumbing system; how to make the most intelligent and 
informed decision about the walk-away point, or, more optimistically, the prospect to project 
investment point. The lack of reliable answers to these questions constitute a significant barrier to 
exploration success, which can only be overcome by improved process understanding. We address 
these questions in the specific case of the two adjacent large igneous provinces in the East Kimberley 
region, with a view to improving geological understanding and exploration success in that specific 
province and in the continent as a whole. 4   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  2 Project Aims  
This project is divided in two main study areas,  the Speew ah Dome region and the Savannah and 
associated intrusions area. Both magmatic provinces have shown evidences of the presence of 
economical mineralisation associated with mafic -ultramafic intrusions, such as the Speewah 
deposit, 4.7 Bt of dissemin ated V -Ti-Fe oxide mineralisation  and a reef anomalously enriched in 
platinum group elements , or the massive Ni -Cu-(PGE) rich sulfide mineralisation present within the 
Savannah intrusion.  By studying these two systems in more details, we hope to gain a bet ter 
understanding of these mineralised systems. By acquiring knowledge on the age, origin, geometry, 
composition and time -tectonic evolution of these system, we can build a more realistic, large -scale 
genetic models which can be used in further exploration  in orogenic belts around the world.  
The main research questions and aims for this project can be dis sected in the following way:  
1. Establish the petrogenetic and geochronological relationship between the Savannah, 
Savannah North, and other mafic -ultramafic  intrusions in their vicinity (Sub -chamber D, 
Copernicus, Dave Hill, Wilsons).  
2. Characterise the parent al magma compositions and internal evolution of Savannah and 
Savannah North intrusions, and extrapolate to mineralisation potential of other nearby 
mafic -ultramafic intrusions in the East Kimberley province.  
3. Evaluate the mineralisation potential of other nearby mafic -ultramafic intrusions in the East 
Kimberley province.  
4. Fingerprint the source and nature of sulfur in the PGE reef associated with the Hart Dole rite 
intrusion at Speewah  
5. Application of findings to targeting models for intrusion -hosted  Ni-Cu-PGE deposits 
applicable to other craton margin orogenic settings in Western Australia, specifically 
Musgrave and Albany -Fraser terranes.  
6. Investigation of geoch emistry and alteration assemblages within multiple Cu - and Au bearing 
vein suites in the vicinity of the Hart Dolerite; employing Tornado mapping, SEM, 
petrography and whole -rock geochemistry.  
  
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  5 3 Geological Background  
3.1 Geology of the East Kimberley  
The Kimb erley Region of Western Australia is dominated by the Paleoproterozoic Kimberley and 
Speewah Basins. The southern and eastern margins of these two basins unconformably  overlies 
older Proterozoic rocks of the Lamboo Province (Grey and Griffin, 1990 ; Phillip s et al., 2016b ; 
Sheppard et al., 2012 ; Thom, 1975 ; Tyler et al., 2012 ). The northeast trending Halls Creek Orogen is 
comprise d of rocks from the Lamboo Province and the deformed margins of the Kimberley and 
Speewah basins (Grey and Griffin, 1990 ; Griffin and Grey, 1990 ). In current tectonic models, the 
Halls Creek Orogen is interpreted as the result of the collision between the Kimberley Craton and 
the North Australian Craton during the Halls Creek Orogeny at 1832 -1808 Ma (Griffin et al., 2000 ; 
Page et al. , 2001 ; Sheppard et al., 2012 ; Tyler et al., 1998a ). This period of crustal accretion was 
characterised by widespread volcanism and the emplacement of pre, syn and post tectonic intrusive 
(granitic to ultramafic) complexes over a zone of several hundred ki lometres. The Lamboo Province 
is divided by major north -northeast trending strike -slip faults  (Figure 1 ) defining boundaries 
between the Western, the Central and the Eastern terranes (Griffin and Tyler, 1993 ; Tyler et al., 
1995 ). 
Our study focuses on 1) sa mples from the Hart dolerite  suite , 2) samples from various Cu -Au-Ag 
prospects within the  Speewah dome , both located in the Western zone  of the Lamboo Province, as 
well as 3) samples from the Savannah Ni deposits and multiple  intrusions in the vicinity, lo cated in 
the Eastern zone  of the Lamboo province ( Figure 1 ). 
3.2 Western Zone (1879 -1810 Ma)  
The oldest exposed rocks in the Western Zone are greenschist to granulite facies turbiditic 
metasedimentary rocks of the Marboo Formation and Mount Joseph migmatite, w ith the former 
dated at 1872  ± 9 Ma (Tyler et al., 1999 ). Felsic volcanic fragments indicate a magmatic arc or 
orogenic  source  (Griffin and Tyler, 1992 ; Tyler et al., 1999 ). Meta -dolerite and  metagabbro sills of 
the 1872 -1861 Ma Runis Dolerite intrude the Marboo Formation  (Griffin et al., 1992 ; Griffin and 
Tyler, 1992 ; Tyler et al., 1999 ). The 1864 -1852 Ma Paperbark Supersuite intrudes the Marboo 
Formation and Ruins Dolerite, and comprises the Whitewater Volcanics, high -level porphyry 
intrusions and volumin ous plutonic rocks  (Griffin et al., 1992 ; Griffin and Tyler, 1992 ). The ca. 1855 
Ma Whitewater Volcanics are considered to be volcanic and sub -volcanic equivalents of the plutons 
of the Paperbark Supersuite. The y occur over a strike length of >600 km and a re associated with 
high -K, I-type granites (monzogranites and syenogranites, lesser tonalite). Associated mafic pluton 
rocks include biotite -bearing norite, gabbronorite, gabbro and diorite  (Griffin et al., 2000 ). 
The Western and Central zones experienced two phases of deformation and metamorphism: (1) 
Hooper Orogeny, interpreted to record the collision of an oceanic arc, or exotic continental 
fragment  (Griffin et al., 2000 ; Tyler et al., 2012 ), represented by the Central Zone, with the rocks of 
the Western  Zone deposited on an inferred passive margin of the Kimberley Craton. In the Western 
Zone, this event is recorded by overprinting of the Marboo Formation and Ruins Dolerite by a layer 6   |  Magmatic sulfide min eral potential in the East Kimberley  parallel foliation and greenschist  facies metamorphism marking D 1/M 1 at 1870 -1864 Ma  (Griffin et 
al., 2000 ; Tyler et al., 1999 ). A second deformation event (D 2/M 2) was ac companied by greenschist -
granuli te metamorphism before and during intrusion of the Paperbark Supersuite. Younger 
magmatism associated with  the Sally Downs Su persuite intrude d the entire Lamboo Province at ca. 
1832 -1808 Ma, in the province -wide Halls Creek Orogen (see later).  
3.3 Central Zone (1865 -1810  Ma) 
The Tickalara metamorphics dominate the Central Zone and consist of upper amphibolite to 
granulite facies maf ic volcanic and intrusive rocks with interlay ered metasedimentary rocks. The 
presence of pillow lavas and turbidites suggest a marine setting. Detrital  zircons from this formation 
provide a maximum depositional age of ca. 1865 Ma  (Bodorkos et al., 1999 ). Mafic rocks in the lower 
part of the sequence are MORB -like in composition  (Griffin et al., 2000 ; Page et al., 1994 ; Sheppard 
et al., 1999 ). 
Deformation events in the Central Zone as similar for those described in the Western Zone, however 
Central Zone even ts are younger, with D1/M1 occurring at 1863 -1850 Ma as evidence by folding in 
the Tickalara metamorphics. Further  folding and medium -high temperature metamorphism 
affected the Tickalara at ca. 1845 ma forming D2/M2, suggesting the Western And Central Zone s 
were amalgamated by this time  (Bodorkos et al., 1999 ). 
Back -arc extension or arc extension at 1845 -1840 Ma in the southern Central Zone led to deposition 
of marine sedimentary rocks and bimodal volcani c rocks of the Koongie park Formation  (Orth, 2002 ). 
Clasts of metamorphic and gra nitic rocks within sedimentary rocks of the Koongie Park Formation 
suggests a continental source. This formation was metamorphosed to greenschist facies and folded 
followed by intrusion of the 1827 -1808 ma Kevin ’s Dam Suite of t he Sally Downs Supersuite during 
the 1832 -1808 ma Halls Creek Orogeny. The Tickalara and Koongie Park Formations host mafic -
ultramafic intrusions that are the subject of this paper.  Page and Hoatson (2000)  identified three 
main episodes of mafic -ultramafic  activity at ca. 1855, 1845 and 1830 Ma.  
3.4 Eastern Zone (1912 -1788  Ma) 
The Eastern Zone of the Lamboo Province records magmatism, volcanism and sedimentation on the 
western margin of the North Australian Craton from c. 1912 to 1788 Ma. The oldest exposed roc ks 
in the Eastern Zone comprise the Sophie Downs Suite (including the Ding Dong Downs Volcanics) 
which record passive margin magmatism and sedimentation between c. 1912 and 1904 Ma. The 
unconformably overlying Halls Creek Group consists of four formations that (in ascending order) are 
the fluvial Saunders Creek Formation, lacustrine or shallow -marine Brim Rockhole Formation , 
shallow -marine Biscay Formation and deep -marine Olympio Formation.  
The Saunders Creek Formation and Brim Rockhole Formation metasands tone units contain detrital 
zircons dominated by Archean age components, whereas Biscay Formation metasandstone units 
contain detrital zircons with dominant Paleoproterozoic age components. Furthermore, calcareous 
and dolomitic metasedimentary rocks in the  Brim Rockhole Formation are intercalated with felsic 
metavolcanic rocks dated at c. 1881 Ma, interpreted as the age of volcanism.  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  7 The Biscay Formation is younger with constituent psammitic and pelitic metasedimentary rocks 
yielding a maximum depositional age of c. 1861 Ma. Both the Brim Rockhole Formation and Biscay 
Formation include intercalated basaltic metavolcanic rocks. Overall, metabasalt rocks have similar 
whole-rock geochemistry and fall on a trend consistent with progressive fractionation, increasing 
crustal contamination from c. 1881 to 1861 Ma.  
The Olympio Formation is disconformable or unconformable on the Biscay Formation and 
represents marine transgression accompanying subsidence. Turbidite metasandstones in the lower 
Olympio Formation contain 1875 –1860 Ma detrital zircon grains with unradiogenic Hf values. These 
data are similar to turbidite metasandstones from the 1864 –1840 Ma Killi Killi Formation in the 
Granites –Tanami Orogen to the southeast that also contain detrital zircons with dominant age 
components at 1870 –1860 Ma. Phillips et al. (2016b) suggests a large turbidite system was active 
along the western margin of the North Australian Craton feeding sedimentary detritus to deep-
marine basins in the Halls Creek and Granites –Tanami Orogens. Based on similar age components 
and Hf isotope data, possible detrital sources for the turbidite system could be the 1864 –1852 Ma 
Paperbark Supersuite in the Western Zone of the Lamboo Province or the 1867 –1862 Ma Nimbuwah 
Complex in the Pine Creek Orogen. The entire Halls Creek Group and Sophie Downs Suite are 
intruded by the c. 1835 Ma Woodward Dolerite with the onset of the Halls Creek Orogeny at c. 
1832 –1808 Ma. 
3.5 The Hart Dolerite igneous province 
The Paleoproterozoic Hart dolerite is part of a large igneous province comprising an estimated 
250,000 km3 of dolerite and granophyre (Griffin et al., 1993) mainly intruding the Western zone of 
the Eastern Kimberley. The Hart dolerite intrudes sedimentary and volcanic rocks of the Speewah 
Basin, and it has been interpreted as being part of 1820-1790 Ma magmatism event in the Kimberley 
and Tanami regions (Sheppard et al., 2012). Part of this research project focuses on the Speewah 
Dome area, its Cu- Au-Ag hydrothermal occurrences as well as the V- Ti-Fe oxide and PGE reef 
mineralisation present within intrusions forming part of the Hart Dolerite. A detailed geological 
description of the Hart dolerite suite is presented in Part II: Sulfur isotope study of the Hart Dolerite 
Suite. 
3.6 The Hall’s Creek Orogen magmatic pr ovince 
The East Kimberley mafic-ultramafic intrusions which form part of the Hall ’s Creek Orogen (HCO) 
magmatic province are confined to the Central zone of the East Kimberley, and form a well-exposed 
north-north-easterly trending belt about 400 km long and at least 120 km wide (Tyler et al., 1995) . 
Most of the mafic-ultramafic intrusions of the East Kimberley are contained within this HCO 
magmatic province, and it is estimated that these intrusions were emplaced at depths between 8 
and 23 km (Hoatson and Blake, 2000). A detailed geological description of the mafic-ultramafic 
intrusions which are the focus of this research study (Savannah and surrounding intrusions) is 
presented in part IV: Savannah and adjacent mafic-ultramafic intrusions. 8   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley

Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  11 1 Introduction  
Sulfur resides in the Earth’s mantle, crust and hydrosphere but is locally concentrated in mineralised 
systems typically associated with ore deposits, where it acts as the primary comp lexing ligand in the 
formation of sulfide minerals. Mantle - and crustally -derived magmas have brought large quantities 
of economic metals from the Earth’s interior to the near surface, and hydrothermal fluids have 
remobilised and re -precipitated these meta ls within the crust as different sulfides. The sulfur itself 
may be sourced from a variety of compositional reservoirs, each with distinct isotopic compositions. 
Mixing and interactions with the mantle, crustal magmas, hydrothermal fluids, country rocks, o r 
meteoric waters imparts specific isotopic signatures, resulting in minerals with a range of isotopic 
compositions. In the study of mineralised magmatic intrusions, traditional mass dep endent 
fractionation of sulfur, 
monitored as   𝛿34𝑆=( 34𝑆

has been used extensively to investigate the geochemical reservoirs from which sulfur is sourced 
and the processes controlling the spatial and temporal composition of hydrothermal fluids 
(Hodkiewicz et al., 2009 ; LaFlamme et al., In Revision ; Palin and Xu, 2000 ) and/or magmas (see 
below) and their associated mineral deposits. More recently, mass  independent fraction of sulfur,

has been utilised to unravel as an indelible tracer to fingerprint source and image the pat hway of 
sulfur in a wide range of mineral systems  (Bekker et al., 2009 ; Chang et al., 2008 ; Fiorentini et al., 
2012b ; Hiebert et al., 2013 ; Lesher and Groves, 1986 ; Penniston -Dorland et al., 2012 ; Selvaraja et 
al., in revision ; Sharman et al., 2013 ). 
Const raining the sulfur isotopic signature of sulfides from magmatic -hydrothermal mineral systems 
is useful in delineating the source of sulfur, and is an important parameter to understand how, when 
and (most importantly from a targeting perspective)  where sulfur saturation occur (e.g., Evans et 
al., 2014 ). In addition, such data provide a better understanding of the geodynamic environment in 
which the mineralising process occurs (e.g., Chen et al., 2015 ; Giacometti et al., 2014 ; LaFlamme et 
al., 2016 ), which im pacts on the targeting rationale applied during exploration (e.g., Fiorentini et al., 
2012b ). 
Sulfides play a key role in the formation of numerous world -class magmatic Ni -Cu-PGE deposits, 
numerous studies have looked at the mass -dependent (δ34S) sulfur isotopic composition of 
magmatic deposits and associated S -bearing country rocks in order to resolve what process triggers 
sulfide saturation  and subsequent ore formation, (e.g., Ripley et al., 1999 ). More recently, mass -
independent (Δ33S) sulfur anomalies were utilised to constrain ore -forming processes associated 
with komatiites (e.g., Bekker et al., 2009 ; Fiorentini et al., 2012b ; LaFlamme e t al., 2016 ) as well as 12   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  mafic intrusions (e.g., Fiorentini et al., 2012a ) and layered intrusions (e.g., Penniston -Dorland et al., 
2008 ). 
The aim of this S isotopic study of the Hart Dolerite is to provide a picture of the evolution of th is 
magmatic systems  both in the context of the ore forming processes operating at the time of 
emplacement, and more broadly in the framework of the volatile recycling processes that operated 
along this craton margin setting in the Mesoproterozoic.  
  
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  13 2 Geological Background  
Spee wah is a richly mineralised, 15 km x 30 km doubly plunging anticline on the eastern margin of 
the Paleoproterozoic Kimberley and Speewah Basins, which exposes a unique window into the 
regional geology of one of the least known areas of the North Australian  Craton. The earliest 
geological history records the syn -orogenic, shallow -water deposition of immature Speewah Group 
siliciclastic rocks, where sandstones of the Tunganary Formation fine upwards to the Valentine 
Siltstone, which contains ~1834 Ma zircons,  and then coarsen to the Lansdowne Arkose. These were 
intruded by three sub -alkaline and alkaline mafic and then two smaller felsic units with an inferred 
age of ~1799 Ma. These magmatic intrusions form a sill that is part of the Hart Dolerite, which local ly 
hosts 4.7 Bt of disseminated V -Ti-Fe oxide mineralisation  and a reef anomalously enriched in 
platinum group elements. In this study we investigated the multiple sulfur isotope signature of this 
intrusion ( Figure 3). 
In the following section,  we provide some key informati on about the lithological units.  The rocks 
that make up the intrusion are shown in Figure 4 and are discussed below, from the stratigraphic 
bottom to the top.  14   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley

Magmatic sulfid e mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  15 2.1 Spotted magnetite gabbro  
As the basal member of the sill, the spotted magnetite gabbro (Figure 4) is a medium to coarse -
grained gabbro characterised by 55  % plagioclase, 30 -40 % calcic pyroxene, 5  % Fe –Ti oxides and up 
to 5 % olivine with minor amounts of fine -grained quartz, K -feldspar, biotite, epidote, apatite and 
sulfide, and traces of very fine -grained zircon and badelleyite. There is a sharp, chilled,  irregular 
contact with the underlying sediments, which is not exposed but has been intercepted in nine drill -
holes. At the contact, the gabbro grades from aphanitic to medium -grained over a 1 -2 m interval. 
Above the chilled margin is 50  m of medium to coa rse-grained, ophitic -textured gabbro, where 
oikocrystic clinopyroxene encloses abundant, finer -grained, euhedral laths of plagioclase. This zone 
contains up to 5  % olivine that is commonly pseudomorphed by a fine -grained mixture of chlorite 
and Fe -oxides. Also present are scattered medium -grained aggregates of ilmenite and titano -
magnetite. Above the ophitic interval, the gabbro grades into sub -ophitic rocks dominated by 
plagioclase and augite. There are initially minor amounts of interstitial, anhedral agg regates of Fe –
Ti oxides that consist of a euhedral to subhedral ilmenite core overgrown by magnetite. Then in the 
upper 50 m of the sill, the minor amounts of Fe –Ti oxides become coarser -grained and exhibit a 
strongly poikilitic texture. This produces a di stinctive spotted appearance on weathered outcrop.  
 16   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   
Figure 4: Photos of the gabbro and granophyre phases at Speewah: a) Spotted Magnetite Gabbro outcrop, b) 
Disseminated Magnetite Gabbro outcrop, c) flow -aligned xenoliths within the basal part of the Dis seminated 
Magnetite Gabbro, d) Pegmatoidal Gabbro outcrop, e) cut core samples of Coarse Felsic Granophyre (HQ core – 64 
mm wide), f) cut core samples of Mafic Granophyre (HQ core – 64 mm wide).  
 
 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  17 2.2 Disseminated magnetite gabbro  
Overlying the spotted magnetit e gabbro with a sharp, regionally conformably but locally irregular 
contact is a second laterally persistent magnetite gabbro  (Figure 4). It is 100 m thick and 
characterised by 10 -20 % disseminated Fe –Ti oxides near the base that decreases upwards to less 
than 1  % near the upper contact. The Fe –Ti oxides are characterised by a core of subhedral ilmenite 
that hosts myrmekitic silicate inclusions. The ilmenite is irregularly overgrown by subhedral 
titanomagnetite with ilmenite exsolution -lamellae that produce s a distinctive “peppered” texture 
on weathered outcrop. The Fe –Ti oxides are commonly associated with very fine -grained, anhedral 
aggregates of pyrite and chalcopyrite. About 15 -25 m above the base of the gabbro there is an 
increase in the chalcopyrite co ntent, which is associated with a laterally persistent PGE + Au horizon. 
The maximum concentration of PGE + Au over 10 cm is approximately 700 ppb at the base of the 
zone. However, anomalism in these elements extends upwards over an interval of about 10 m.   
The remainder of the disseminated magnetite gabbro consists of 30 -40 %, medium to coarse -
grained, locally flow -aligned laths of plagioclase and 30 -35 % blocky to elongate augite showing well 
developed lamellar and granular -exsolution of pidgeonite. Inter stitial to these minerals is either 
fine-grained K -feldspar or granophyric intergrowths of quartz and K -feldspar, which increase from 
traces to 1 -2 % near the top but may include an upper 0.5 m felsic contact dominated by granophyric 
material. Other trace minerals include olivine, apatite and very fine -grained zircon and badelleyite.  
The relationships of the disseminated magnetite gabbro with overlying rock types at Speewah are 
more variable than for the underlying spotted magnetite gabbro. In places, the u pper contact is 
sharp and planar against sills of pegmatoidal gabbro. Elsewhere, there is a chilled contact with the 
Valentine Siltstone, or a gradation over 30 m to granophyric -textured rocks that also show an 
intrusive contact with the Valentine Siltston e. Another diagnostic feature of the disseminated 
magnetite gabbro is the presence of flow -aligned xenoliths. The xenoliths are up to 30 cm in 
diameter near the base of the gabbro but decrease in size and abundance upwards. Xenoliths are 
composed of either : (i) very coarse -grained orthocumulate gabbros, (ii) medium to fine -grained 
gabbros with abundant, blocky, extensively sericitised plagioclase, (iii) felsic to granitic rocks, or (iv) 
partially resorbed siltstones.  
2.3 Pegmatoidal gabbro  
Sills of pegmatoidal gabbro (Figure 4) are only locally developed and show variable thickness and 
contact relationships. Thin sills cross -cut both the spotted magnetite gabbro and the disseminated 
magnetite gabbro. Larger sills are mapped with an upper contact of granophyre -textured, felsic 
phases of the Hart Dolerite and a lower erosive contact on the disseminated magnetite gabbro. Also, 
in places there are intrusive sills of pegmatoidal gabbro between the spotted magnetite and 
disseminated magnetite gabbro. The pegmatoidal ga bbro is medium to very coarse -grained, 
grayish -green and has an ophitic texture. It contains 40 -50 % plagioclase, 30 -40 % Ca -pyroxene, 5  % 
elongate Fe –Ti oxides, up to 5  % granophyric intergrowths and traces of biotite and zircon.  18   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  2.4 Coarse felsic and mafic granophyres  
The coarse felsic granophyre (Figure 4) outcrops either as a discontinuous, podiform, 5 -20 m thick 
sill above the disseminated magnetite gabbro near the King River Fault or as 1 -2 m wide, N to NNE -
trending dykes intruding the gabbros in the cor e of the study area. The coarse felsic granophyre is 
dominated by massive, medium to coarse -grained, pink K -feldspar that has granophyric 
intergrowths with quartz. Internally, the intergrowths exhibit rectangular, parallel or amoeboid 
textures, which becom e coarser -grained from core to rim. The mafic granophyre is characterised by 
an abundance of sericite and chlorite -altered, dolerite -textured, xenoliths up to 2 cm in diameter. 
These are hosted by a matrix containing up to 40  % fine -grained, subhedral quar tz between areas 
rich in very fine -grained chlorite and sericite. Distributed unevenly throughout the rock are dark 
pink to brown, irregular -shaped, granophyric intergrowths of quartz and K -feldspar to 5 mm and 
minor plagioclase laths that are generally al tered to epidote. The mafic granophyre is less common 
but intimately associated with the coarse felsic granophyre. It outcrops intermittently as pod -like 
sills along the King River Fault and locally occurs as dykes in the same st ructures as the coarse fels ic 
granophyre.   
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in t he East Kimberley   |  19 3 Methodology  
The igneous samples from the Hart Dolerite at Speewah (the host of the PGE reef), used for this 
study, did not contain any sulfide phases large enough to be suitable for in -situ measurement. 
Hence, bulk rock analysis was preferre d. The methodology used for bulk Fluorination Mass 
Spectrometry was as follow:  
Sulfide-bearing samples were microdrilled using a 1 mm diameter hand drill. Each sample was 
microdrilled multiple times. Sulfur was chemically extracted from the 15 –30 mg powde rs to form 
silver sulfide by chromium reduction as described by Canfield et al. (1986)  Silver sulfide was 
fluorinated at 225  °C in a Ni bomb under F2 atmosphere for nine hours to produce SF 6. The samples 
were purified cryogenically and by gas chromatograph y and introduced by SF 6 line into a Thermo 
Electron MAT 253 mass spectrometer fitted with a dual inlet to measure 32SF533SF5+, 34SF5+, and 
36SF5+. Sulfur isotopic ratios are expressed on the V -CDT scale, on which the δ34S, Δ33S, and Δ36S 
values of the Ag 2S reference material, IAEA -S-1, are taken to be -0.3 ‰, 0.094  ‰, and -0.7 ‰, 
respectively (Wing and Farquhar, 2015 ). The precision and accuracy of the bulk fluorination system 
is evaluated by repeat analyses that return uncertainty (2SD) on δ34S, Δ33S, and Δ36S values as better 
than 0.15  ‰, 0.02  ‰ and 0.2  ‰, respectively. The Δ33S and Δ36S values are calculated in the same 
manner as desc ribed above:

1000+1)0.515
−1]×1000  
 
∆36𝑆= 𝛿36𝑆𝑚𝑒𝑎𝑠𝑢𝑟𝑒𝑑 −[(𝛿34𝑆𝑚𝑒𝑎𝑠𝑢𝑟𝑒𝑑

In order to classify the relative location of the analysed samples within the sill, it is necessary to 
construct a generalised cross -section that shows that the overall sill is about 452.5  m thick. 
Accordingly, the samples that were analysed as part of this study can be arranged as shown below, 
with the understanding that the three samples from the Disseminated magnetite gabbro are 
stratigraphically above  the PGE + Au reef, whereas the samples from the Spotted magnetite gabbro 
unde rlie that mineralised horizon.

SDH09 -01 506.2m is at -429.3m (Spotted magnetite gabbro)  
SDH08 -05 52.9m is at -431.5m (Spotted magnetite gabbro)  
SDH08 -05 41.7m is at -440.5m (Spotted magnetite gabbro)  
 
The six samples from the Hart Dolerite at Speewah we re collected from drill cores SDH08 -05 (at 
depths of 51.7  m, 52.95  m), SDH08 -06 (at a depth of 14.9  m), and SDH09 -01 (at depths of 186.3  m, 
205.0  m and 506.2  m). The six samples yield δ34S values ranging between +0.11  ‰ and +0.87  ‰, 
with non anomalous Δ33S (-0.02  ‰), as shown in Figure 5. All the results can be found in Table 1.

Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  21  
Figure 5: Multiple sulfur isotope results for the Hart Dolerite intrusion at Speewah presented in Δ33S-δ34S space. Data 
from Penniston -Dorland et al. (2012) ; (2008 ). 
The data indicate that samples from the Disseminated magnetite gabbro and Spotted magnetite 
gabbro display statisti cally identical δ34S values ( Figure 5). It is argued that assimilation of sulfur 
from the country rocks was negligible, and that the ore forming process that led to the formation of 
the PGE+Au reef was due to either crystal differentiation and/or sudden ch ange of oxygen fugacity 
due to the incorporation of sulfur-free country rocks, similarly to the Stella intrusion in South Africa  
(Maier et al., 2003 ). The mantle -like values may reflect input from depleted mantle (δ34S = -1.3 ‰; 
Labidi et al. (2013) ), a bu lk Earth chondritic reservoir (δ34S = 0  ‰), or a metasomatised continental 
lithospheric mantle (δ34S = +2  ‰; Ionov et al. (1992) ). 
Interestingly, spatially associated with the reef the base of the Disseminated magnetite gabbro 
contains significant vanadium  mineralisation, which occurs as an oxide within titanomagnetite 
crystals at concentrations of up to 2.64 wt% V 2O5. These grains are disseminated up to 20  %, with 
the greatest concentration occurring within the basal 15 metres (slightly below the PGE+Au re ef), 
gradually decreasing upwards through the rest of the unit. A shift in oxygen fugacity would trigger 
the saturation of vanadiferous magnetite. However, this process (if it occurred) appears to be cryptic 
to our sulfur isotope dataset ( Figure 5). As a r esult, the Speewah multiple sulfur isotope dataset 
appears to be on e of the homogenous and mantle -like occurrences on Earth, with a much stronger 
visible contamination process recorded in the deposits of the Eastern and Western Bushveld as well 
as the Plat reef (Penniston -Dorland et al., 2012 ; Penniston -Dorland et al., 2008 ). 
  
22   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  Part III The Speewah Dome 
Au-Ag-Cu veins  
 Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  23 1 Introduction 
The aim of this pilot study was to characterise the various veins types carrying Cu and Au within the 
Speewah Dome area. A set of samples was collected by Ken Rogers (Speewah Mining P/L), 
representing the various vein types observed within the system. Figure 6 shows the location of these 
samples. Within this set of sample, 21 (10 outcropt samples and 10 drill core samples) were mapped 
using the Tornado micro-XRF mapper at ARRC. Results are presented in the following section. Using 
the results of this mapping, a subset of two samples were selected for a more detailed petrographic 
and mineral composition study. 
A second part of the study focused on investigating  if trace element contents and their pattern in 
calcite veins differ between the “mineralised” and  “non” -mineralized sample. Calcite can 
incorporate a range of trace elements including HREE, Sr and Pb whose patterns might be distinct 
for ore-related systems. In order to test this, two samples were selected to be analysed by laser 
ablation inductively coupled mass spectrometry (LA-ICP-MS) for their trace element contents. For 
both samples, transect analyses across the veins were conducted. Sample SDH10-6-176.1 represent 
an arguably “non”-mineralized sample with no apparent ore-minerals whereas sample SDH10-5-
105.5 contains sulfide bearing calcite veins and belongs to a mineralized system. 24   |  Mag matic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   
Figure 6: Location map of samples used fo r this study  of Au -Ag-Cu mineralisation in veins from the Speewah Dome. 
Sample location indicated by white circles with sample names next to it . 
 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  25 2 Methodology  
2.1 Micro XRF mapping  
One of the main analytical technique employed in this part of the study is microb eam scanning X -
ray fluorescence analysis on roughly polished rock slabs of half curt core, accomplished with the 
Bruker Tornado™ desktop equipped with a rhodium target X -ray tube operating at 50 kV and 500 
nA without filters and an XFlash ® silicon drift X -ray detector. Maps were created using a 40 -µm spot 
size on a 40 -µm raster with dwell times of 5 ms per pixel. Maps are represented as unquantified 
background -corrected peak height data for K α peaks for each element, scaled linearly between 
minimum and maximum measured counts over the sample. X -ray spectra for each 40 -μm pixel are 
stripped and quantified using the Bruker ESPRIT software, which gives standard -less semi -
quantitative analyses for  elements heavier than Na, with estimated detection limits for the given 
operating conditions and dwell times of about 1,000 to 2,000 ppm for first -row transition metals.  
2.2 Laser Ablation ICP -MS 
Polished thin sections were analysed by LA -ICP-MS at the Univer sity Western Australia, Perth, 
Australia. LA -ICP-MS analysis was conducted using an X -series II quadrupole ICP -MS attached to an 
Analyte G2 Excimer laser system (193 nm wavelength) with a standard dual volume cell. The He 
carrier gas was set at 1 l/min (MF C1= 0.7 l/min and MFC2= 0.3 l/min) and was mixed with a nebulizer 
flow of argon (0.7 l/min) in a glass mixing bulb. A steady flow of ~9 ml/min N 2 was added in order to 
enhance sensitivity and reduce oxide production before being introduced into the plasma.  The mass 
spectrometer was tuned to maximum sensitivity at plasma conditions of Th/U ~ 1 in a NIST610 glass, 
and the production of molecular oxide species was monitored by maintaining a low ThO+/Th+ ratio 
~0.025 %. The laser ablation repetition rate was 10  Hz, with a laser fluence of 5J/cm2. A spot size of 
40 micrometres was used for all unknowns and standards. The dwell time for each element was set 
at 10ms. A 30 s background was collected prior to each analysis. Element concentrations were 
reduced with th e Iolite software package (Hellstrom et al., 2008 ) using bracketing analysis of 
NIST610 as an external standard with the reference concentrations taken from  Jochum et al. (2011) . 
CaO was used as the internal standard values and NIST612 was analysed an d use d as a secondary 
standard.  26   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  3 Results

3.2 General observations  on results of  micro XRF mapping  
Most samples seem to show the following sequence of event s: 
1) Potassic alteration. Most samples seem to be composed of potassic altered basalt, gabbro, 
or granophyre which have then been brecciated and cross cut by multiple sets of veins. Many 
of the breccia samples contain K -rich clasts.  
2) Silicification of the rocks – A lot of samples are cross cut by quartz veins or brecciated with a 
quartz rich cement.  
3) Carbonate veining. The Ca rich fluids going through the system seem to be the last episode 
of veining that the rocks record.  
Cu mineralisation seems to be associated with this latest Carbonate rich event in many of the 
samples  
46   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley 3.3 Petrography and mineral Chemistry study of a subset of samples 
As stated previously at the beginning of this section of the report, 2 samples were selected to be 
studied in more details: SDH-5-105.5 (Figure 28) and SDH10-6-176.1 (Figure 28). Both samples come 
from the central part of the Speewah Dome, and both contain carbonate –rich veins, however, one 
of them contains Cu mineralisation,  in the form of chalcopyrite mainly,  whereas the other one is 
barren (Figure 28, Figure 29, and Figure 30). 
Figure 28: Left: Mineralized sample SDH10-5- 105.5 and a representative polished thin section. Right: “Non” -
mineralized sample SDH10-6-176.1 and its corresponding polished thin section. 
3.3.1 Petrographic study 
After a more detailed petrographic study, the composition of the vein in both samples was 
confirmed as being fluorite, calcite, and/or quartz with or without barite minerals (Figure 29 and 
Figure 30). It seems that calcite veins postdate fluorite crystallization. No clear paragenetic 
relationship between barite and calcite was observed besides their coeval formation. The calcite 
grains are clear without any significant inclusions.

dark grey) in BSE mode. Light grey at the top of the BSE image represents fluorite. 
Figure 30: Polished thin section of “mineralized” sample SDH10 -5-105.5 and a zoomed in BSE images showing barite 
(light grey) and calcite (dark grey) coexisting in the vein. Barite is in contact with vein sulfide.

50   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potent ial in the East Kimberley  3.3.3 Summary and implications:  
Both samples show very low trace element concentrations including Pb and Sr. The strong depletion 
of Sr is most likely due to its strong partitioning into barite, whic h is readily present in the studied 
samples. Low Pb concentrations can be explained by the presence of sulfide minerals, which are 
major Pb sinks. Since the trace element patterns are very similar and the concentrations vary not 
significantly, it can be in ferred that the “non” -mineralized sample (SDH10 -6-176.1) and its 
respective calcite vein belong to a very similar/same system as the mineralized sample (SDH 10-5-
105.5). It is possible that vein -forming fluid in sample (SDH10 -6-176.1) became depleted in Pb and 
other trace elements by the formation of ore -minerals, which however are not represented in the 
studied drill core sample. The low Sr concentrations in both samples suggest that barite and calcite 
formed coevally, which is also likely for sulfides and calcite given the low Pb contents of calcite.
