  52   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Part IV Savannah and 
adjacent mafic-
ultramafic intrusions Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  53 1 Introduction 
World-class orthomagmatic Ni- Cu-PGE systems are most commonly associated with large igneous 
provinces (LIPs) associated with mantle plumes (REF). The occurrence of Ni-Cu sulfide systems in 
orogenic settings, be it in pre-orogenic arc, syn-collisional or post-collisional settings, have been 
considered to be less prospective and less likely to host major deposits or camps. However, the 
occurrence of a number of Ni- Cu-PGE systems in high-grade metamorphic belts and orogens, such 
as the Ntaka Ni-Cu systems (Barnes et al., 2016c ; Tirschmann et al., 2010), Nova-Bollinger (Maier et 
al., 2016) or the Savannah deposit, focus of our study, indicate that these settings may be more 
prospective than previously thought.  
This chapter presents the results of an integrated study of Savannah and adjacent mafic-ultramafic 
intrusions, aiming at understanding the Savannah and Savannah North mineralised intrusions, their 
relationship between one another as well as with other intrusions in their vicinity (Sub-Chamber D, 
Wilson’s, Dave Hill, Copernicus)  and cross cutting or intruding rocks (Turkey Creek gabbro, Tickalara 
Metamorphics). Our study combines and integrates multiple datasets:  
•Geochronology study of the various lithologies (Shrimp U-Pb dating, TIMS U-Pb dating 
and Ar-Ar dating)
•O isotope study on zircons used for the geochronology study
•S isotope study of the various sulfide types and textures observed within the Savannah  
and Savannah North 
mineralised intrusions
•Geochemistry and mineral chemistry data collected on samples from all the various 
intrusions and their main host rock (Tickalara metamorphics)
•Anisotropy of magnetic susceptibility (A MS) and palaeomagnetic  study of the various 
mafic ultramafic intrusions 
By combinin g the result s fro m th ese variou s techniqu es, this proj ect aime d to: 
(1) Shed ligh t on the geomet rical and chrono logical relationsh ip betwee n the Savann ah an d the 
Savanna h Nort h minerali sed intrusion s. Both intrusion s are current ly though t to have intrude d the 
Tickalara Metamorphic s at th e same t ime (withi n erro r of the geochronol ogical techniqu es used) . 
However, curren t geologic al informatio n on these two intrusion s imp lies that th ey have undergone 
differen t deformatio n histor y: Savanna h Nort h is a large bathtu b shape d intru sion wit h evidences 
of sub-horizont al magmat ic layerin g, and massiv e sulfide s presen t at its bottom , indicatin g tha t it 
wou ld current ly be close t o its origin al orientation . On the oth er hand , the Savann ah intru sion shows 
characterist ic features o f a mineralised blad ed dyk e, with its elongate d shap e, evidenc es of sub-
vertic al NW- SE magmati c layerin g, and massiv e sulfide s presen t all alon g its north-wester n contact 
with the count ry rock s, though t to have originally bee n the botto m contac t of the intrusion . In this 
study we will (a)  compare sulfide compositions, mineral composition and whole rock geochemistry  
of the two intrusions, (b) perform high precision dating (TIMs) on zircons, (c) complete a 
palaeomagnetic  and AMS study looking at the deformation history of both intrusions, and (d) 
study the S source of sulfides from both intrusions. Combining all these results should allow us to 
gain a  better understanding of the chronology of events and the geometry of the Savannah  
mineralised  system.54   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberle y (2) Identify some of the critical ingredients in the genesis of magmatic nickel sulfides present at 
Savannah and Savannah North.  Use these to evaluate the prospectivity of other mafic -ultramafic 
intrusion in the vicinity.  
(3) Use the re sults of these various datasets to get a better understanding of the tectonic setting 
of the East Kimberley Ni -Cu systems.   
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  55 2 Background Geology  
This part of the study focuses on the Savannah mineralised intrusion, as well as other mafic -
ultramafic intrusions  in its vicinity: Savannah North and Sub Chamber D located between 50 -500 
northwest and northeast of Savannah, and Dave Hill, Wilson’s and Copernicus located respectively 
2 and  4 and 50 km south -southwest of Savannah (Figure 34). All these intrusions are c umulate 
textured mafic to ultramafic intrusions . Savannah, Savannah North, Sub -Chamber D and Copernicus 
are intruding feldspar  – plagioclase  – quartz  ± sillimanite  ± graphite paramigmatite and mafic 
granulite of the Tickalara Metamorphics  (Savannah, Savann ah North and Sub Chamber D ). 
Within the vicinity of the Savannah, Savannah North and Sub -Chamber D intrusions, the Tickalara 
Metamorphics were probably derived from sub -aluminous pelites and psammites . They also contain 
mafic granulites (called ‘Early Mafi c Dykes’ in our study) which igneous protolith is interpreted as 
dolerite or gabbroic dykes and sills.  Another intrusive body which is not a layered cumulate body, 
and that cross cuts the Savannah intrusion was taken into account in this study; the Turkey Creek 
Gabbro.  The Savannah and Savannah north intrusions have been interpreted as intruding the 
Tickalara Metamorphics post peak -metamorphic conditions (Thornett, 1981 ). 
  56   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   
Figure 33: Location Map - Savannah and Speewah Dome.   
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  57 Figure 34: Detailed geology map of the Savannah intrusive system. Red dots represent the surface expression of 
samples with geochemical data and black dots represent the surface expression of all the other samples. 
58   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley 2.1 The Savannah intrusion 
The Savannah intrusion outcrop shape is that of a bladed-dyke, measuring 1.75 km long by 0.35 km 
wide. It is dominated by harzburgite-peridotite at the base (south), overlain by troctolite and olivine 
norite, then by a long, progressively narrowing zone of norite in the north. Igneous layering 
preserved in outcrop immediately to the north where the intrusion is cut by the Turkey Creek 
Gabbro (TCG) dips steeply north indicating the intrusion has been tilted through almost 90 degrees 
and is possibly lying on its side (Hicks et al., in press) .  
At depth (500 and 900 meters deep), the Savannah intrusion is cross cut by two large faults now 
infilled by an aplite and a mafic dyke. These faults produce a normal displacement of the intrusion 
of about 400 meters. Samples from the aplite dyke were collected as part of this study in order to 
produce geochronological constrains. 
Ni-Cu-Co-(PGE) mineralisation within the Savannah intrusion was first discovered in 1974 by 
Australian Anglo American (Hoatson and Blake, 2000) during a gossan search to follow up a 142ppm 
Ni, 81ppm Cu stream sediment anomaly. The mineralisation is composed of primary magmatic 
“breccia -textured” pyrrhotite -pentlandite-chalcopyrite rich massive sulfides occurring within a 
marginal (contaminated) noritic unit developed about the peridotitic base of the Intrusion. The 
marginal norite is up to 40 m thick and dips sub-vertically towards the south. Prior to mining it 
outcropped as a persistent 250 m long limonite-goethite rich gossan with a base of oxidation 
typically between 10 and 30 m beneath the gossan outcrop. Within the overlying peridotite unit, 
fine grain disseminated, iron rich magmatic sulfides are often developed over broad zones. 
2.2 The Savannah North intrusion 
The Savannah North intrusion (formerly North Olivine Gabbro) outcrops directly to the north and 
north-west of the Savannah mine as a 1.25 km2 body of interlayered norite, troctolite and olivine 
gabbronorite. Based on the available drilling and geophysical datasets the Savannah north intrusion 
was emplaced along an east west direction over an estimated strike length approaching 2 km. It 
remains open to the east and west (Hicks et al., in press). The base of the intrusion dips gently 
towards the north suggesting the body has been tilted 10-20 degrees off vertical towards the north. 
Like Savannah, Savannah North exhibits well preserved primary cumulus textures. Compositional 
layering, reflecting a near vertical, upwardly fractionated cumulate sequence, is recognised on a 
broad scale. Olivine becomes increasingly more prevalent with depth. Near the base of intrusion is 
a persistent 150-200 m thick peridotite unit. Contacts between the compositional layers can be 
sharp but are often gradational. Typically the layering exhibits a gentle (~20 degree) northerly dip 
in-keeping with the apparent tilt of the intrusion. 
A contaminated chilled zone, up to 20 m thick is consistently developed at the margins of the 
Savannah north intrusion against the enclosing paramigmatite and mafic granulites of the Tickalara 
Metamophiccs. The contaminated chilled zone is dominated by equigranular, fine to medium 
grained lithologies of gabbro to gabbronorite composition. Often the contact between the intrusion 
and enclosing Tickalara Metamorphics is marked by the presence of younger mafic to aplitic dykes 
that were emplaced along the contact. This is particularly the case when the base of the SNI is in 
proximity to the 500 Fault (described above in section 2.1 ).  
Magmatic sulfide minera l potential in the East Kimberley   |  59 The magmatic Ni -Cu-Co rich sulfide mineralisation of the Savannah North intrusion  is described in 
terms of an Upper (UZ) and Lower (LZ) zone. The UZ mineralisation is developed more or less at the 
base of the intrusion whereas the LZ is developed off contact within the Tickalara Metamorphics 
below the intrusion. The mineralisation within both zones typically co nsists of magmatic “breccia -
textured” pyrrhotite -pentlandite -chalcopyrite rich massive sulfides.  
The UZ mineralisation, like as occurs with the Savannah intrusion , is developed within the 
contaminated chilled zone at the base of the Savannah north intrusio n. Typically the UZ is developed 
at the base of the contaminated chilled zone in contact with the underlying Tickalara Metamorphics, 
but can also form as discreet lenses above the contact. The mineralisation can extend over 300  m 
along the contact and atta in maximum thicknesses of 10 -15 m. Where not affected by dykes the 
mineralisation typically grades 2.0  % to 2.7  % Ni, 0.5  % to 1.5  % Cu and 0.1  % to 0.2  % Co. Clasts of 
contaminated chilled zone, garnet rich sulfide mineralisation, fragments and melts vein s of Tickalara 
Metamorphics are common within the UZ.  
The LZ mineralisation is almost identical to the UZ. It consists of a series of lenses within the Tickalara 
Metamorphics underlying the intrusion . The lenses dip towards the NW at ~50 degrees. The LZ 
mineralisation is typically more massive (slightly higher grade) with fewer breccia clasts than the UZ. 
Mafic and aplite dykes, similar to those occupying the 500 and 900 fault structures are often present 
about the LZ mineralisation and may indicate a possi ble link between the LZ and the faults.  
Several other mineralisation styles are recognised within the Savannah North intrusion . Within the 
thick peridotite zone developed at the base of the intrusion are broad zones of ~5  %, fine 
disseminated magmatic nick el sulfide mineralisation. This mineralisation is typically pyrrhotite rich 
and grades between 0.15  % and 0.35  % Ni. Small areas of disseminated and coarse blebby 
mineralisation, occasionally with thin intervals of semi -massive to massive sulfides, are oft en 
present in the contaminated chilled zone about the UZ. Though less consistent than the UZ this 
mineralisation does approach mineable widths and grade in places. Away from the UZ the 
contaminated chilled zone is commensurately less well mineralised.  
To the west of the currently drilled Resource area, the UZ has moved higher off the basal contact of 
the intrusion. In this area the UZ has only been intersected in a few drill holes and consists of a 10  
m to 20  m thick zone of 20  % to 40  % matrix mineralisati on. Thin intervals of coarse blebby to semi -
massive to massive sulfides do occur within the matrix sulfides. The UZ matrix mineralisation in this 
area is typically developed within the peridotite and is underlain by a 20 -30 m thick un -mineralised, 
contamin ated chilled zone sequence. The grade of the UZ matrix mineralisation is typically lower at 
between 0.90  % to 1.2  % Ni, 0.40  % to 0.60  % Cu and 0.05  % to 0.06  % Co.  
2.3 The Sub -Chamber D intrusion  
The Sub -Chamber D intrusion is a large mafic -ultramafic intrusi on located 50 -100 meters northeast 
of the Savannah intrusion. Its surface expression is a large 1 by 1km rounded shape. Nothing has 
been published yet on the geology of this intrusion and very little information is available. This 
report will presents resu lts from both surface outcrop samples and samples collected from the only 
drill hole that was recently drilled by Panoramics through the core of the intrusion.  60   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  2.4 The Dave hill intrusion  
The Dave Hill intrusion located 2 km southwest of the Savannah intrusion , is described by Hoatson 
and Blake (2000)  as an arcuate mafic sheet, 1 km wide by 3.5 km long. Like Savannah and Savannah 
North, Dave Hill exhibits well preserved primary cumulus textures, with a rhythmic layering of 
olivine gabbro and olivine gabbronorit es, overlayed by a mafic sequence. Observed layering dips 
between 40 -85 degrees towards the southwest. The intrusion is thought to be approximately 0.8 
km thick. The Dave Hill intrusion intrudes the Tickalara Metamorphics, and is itself intruded by 
lenses of a fine grained olivine miccrogabbro, the Turkey Creek Gabbro.  
 The Wilson’s intrusion  
The Wilson’s intrusion, located 4 km southwest of the Savannah intrusion, is described by Hoatson 
and Blake (2000)  as a 3 by 6 km lobate intrusion, displaying weakly l ayered mafic sheets intruded by 
irregular plug -like ultramafic bodies. The Wilson’s intrusion  also exhibits well preserved primary 
cumulus textures, and seems to be  intruded by the Turkey Creek gabbro, as we ll as ovoids an d dykes 
of leucogranites and aplit es, and cross cut by quartz veins and pegmatites. Layering within the 
Wilson intrusion is not obvious, and the stratigraphy which is not well defined is described by 
Hoatson and Blake (2000)  as massive serpentinised peridotite cutting through anorthisite, 
leucogabbro and troctolite.  Out of all the intrusions in this study, Wilsons seems to contain the 
largest area of ultramafic rocks.  
2.5 Turkey Creek Gabbro  
The Turkey Creek Gabbro (TCG)  (Hicks et al., in press ) is an important intrusion in the vicinity of the 
Savannah deposit . The TCG has been assigned by previous workers as belonging to the regionally 
extensive and younger Turkey Creek microgabbro -norite intrusive suite. It outcrops at Savannah as 
a single 1.5  km long, NE trending, steeply dipping body up to 3 50 m thick. It partly encases the 
Savannah intrusion  at surface, wrapping around the northern side of the Savannah orebody before 
cutting through the SI ~200  m NE of the Savannah open pit. At a depth of ~300 -400 m the dip of the 
TCG flattens considerably, thereafter adopting a consistent 4 0-50 degree dip towards the NW. For 
the most part the TCG passes between the Savannah and Savannah north intrusions  at depth but to 
the east of mine Section 5900  mE the TCG and Savannah north instrusions  increasingly abut one 
another suggesting at some point further to the east the TCG may also cut the Savannah North 
intrustion.  
2.6 Structure and emplacement of Savannah and Savannah North  
The structural setting and emplacement histories of the various intrusions at Savannah are  complex. 
The Tickalara Metamorphics have been deformed several times by more than one phase of medium 
to high grade metamorphism. In addition to the peak granulite metamorphic event dated at ~1852  
Ma, deformation effects of the ~1835  Ma Hall Creek Orogeny  include the amphibolite facies 
metamorphism and pervasive foliation developed in the 100 km long by 8 km wide  Mabel Downs 
Tonalite located 5k m to the east of Savannah.  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  61 Based on field observations the Savannah north intrusion appears to be significantly less tilted off 
vertical compared to the Savannah intrusion and therefore appears to be the younger of the two 
intrusions. Field relationships indicate the Turkey creek Gabbro is slightly younger than both the 
Savannah and Savannah North intrusion. It clearly cuts through the Savannah intrusion at surface. 
Exploration drilling at depth to the north and below the Savannah north has intersected narrow 
intervals of Savannah above the Turkey Creek Gabbro indicating that it was emplaced along a pre-
existing normal fault structure that cut through the Savannah intrusion  
Two significant faults (500 and 900 Faults) are recognised within the Savannah mine workings (as 
stated above). Both faults dip at between 35-50 degrees towards the NW and have measured offsets 
of the order of 200-300 m. The age of the two faults is probably similar as they appear to form a 
complex, anastomosing network of smaller faults and link structures. Within the Savannah mine 
workings the faults are observed to demonstrably cut both the Savannah intrusion and Turkey Creek 
Gabbro and are therefore interpreted as younger. 
Two dykes suites of differing composition typically occupy both faults. First emplaced was a fine 
grained doleritic mafic suite that was subsequently intruded an aplitic dyke suite. The dykes are 
variably deformed and often intricately intermingled with each other. Both dyke suites typically 
exhibit a weak pervasive foliation that can become an intense penetrative shear fabric in places 
indicating repeated movement along the host faults. Most observed dyke fabrics are aligned with 
the orientation of the host fault. Mafic and aplitic dykes identical to those occupying the 500 and 
900 Faults are common about Savannah, cutting virtually all other rock types. They are observed to 
occupy other smaller fault structures and are often developed along the margins of earlier 
intrusions. 62   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley 3 Materials and Methods 
3.1 Sampling 
Multiple sets of samples were collected during the length of this project, for different purposes 
Details on all collected samples can be found in Supplementary Materials Table S2 . Figure 34 shows 
the location (surface expression) of all samples collected. Sample in red are the samples for which 
geochemical analyses were acquired. A total of 337 samples were collected during this project, both 
outcrop ( 80) and drill core samples ( 257). These samples were used for geochemical analyses, 
petrographic study, micro-XRF mapping 
A subset of 21 samples was collected specifically for a detailed geochronology study of the various 
mafic ul tramafic intrusive (Savannah, Savannah North, Wilson’s, Dave Hill, Sub Chamber D, and 
Turkey Creek Gabbro), as well as cross cutting dykes (Aplite present in the 500 and 900 Faults, and 
Early mafic dykes present in the whole system) and sedimentary host rock (Tickalara metamorphics) . 
All the samples used for the geochronology study are described in details in Supplementary 
Materials Table S3. Selection of these particular samples was done following a specific protocol 
which allowed us to insure the best possible zircon recovery during mineral separation. Firstly, areas 
of the core showing evidences of the presence of pockets of more differentiated melt were selected. 
These areas were then analysed using portable XRF, in order to get a first idea of their zirconium 
content. Samples showing elevated reading for Zr where then selected, collected and sent for 
mineral separation. 
3.2 Sulfur isotopes 
At Savannah, samples from the magmatic intrusions were chosen from drill core to represent sulfide 
occurrences with different textures (blebby, disseminated, massive) from various sulfide phases 
(pyrrhotite, pentlandite, chalcopyrite). Sulf ur isotope measurement on these sulfides was carried 
out in situ, which allows for the targeting of specific domains in individual sulfi de phases thanks to 
the small amount of material analysed (20 µm × 20 µm × 2 µm). Multiple sulf ur isotopic ratios on 
chalcopyrite, pyrrhotite and pentlandite were determined in-situ using a CAMECA IMS1280 large-
geometry ion microprobe located at the Centre for Microscopy, Characterisation and Analysis 
(CMCA), University of Western Australia (UWA), following the procedures defined in LaFlamme et 
al. (2016). Appendix 1 describes the samples and the methodology in more details. 
In order to provide additional constraints on the multiple sulf ur isotope signature of magmatic Ni-
Cu sulfide mineralisation associated with the intrusions at Savannah, selected mineralised samples 
were also analysed by bulk measurement technique. In addition, in order to test the multiple sulf ur 
isotope signature of the country rocks at Savannah, samples from the Tickalara metamorphics were 
analysed in bulk. In this case, this type of measurement was needed instead of the in situ approach 
because no visible sulfides occurred in the available specimens. Bulk measurements homogenise a 
larger proportion of sample and yield very precise results. Bulk measurements were completed by 
fluorination coupled with gas-source mass spectrometry were analysed at the Stable Isotope 
Laboratory of the Department of Earth and Planetary Sciences at McGill University, Montreal,  
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  63 Canada. We provide below analytical details on in situ Secondary Ion Mass Spectrometry and bulk 
Fluorination Mass Spectrometry.  
3.2.1 Secondary Ion Mass Spectrometry (in situ  microbeam measur ements ) 
Briefly, mounts were made by coring small (3.2 mm in diameter) pucks of core and casting epoxy to 
form 25 mm diameter mounts. After casting, the mounts were mechanically ground (1200 grit), and 
polished in three successive steps (9, 3, and 1 µm) wi th medium nap diamond polishing compound. 
Mounts were imaged in reflected light and carbon coated (10 nm thickness) for Secondary Electron 
Microscope (SEM) and analysis at the CMCA, UWA. Characterisation of small (<50 µm) sulfides by 
Energy -dispersive X -ray spectroscopy (EDS), and identification of fractures, inclusions and elemental 
zoning by backscatter electron (BSE) imaging occurred using a Tescan Vega -3 SEM under the 
operating conditions of 15 kV with a focused 6.0 nA beam.  
Trimmed mounts (to a 5 mm w idth) were coated with 30 nm of gold were loaded with a standard 
block into the sample chamber. A 2.5 -3.5 nA focused Cs+ primary beam interacted with the sample 
at 20 keV. The beam, in Gaussian mode, bombarded the sample surface to create a 15 µm analytica l 
pit. Following a 30 s pre -sputter to form a 25 µm × 25 µm raster, secondary sulfur ions from the 
target sample were extracted at −10 kV and admitted to the mass spectrometer with a field 
magnification of 133×, with automated centering of the secondary be am in the field/aperture (both 
x and y; aperture size 4000 μm) and entrance slit (x direction only; slit width 60 μm). The NMR 
magnetic field controller locked the axial mass at the beginning of each session, and the mass 
spectrometer operated at a mass re solution (M/ΔM) of about 5000 with an exit slit width of 250 μm 
on the multi -collector, which is adequate to off centre the 33S peak from the 32S1H peak.  
Isotopes 32S, 33S and 34S were simultaneously detected by three Faraday Cups using amplifiers with 
1010 Ω (L’2), 1011 Ω (L1), and 1011 Ω (FC2 or H1) resistors. Data were collected over 123 s of 
acquisition time in 20 integration cycles. In the case of unknown material, measurements were 
interspersed with Sierra pyrite (δ34S = +2.17  ‰, Δ33S = −0.02  ‰) corre ct for drift, and monitor 
internal sample repeatability. As well, analyses of matrix -matched reference material were used to 
calibrate isotope ratios for chalcopyrite using Nifty -b (δ34S = −3.58  ‰, Δ33S = +0.06‰), pyrrhotite 
using Alexo (δ34S = +5.23  ‰, Δ33S = −0.96  ‰), and pentlandite using VMSO (δ34S = +3.22  ‰, Δ33S = 
0.00  ‰). Data reduction followed procedures described in  LaFlamme et al. (2016) .  
3.2.2 Fluorination mass spectrometry (bulk)  
See description given in Part II, section 3 (Methodology).  
3.3 Geochemistr y 
A total of 163 samples were analysed for this project by Genalysis - Intertek laboratories in Perth. 
43 of those are sample collected within the mineralisation at both Savannah and Savannah North, 
and were analysed only for Platinum  Group elements (PGEs) . Concentrations of PGEs were obtained 
by inductively coupled plasma -mass spectrometry (ICP -MS) after a sulfide fire assay collection. All 
the other samples (116) were analysed for whole rock geochemistry (major elements: Al, Ba, Ca, Cr, 
Fe, K, Mg, Mn, Na,  P, S, Si, Ti and Zr) by X -Ray Fluorescence Spectroscopy after a fused disk 64   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley preparation. Within these, most samples (73) were also analysed for trace elements geochemistry, 
as well as semi-metals. To obtain concentrations in the following elements: Ba, Ce, Cs, Dy, Er, Eu, 
Ga, Gd, Hf, Ho, La, Lu, Nb, Nd, Pr, Rb, Sm, Sn, Sr, Ta, Tb, Th, Tm, U, W, Y, Tb, Zr, Cr, Sc, V, a lithium 
metaborate/tetraborate fusion was applied before the samples could be analysed by either 
inductively coupled plasma-mass spectrometry (ICP-MS) or Inductively Coupled Plasma Optical 
(Atomic) Emission Spectrometry (ICP-OES).To get concentrations in Ag, As, Be, Bi, Cd, Co, Ge, In, Li, 
Mo, Pb, Re, Sb, Se, Te, Tl , Cr, Cu, Na, Ni, Zn, a multi-acid digest including Hydrofluoric, Nitric, 
Perchloric and Hydrochloric acids in Teflon Tubes was applied before analysing the samples by either 
inductively coupled plasma-mass spectrometry (ICP-MS) or Inductively Coupled Plasma Optical 
(Atomic) Emission Spectrometry (ICP-OES). Finally, C and S concentrations were obtained by 
Infrared Spectrometry after an Induction Furnace process, and LOI was determined by Thermal 
Gravimetric Analyser. 
3.4 Mineral chemistry 
3.4.1 Major elements chemistry (microprobe and SEM) 
Major element concentrations in olivine, orthopyroxene, clinopyroxene, plagioclase and amphibole 
were determined at the Australian Resources Research Centre (CSIRO, Kensington) using a TESCAN-
MIRA (FEG) SEM fitted with an Oxford Instruments X-MaxN EDS detector using a voltage of 20 kV, a 
current of 1.2 mA and a spot size of ~5 µm. The accuracy and precision of the analyses were 
monitored during each analytical session using reference minerals (anorthite, augite, hypersthene, 
San Carlo olivine and labradorite from the Smithsonian institute) analysed as unknown at the start, 
middle and end of the session. 
3.4.2 Trace element mineral chemistry (Laser ablation ICP-MS) 
Trace element concentrations in olivine, orthopyroxene, clinopyroxene, plagioclase and amphibole 
were determined using laser-ablation inductively coupled mass spectrometry (LA-ICP-MS) at the 
GeoHistory facilty (John de Laeter Centre, Curtin University in Perth). The LA-ICPMS used comprises 
a Resonetics S-155-LR 193nm excimer laser ablation system coupled to an Agilent 7700x quadrupole 
ICPMS. The analysis were carried out in spot mode using 75 µm diameter beam, a laser frequency 
of 7Hz, an acquisition time of 45 seconds after 15 seconds of gas background measurements (He- Ar 
gas mixture). The data were processed using a combination of Iolite software (Hellstrom et al., 2008) 
The instrument was calibrated using NIST610 certified reference material and the accuracy and the 
precision of the analysis during each analytical session was monitored using three reference 
materials (NIST612, BHVO2G and BCR2G) analysed as unknown during the course of each analytical 
session. Si was used as an internal standard for all the silicate minerals. 
3.5 Oxygen isotopes 
Oxygen isotope ratios (18O/16O) in zircon were determined in samples NIM011 and SPHGE01 via 
secondary ion mass spectrometry (SIMS) using a Cameca IMS 1280 multi-collector ion microprobe 
at the Centre for Microscopy, Characterisation and Analysis (CMCA), University of Western Australia 
(UWA). The sample mount was re-polished to removed SHRIMP analytical pits before cleaning with Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  65 detergent, distilled water and ethanol in an ultrasonic bath and coated with gold (30 nm in 
thickness) prior to SIMS analyses.  
Oxygen secondary ions are sputtered from the sample by bombarding its surface with a 10 kV, 
Gaussian Cs+ beam with intensity of 3 nA. This primary beam is rastered over a 10 x 10 µm area at 
the surface of the sample. Secondary ions are admitted in the double focusing mass spectrometer 
within a 110 µm entrance slit and are focused in the center of a 4000 µm field aperture (x 130 
magnification). They are energy filtered using a 40 eV band pass with a 5 eV gap toward the high-
energy side. 16O and 18O are collected simultaneously in Faraday cup detectors fitted with 1010 Ω 
(L’2) and 1011 Ω (H’2) resistors, respectively, and operating at a mass resolution of ~2430. The 
magnetic field was regulated using NMR control.  
Each analysis includes a pre-sputtering over a 15 x 15 µm area during 20s and the automatic 
centering of the secondary ions in the field aperture, contrast aperture and entrance slit. Each 
analysis then consists of 20 four-second cycles, which give an average internal precision of 0.16 ‰ 
(2 SE). The analytical session was monitored in terms of drift and precision using at least two 
bracketing standards (Temora II; 8.2 ‰; (Black et al., 2003)) every five sample analyses. To monitor 
accuracy, two secondary standards were used; OGC-1 (multiple grains from a Pilbara granite; OG1 
of Stern (2001)) and M257 (single-crystal U -Pb SIMS standard; (Nasdala et al., 2008) ). The δ18O 
reference data for these standards are 6.01 ± 0.14 δ18O ‰ (2σ) for OGC (collected via SIMS; (Stern 
and Ickert, 2010)), and 13.93 ± 0.22 ‰ (2σ) (collected via laser fluorination; (Nasdala et al., 2008)). 
Standard data are presented in Supplementary Materials Table S4, and demonstrate good 
agreement with published values, except for one grain of M257, which yielded 15.02 ± 1.31 ‰. This 
is within error of the published value, but variability in the data led to a large error. Due to the 
stability and low standard deviation of the corresponding TEMOA (0.14) we interpret this variability 
as heterogeneity in this chip of the M257 standard zircon. All other standard data are correct within 
analytical error and indicate data acquired during this session are accurate. 
Instrumental mass fractionation (IMF) was corrected using Temora II following the procedure 
described in Kita et al. (2009). The spot- to-spot reproducibility was 0.3 ‰ (2 SD) on Temora II during 
the analytical session. Uncertainty on each δ18O spot has been calculated by propagating the errors 
on instrumental mass fractionation determination, including the error on the reference value of the 
standard and standard deviation of the mean oxygen isotope ratio measured on the primary 
standard during the session, and internal error on each sample data point. Raw 18O/16O ratios and 
corrected δ18O (quoted with respect to Vienna standard mean ocean water or V SMOW ) are presented 
in the Supplementary Materials Table S4. 
3.6 Geochronology 
3.6.1 Shrimp dating 
Several large ~10 kg samples were collected from diamond drillcore for U-Pb zircon SHRIMP 
geochronology. Approximately 2 –3 kg of least-altered sample was processed for mineral separation 
at Geotrack Pty Ltd in Melbourne, Victoria. Zircons were separated using industry-standard 
techniques and mounted on 25 mm diameter epoxy-resin mounts with chips of M257 zircon (main 
U/Pb calibration standard, 561.3 Ma, 840 ppm 238U; Nasdala et al. (2008)), NBS610 glass, OGC- 1 
(Pilbara granite zircons, 207Pb/206Pb age 3465 Ma, equivalent to OG1 of Stern et al. (2009)) and 66   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley TEMORA (417 Ma; Black et al. (2003)) . Mounts were subsequently polished to expose grains in 
section and coated in high-purity gold (40 nm) prior to imaging. Cathodoluminescence (CL) and 
backscattered electron (BSE) images were taken using a TECSAN MIRA scanning electron microscope 
(SEM) at the CSIRO, Kensington (Perth), Western Australia.  
Isotopic analyses were performed on the SHRIMP II instrument at the John de Laeter Centre of Mass 
Spectrometry at Curtin University. Typically, a primary ion (O2–) beam of ca. 20 µm diameter, with a 
current of 2.0 –2.5 nA was used. Six data collection cycles were performed per analysis. Unknown 
analyses were referenced to multiple analyses of the M257 (206Pb/238U = 0.09100 ± 0.00003 Nasdala 
et al. (2008)) or and CUYZ (238U/206Pb age 568.55 Ma; 207Pb/206Pb age 569.49 Ma; U = 582.7 ppm; Th 
= 82.7 ppm) standards for U/Pb calibration. Geochronological data were collected over nine 
sessions; standard (M257) data collected in each session constituted 42 to 5 analyses resulting in 1σ 
uncertainties of 2.6 to 0.5 % for Pb*/U, together with U/Pb calibration uncertainties of 0.64 to 0.24 
%, respectively. For each analytical session, analyses of the OGC Archean secondary standard (c. 
3465 Ma; Stern et al. (2009) ) were always within 2σ of the published value  (See Supplementary 
Materials Table S5) . The TEMORA 238U/206Pb standard was  also analysed to check the set-up, 
machine performance and data quality for the 238U/206Pb system. These analyses were within error 
of the published value (c. 417 Ma; Black et al. (2003) ). 
3.6.2 TIMs dating 
Zircon grain mounts previously used for SHRIMP analysis were provided for two samples, NOG- 11 
and SAV-21. Using SEM and CL images, grains were selected that were free from obvious core-rim 
relationships, and that had been previously analyzed for U-Pb geochronology in order to facilitate a 
direct comparison. 
Zircon grains were removed from the epoxy mounts, rinsed in acetone to remove traces of epoxy, 
then rinsed in aqua regia. They were photographed, and pre-treated according to the chemical-
abrasion method of (Mattinson, 2005), with 60 hours of annealing at 850˚C followed by 12 hours of 
leaching in HF and HNO 3.  Six single grains were selected, and were cleaned with concentrated 
distilled HNO 3 and HCl.  Due to their small size, no chemical separation methods were required. For 
ID-TIMS analysis, the samples were spiked with an in-house 205Pb-235U tracer solution, which has 
been calibrated against SRM981, SRM 982 (for Pb), and CRM 115 (for U), as well as an externally-
calibrated U-Pb solution (the JMM solution from the EarthTime consortium). This tracer is regularly 
checked using “synthetic zircon” solutions that yield U -Pb ages of 500 Ma and 2000 Ma, provided 
by D. Condon (BGS). 
Dissolution and equilibration of spiked single crystals was by vapour transfer of HF, using Teflon 
microcapsules in a Parr pressure vessel placed in a 200°C oven for six days. The resulting residue 
was re-dissolved in HCl and H 3PO 4 and placed on an outgassed, zone-refined rhenium single filament 
with 5 µL of silicic acid gel. U –Pb isotope analyses were carried out using a Thermo Triton T1 mass 
spectrometer, in peak-jumping mode using a secondary electron multiplier. Uranium was measured 
as an oxide (UO 2). Fractionation and deadtime were monitored using SRM981 and SRM 982. Mass 
fractionation was 0.02 ± 0.07 %/amu. Data were reduced and plotted using the software packages 
Tripoli (from CIRDLES.org) and Isoplot 4.15 (Ludwig, 2012) . All uncertainties are reported at 2σ.  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  67 The weights  of the  zircon  crystals  were calculated  from  measurements  of photomicrographs  and 
estimates of the third dimension. The weights are used to determine U concentration and do not 
contribute to the age calculation, and an uncertainty of 50% may be attributed to the concentration 
estimate. 
3.7 Anisotropy of magnetic susceptibility (A MS) and Palaeomagnetic
study
To characterise the magnetic properties of the studied deposits, it is imperative to get a spatial 
understanding of the magnetic properties of the host rocks and mineralised lithologies at deposit 
and sample scales. Magnetic susceptibility (Mag sus) data provides a measure of the strength of 
induced magnetisation that rock will have when placed in an external magnetic field (such as the 
earth’s main field). It does not provide any discrimination of what mineral is responsible for that 
magnetisation, nor does it provide a reliable quantitative measure of the concentration of 
magnetic minerals. Magnetic susceptibility units are dimensionless (they are just numbers), but 
values depend on the convention used. All magnetic susceptibilities in this study are in the SI 
system. For comparison, any magnetic susceptibilit ies measured in the CGS system must be 
multiplied by 4π (approximately 12.6). To give sense of the values reported in this study, (Austin et 
al, 2016) found that pure magnetite has magnetic susceptibilities ra nging from 1 and 6 SI, hematite 
ranged from 0.001 to  0.04 SI. Monoclinic pyrrhotite is highly va riable (e.g., Austin et al., 2013), 
ranging between 0.07 and 3 SI, whereas hexagonal pyrrhotite is essentia lly non-magnetic. 
Monoclinic pyrrhotite has only very low susceptibility. Magnetic susceptibility values do not 
provide a reliable indication of the strength of remanent magnetisation of a rock, which can 
be as strong as or even much stronger than the induced magnetisation arising from 
magnetic susceptibility. A major application of the magnetic susceptibility measurements is 
to provide an estimation of the bulk susceptibility of the deposit or prospect, which is in turn used 
to constrain geophysical modelling and inversion. Specific gravity (SG, i.e. density) data are also 
very useful, especially for deposits where high‐resolution magnetics and gravity are both available. 
Cross‐ plots of SG data and Mag Sus data are far more diagnostic of lithologies than are individual 
values alone. However, for this study even having both SG and Mag sus values does not provide 
unique characterisation of the rocks, because magnetite and pyrrhotite can have very similar 
SG and Mag sus values, and because high SG and modest Mag sus values can be due either to a 
large percentage of hematite, or a much smaller percentage of magnetite or pyrrhotite 
together with non‐magnetic ore phases. 
3.7.1 Glossary of magnetics terms 
This section outlines specialist magnetics terminology that may be of benefit to readers who are 
unfamiliar with the field. A general overview on some of the myths, methods and mysteries 
surrounding magnetic properties is provided in Austin et al. (2013), part of which is summarised 
below. 
Magnetisation 
Magnetisation within most rocks containing magnetic minerals is comprised of two 
components; induced magnetisation and remanent magnetisation, and it is important to 
consider both when modelling geological bodies.  
Induced magnetisation  is a response to the ambient field, and is equal to the product of the 
magnetic susceptibility and the inducing field. For isotropic rocks, it is parallel to the internal 68   |  Magmatic su lfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  inducing field, which for weakly magnetic rocks is essentially in the regional geomagnetic field 
direction. Remanent mag netisation is retained in the rock over geological time spans (i.e., it is not 
induced  by the Earth’s field) by fine‐grained minerals such as magn etite, pyrrhotite and hematite.  
Natural Remanent Magnetisation (NRM)  is often acquired during thermal or metasomatic events. 
As the rocks cool through the Curie point they acquire a remanence alon g the magnetic field 
direction of the Earth at that time. The orientation of remanent magnetisation can be stable for 
billions of years, or it can be highly dynamic, acquiring new magnetisation over days to years. Its 
stability is primarily a function of g rain size (see MD magnetite and SD magnetite below).  
Koenigsberger ratio : Is the ratio of the remanent magnetisation to the induced magnetisation in a 
rock. The resultant number will be a product of a number of factors, but mostly the mineralogy, 
grain si ze and grain shape. Some typical values for the common magnetic minerals are provided in 
Table 3. 
Magnetic Mineralogy  
A key factor in consideration of the magnetisation of a rock is its magnetic mineralogy; in particular 
whether the main ferromagnetic mine ral is magnetite, hematite or pyrrhotite. Magnetite, 
pyrrhotite and the solid solution titano -magnetite series all have different Curie temperatures, 
which can provide diagnostic evidence of the main magnetic phase of a rock. These different 
minerals also have different remanent magnetisation properties, and different Koenigsberger 
ratios, but these characteristics are also highly variable for the individual minerals, and do not 
always provide reliable indication of the specific magnetic mineralogy. If, for  example a rock 
contains hard and soft magnetisation in magnetite, plus hard magnetisation in pyrrhotite, all with 
different directions, the total remanent magnetisation directions will be highly variable, because 
those directions are the vector sum of all  components (each in proportion to the concentration of 
the mineral which carries it). This phenomena is particularly relevant if there is a large degree of 
variability in the iron and sulfide contents, the titanium‐ iron ratio, and the grain size of the m agnetic 
carriers, all of which control the mineralogy of the magnetic carriers, the total intensity of 
remanence, and its relative intensity to magnetic susceptibility (i.e., Koenigsberger ratio).  
MD Magnetite : Multi‐domain magnetite comprises grains larg e enough that the internal structure 
subdivides into individual magnetic domains. These domain walls can move easily at low 
temperatu res, resetting the direction of the remanent magnetisation they carry.  
SD magnetite: Single  domain magnetite. This is fine‐ grained magnetite that has the ability to carry 
very stable remanent magnetisation. Because the SD magnetite comprises grains too small to form 
internal walls that result in the self‐cancelling domains found in MD magnetite ( Figure 35). This 
means that the  magnetisation direction present can only be changed by exposure to high 
temperatures, or to extreme electromagnetic fields (e.g., from lightning strikes). The temperatures 
required to remagnetise rocks with magnetisations carried by single domain magnetit e are close to 
the Curie temperature of magnetite (585°C).  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  69 Figure 35: Shows the difference between Multi domain magnetism and Single domain magnetism. A. Grain of 
ferromagnetic material subdivided into domains (Multi‐domain) which reduced the magnetostatic energy of the 
grain. B. Uniformly magnetized (single‐domain) grain grain where magnetisations shown by the arrow, and surface 
charge by +/‐. Grains that are intermediate ar e called pseudo single domain. (after Butler, 1998).

3.7.2 Palaeomagnetic Sample Preparation 
Drill core was marked‐up in the standard palaeomagnetic method illustrated in Figure 36. 
The resultant  azimuth and dip  correspond to a plane normal  to the drill hole. A  25 mm diamond 
core drill  was used to drill down the axis of each sample. The core was then sawn into 22 mm 
long segments referred to as ‘rounds’. Cylinders of this dimension equate to a good approximation 
of a dipole  magnetic source (Riisager and Abrahamsen, 2003). At least three rounds were made 
from each sample where possible, in order to provide statistically reasonable mean values 
for the petrophysical measurements. Preparing three samples also allowed for one sample to be 
used for geochemistry and mineralogy and one for Alternating Field Demagnetisation (AFD), 
with one reserved for secondary analysis. Samples were labelled and marked up 
(Figure 36) for palaeomagnetic (see Section 12.4)  and AMS (see Section 12.3) measurements.

including AMS mark- up.

Magnetic susceptibility measurements were made using an Agico M FK1‐A Kappabridge 
magnetometer (Figure 38).Bulk susceptibility measurements were taken with the field strength set 
at 200 A/m. Magnetic susceptibility measurements were made on all three specimens from each 
sample to gain a representative mean value. The M FK1‐A calculates magnetic susceptibility values 
based on a nominal sample volume of 10 cm3 and as such, the results were later corrected using 
volumes calculated during density measurements. 
Figure 38: Agico MFK1‐A Kappabridge magnetometer.  
3.7.5 Natural Remanent Magnetisation (NRM) 
The direction of remanent magnetism is important in understanding the overall magnetization 
strength and direction in highly magnetised ore bodies (e.g., Peculiar Knob; Schmidt et al., 2007) . 
Understanding remanent magnetism is cru cial to determining confidence in the resultant 3‐D model 
because it facilitates a reliable estimation of the impact of remanent magnetization on the overall 
(i.e., induced + remanent) magnetization of the prospect. Where magnetised rocks have a high 
Koenigsberger ratio (high ratio of remanent to induced magnetization), and where the remanent 
magnetisation direction is significantly oblique to the inducing field, anomalies will be incorrectly 
modelled if they do not account for the remanent magnetisation.  
At least two rounds from each sample underwent natural remanent magnetisation (NRM) 
measurements. The process requires the input of the sample orientation data to correct the 
measured magnetisation direction to geographic coordinates. All measurements of magnetic 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  73 remanence were made using either the 2G Enterprises 755R three‐axis cryogenic magnetometer 
(Figure 39 a, b) or a custom made CSIRO three‐axis spinner fluxgate magnetometer ( Figure 39c). The 
2G cryogenic magnetometer uses three superconducting‐quantum‐interference‐devices (SQUIDS) 
to measure the three components of the magnetic field with magnetic dipole moment noise of less 
than 1 x 10‐12 A/m. 
Strongly magnetised samples were measured on the 3‐axis spinner magnetometer as such values 
are beyond the reliable range of the 2G cryogenic magnetometer. The 3‐axis spinner utilises a 
fluxgate magnetometer positioned adjacent to the sample spinning mechanism. The results of the 
NRM measurements yielded a magnitude, declination, and inclination of the magnetisation 
direction. 
Figure 39: A & B) 2G Enterprises 755R Cryogenic SQUID Magnetometer; C) Custom made CSIRO 3‐axis spinner 
magnetometer 
3.7.6 Stepwise AF Demagnetisation (degaussing or AFD) 
Two demagnetisation techniques were used to resolve the various components of magnetisation 
within each sample. Low temperature demagnetisation was carried out on at least two rounds from 
each sample. The process involves submerging the specimens in liquid nitrogen in order to pass 
through the Verwey transition and thereby eff ectively ‘clean’ the samples of part or all of their ‘soft’ 
component of magnetisation. The samples were left to stabilise at liquid nitrogen temperature (77 
K, ‐196°C) for 30 minutes and then moved into a Mu metal shield where they warmed to ambient 
temperatures shielded from Earth’s magnetic field. The remanent magnetisation of each sample 
was re‐measured after they had returned to ambie nt temperature. 
Specimens were also progressively demagnetised using an alternating field of increasing intensity 
to incrementally remove the ‘harder’ components of remanent magnetisation prior to each 
measurement. AFD was carried out using the 2G Enterpri ses 755R three‐axis cryogenic 
magnetometer with in‐line 2G 600 Series AF demagnetisers ( Figure 39A – smaller cylindrical 
chamber at the front of the machine). AFD utilises two sets of perpendicular coils (aligned along the 
x‐ and z‐axes of the sample) with  a rapidly alternating current which produce a precisely controlled 
magnetic field. The field acts to randomise the magnetic moments of the rock in the x‐, y‐, and z‐
axes by  rotating  the  sample  through  90°  during  degaussing.  Measurements  of the  remanent 
magnetisation  are  taken  after  subjecting  the  sample  to increasing  magnetic  field  strengths. 
Typically, samples were subjected to fifteen field strength steps: 2, 5, 7, 10, 15, 20, 25, 30, 40, 50, 
60, 70, 80, 90, and 100 mT. Samples that retained a stable magnetisation after being demagnetised 
to 100 mT underwent further AFD in fields up to 140 mT. 
The results  of each  analysis  (magnetisation  vectors)  were  plotted  on stereonets  and  Zijderveld 
diagrams.  Both  were  used  to assess the  migration  of the  magnetisation  vectors  with  successive 
stepwise  demagnetisation.  Analysis  of the  magnetisation  components  was  performed  using  an 
interactive version of Linefind (Kent et al., 1983), in which linear segments are fitted to data points 
weighted according to the inverse of their measured variances (as for Schmidt and Williams, 2011) , 
otherwise known as a principal component analysis (PCA). 
It is common for viscous remanent magnetisation (VRM) to be overprinted due to stress release in 
the magnetic  field  within  the  steel  drilling  rods  in an orientation  that  is coincident  with  the  up 
directed  drilling  orientation  (in the  southern  hemisphere;  in the  northern  hemisphere,  drilling 
induced  remanence  tends  to be directed  down  the hole).  So the stereonet  is also  used  to assess 
whethe r a sample may have acquired isothermal remanent (i.e., drilling‐induced) magnetisation in 
the drilling orientation. It should be noted that even where stable components of magnetisation are 
derived,  that  the  strongest  component  of magnetisation  may be  a VRM,  and  hence  the  in situ 
remanence will be parallel or close to the direction of the local geomagnetic field. 
3.7.7 Drilli ng Induce d Magnetisation 
Drilling  induced  magnetisation  (DIM)  is a class  of Isothermal  remanent  magnetisation,  which  is 
common in MD grains (Audunsson and Levi, 1989), i.e., it tends to mostly overprint VRM (viscous 
remanent magnetisation). It occurs when the upward magnetic field is deflected into the orientation 
of the drill rod (Figure 40). Then, during drilling, small vibrations and or heat associated with grinding 
the rock at the drill bit demagnetise the rock. The rocks then remagnetise in the field inside the rod, 
and acquire remanence in the rod orientation.  
DIM totally to partially re‐magnetises a sample such that the measured NRM di rections are parallel 
to the drilling  orientation.  In the southern  hemisphere  the magnetisation  vector  is usually  anti‐
parallel to the drill string plunge (i.e., upward oriented), whereas in the northern hemisphere it is 
generally parallel (i.e., downward oriented). 
There are only a few datasets that can give us a quantifiable perspective on exactly how much DIM 
enhances the NRM intensity of a given rock. A study by Audunsson and Levi (1989), showed that 
the DIM intensity within a single sample increased by at least a factor of five from the centre of 
the drill core to the drill string's cutting surface, where it appears to have been produced. If we 
use this observation as a starting hypothesis assuming a linear decline in the intensity of DIM from 
the edge of the core to the centre (where there is zero enhancement) we can make a rough 
calculation of the intensity of the DIM relative to the area of the core (in cross‐section) as shown in 
Figure 41. Although this is a very approximate calculation, it does suggest is that there should be 
an increase in the NRM intensity by a factor of approximately 3.3 on average for rocks affected 
by DIM. This is a easonably conservative estimate, given that De Wall and Worm (2001) 
74   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  75 suggested that the increase in DIM from core to the outer core surface could be closer to a factor 
of ten. 
Figure 40: Illustration of how drilling induced magnetisation is acquired 
Figure  41: Results  of a basic  calculation  used  to estimate  the  increase  in intensity  of NRM  due  to drilling  induced 
magnetisation (DIM). In this calculation there is a linear increase in magnetisation from the core of the sample to 
the outer edge of 5, and this corresponds approximately to an enhancement due to DIM of ~3.3 times in situ NRM.

(Queensland) . Some of the samples were obtained via surface drilling using a handheld alloy 
barrelled drill by Austin et al. (2013), and others were extracted from a section of core produced 
by a conventional diamond drill rig (collected as part of this study). The samples were 
checked using a Tescan Integrated Mineral Analyser (TIMA; see section 12.3.2) to ensure that 
they have approximately comparable mineralogy and grain size, and it was confirmed that both 
have a similar texture with similar mineralogy, dominantly barite‐magnetite‐chalcopyrite
±sphalerite ( Figure 4 2). 
Figure 42: Shows TIMA scans of two of the samples included in the study, MNK003 (drilled at surface) and MON005 
(sampled from Diamond Drill core). Note that the mineralogy is very similar and the grainsize is comparable. Field of

Density, magnetic susceptibility, NRM and Koenigsberger ratios of the samples were then compared 
using a number of graphs (Figure 43 a‐I; a‐c being samples collected at the surface, g‐I being samples 
affected by DIM). The results from both DIM affected samples and surface sampled specimens were 
averaged and are plotted relative to each other in Figure 43 d and f. The averaged results of density 
and magnetic susceptibility are comparable for both subsets of data, with density of approximately 
4 g/cc, and magnetic susceptibility of approximately 0.5 SI (Figure 43 d). This indicates that they 
have approximately equal proportions of magnetite on average. However, the same datasets show 
a large disparity in the average NRM and also Koenigsberger ratio. What the results appear to show, 
is that the DIM affected samples have NRM that is approximately 3 times stronger than for very 
similar samples that are not affected by DIM. These measurements are consistent with the 
observations of Audunsson and Levi (1989). 
These results should be used to evaluate the remanent magnetisation data presented in this report. 
Any instances of extreme remanent magnetisation should be treated with caution, and it should be 
assumed that the NRM values may be enhanced by DIM to somewhere in the order of 2 ‐ 5 times 
the original (pre‐drilling) NRM intensity.

3.7.8 Anisotrop y of Magneti c Susceptibilit y (AMS) 
Anisotropy  of magnetic  susceptibility  (AMS)  analysis  was  carried  out on the  majority  of samples. 
Measurements  were  generally  made  on two rounds  but  a third  was  included  when  rounds  were 
available.  AMS  provides information about  magnetic fabrics  in rocks  by effectively  measuring  any 
lineation or foliation in magnetic grains. The results are presented as an ellipsoid characterised by a 
long,  an intermediate  and  a short  axis.  AMS  analysis  was  undertaken  on the Agico  MFK1‐A 
Kappabridge magnetometer. The process requires the input of the sample orientation data to allow 
for production of geographic declination and inclination data. Samples are inserted and rotated in 
three  orientations to  measure the  magnetic  susceptibility  in the  x, y, and  z directions  (Figure  44). 
Sixty four measurements are taken while rotating about each axis. The field sensor is zeroed after 
the sample  is inserted  into the pick‐up  coil thereby  eliminating  any field  bias from the 
measurements made as the sample is rotated. 
78   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley A variety of parameters are calculated (Figure 45) from the orientation of the susceptibility tensor 
and principal susceptibility directions measured along each axis. The direction of anisotropy is given 
in geographic coordinates (when sample orientation data is available). Three principal directions are 
calculated: K1 – long axis, K2 – intermediate axis, and K3 – short axis. The values L, F, and P (in blue, 
Figure 45) are the various ratios of the three axes with P representing the maximum elongation 
(K1/K3) and the effective maximum anisotropy.

4.1 In situ multiple sulfur isotopes 
As part of the in situ multiple sulfur isotope completed by SIMS targeting the different sulfide phases 
(chalcopyrite, pentlandite and pyrrhotite) within the mineralisation assemblage in both the 
Savannah North and Savannah intrusions, with various sulfide textures sampled (blebby vs 
disseminated). 
A summary of multiple sulf ur isotope results with representative sample information is presented 
in the Supplementary Materials Table S6. Briefly, sulfide phases yield δ34S values that range from 
+0.86 ‰ to +5.42 ‰ but are dominantly between +3 to +5  ‰, with the exception of one sample
(KUD1525- 776 – breccia ore at the basal contact) that yields δ34S values between +0.8 and +2.3 ‰.
Δ33S values are not anomalous and equal zero. Results are presented in Figure 46.

4.3 Discussion 
4.3.1 Source reservoir for sulf ur at Savannah 
The sampling program at Savannah was conducted in order to investigate whether assimilation of 
sulfidic metasediments in the Tickalara metamorphics country rocks was the principal mechanism 
that would trigger sulfide supersaturation in the emplacing intrusions. The multiple sulf ur isotope 
data acquired here provide strong evidence that this is the case. In fact, although likely mor e 
variable than currently ascertained, the δ34S sulfur isotope signature of the Tickalara metamorphics 
is consistently heavy (>+3.0‰). Similarly, the results from the in situ and bulk sulfur isotope multiple 
sulfur isotope analysis from the magmatic sulf ides yield δ34S values between ~+1- 5‰ and ~+5.0‰, 
with the majority of results clustering around ~+3- 5‰. It is concluded that assimilation of sulfur 
from the country rocks is key to the ore forming process, as crustally derived sulf ur makes up at 
least 50% of the sulf ur isotope budget in the magmatic mineralisation. Having said that, it is 
important to make the point that the limited dataset from the Tickalara metamorphics may not be Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  81 representative of its entire sulf ur isotope range, with the likelihood that much heavier domains 
could exist. 
There are some interesting observations to be made. The basal portion of the North Savannah and 
Savannah intrusions yield the lightest δ34S values between +1- 3‰. The interior portions of the 
intrusions are shifted to heavier isotopic ratios that are similar to the sulf ur isotope composition of 
the Tickalara metamorphics (δ34S = >+3.0‰). The questio n arises as to whether the magmas were 
emplaced already sulfide supersaturated, with a sulf ur budget derived predominantly from the 
mantle, subsequently assimilating country rocks only later on during their emplacement history 
(Scenario 1), or whether the observed variable sulf ur isotope signature of the magmatic sulfides at 
Savannah simply mimics the naturally occurring sulf ur isotope variability of the Tickalara 
metamorphics (Scenario 2). 
It is envisaged that if Scenario 1 were applicable, it would be possible to see evidence of this evolving 
process in other datasets that were acquired during this study, as the sulfides would have notably 
different metal tenors and the geochemical signatures of the intrusions would have recorded 
variable degrees of metal depletion in different areas. This is not the case. Instead, we suggest that 
Scenario 2 is most likely. Hence, these values reflect a significant input of crustal contamination in 
the ore -forming process, whereby the resulting sulfur isotope signature of t he magmatic sulfides 
reveals mixing between S -bearing country rocks (Tickalara metamorphics ) with either depleted 
mantle ( δ34S = -1.3‰; Labidi et al. (2013) ), a bulk Earth chondritic reservoir ( δ34S = 0‰), or a 
metasomatised continental lithospheric mantle  (δ34S = +2‰; Ionov et al. (1992) ). In any case, from 
a sulfur isotope point of view the Savannah and Nog intrusions reached sulfide supersaturation 
through similar processes.  
4.3.2 Comparison of in situ and bulk data for magmatic sulfides at Savannah 
The significantly different δ34S values between the Savannah and Savannah North massive sulfides 
are crucial in deciding one fundamental question: is the Savannah North orebody a distinctly 
different system from Savannah, or could it have been a tectonically mobiliz ed portion of Savannah 
ores, fortuitously emplaced beneath a different intrusion? The isotopic data are unambiguous: they 
are two distinctly different orebodies, with different S sources. The only complication is that while 
the disseminated sulfides and se mi-massive sulfide -matrix ore samples at Savannah have the same 
S isotopic composition, the blebby disseminated sulfides from Savannah North are distinct from the 
Savannah North semi -massive breccia ores, but match closely with Savannah. The suggestion is that 
the Savannah North magmas may have incorporated some Savannah ore, subsequent to deposition 
of the Savannah North massive ores, which were sourced separately. The Savannah North ores 
overall display a much broader spread of S isotopic composition, imp lying multiple sources.  
In situ and bulk isotope data can be used in conjunction to better assess the sulf ur source 
reservoir(s) that was/were tapped to trigger supersaturation of an immiscible sulfide liquid 
(LaFlamme et al., 2016). In fact, while in situ data provide insights into the intra-grain variability of 
texturally constrained sulfide grains within any given sample, the bulk isotope data provide much 
higher precision on a homogenised aliquot of sample. In this study we combined the two 
techniques: the resulting datasets are consistent with one another, with average values around 
~+3.5‰. Interestingly, all data from magmatic sulfides at Savannah display only positive values, 82   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley unlike the sulf ur isotope signature of komatiite-hosted nickel-sulfide mineralisation, which is 
generally negative (Bekker et al., 2009). Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  83 5 Petrography 
5.1 Petrography of the Savannah area intrusions 
The intrusions of the Savannah area comprise four layered mafic-ultramafic bodies (Savannah, 
Savannah North, Dave Hill and Wilson’s –  see Figure 34) two of which (Savannah and Savannah 
North) contain known economic Ni-Cu sulfide mineralisation, one layered mafic body (Sub-Chamber 
D), a cross cutting mafic dyke-like body (the Turkey Creek Gabbro) and a number of granulite-facies 
small mafic dykes. The Copernicus body is a small mineralised tube-shaped pyroxenite-peridotite 
intrusion some distance to the south of the main cluster of intrusions (and, as we will see, somewhat 
younger). 
The detailed petrology and internal structure of the intrusions are beyond the scope of this paper, 
but we summarise briefly here. The Savannah intrusion is a curved dyke-like body with parallel 
steeply-dipping sides and internal layering normal to the sides. The internal layering dips steeply to 
the north, as does the semi-massive orebody developed along the basal contact. The intrusion is 
interpreted as an originally blade-shaped dyke (Barnes et al., 2016a), with the mineralisation 
developed along the bottom (southern) “blunt” edge. The geometry is similar to tha t reported for 
the Expo suite mineralised dykes in the Cape Smith Belt, north-eastern Canada (Mungall, 2007). The 
lower zone, occupying the lower third of the dyke, consists of interlayered cumulate peridotite, 
troctolite and olivine gabbronorite, giving away upward to a sequence dominated by cumulate 
norite. The Savannah North intrusion, in contrast, consists almost entirely of peridotite and olivine 
gabbros interlayered on 10-100m scale. The geometry is not fully understood, but is probably best 
represented as a steep -sided funnel with original shallow inward dipping layering, now dipping at 
20-40 degrees to the north. The Dave Hill and Wilson’s intrusions contain similar ranges of
interlayered rock types, and also show 10-s to 100 m scale layering. Turkey Creek consists largely of
hornblende-bearing non cumulate gabbronorites, and Sub-Chamber D of shallow-dipping, weakly
layered hornblende gabbronorites ranging from cumulate to non-cumulate textures.
Peridotites from the Savannah and Savannah North intrusions are typical poikilitic olivine cumulates, 
ranging from orthocumulates with zoned interstitial plagioclase to heteradcumulates with little or 
no plagioclase and large oikocrysts of both orthopyroxene and clinopyroxene (Figure 47, Figure 48) 
pyroxenes are typically overgrown by a pale brown aluminous kaersutitic hornblende, and both 
show extensive exsolution of the other pyroxene (opx in cpx, cpx in opx). Peridotites are also present 
in the Wilson’s intrusion with similar textures, but (se Geochemistry se ction). 84   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   
Figure 47: Petrography of interlayered peridotite and troctolite cumulates, lower zone of Savannah Intrusion.  
 
 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimb erley   |  85

oikocryst.  
86   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Troctolites are commonly finely interlayered with peridotites within the lower zone of the Savannah 
intrusion (Figure 47). Troctolites in both Savannah (Figure 47), Savannah North (Figure 48) are 
characteristic olivine-plagioclase mesocumulates with subhedral lathy plagioclase, occasional 
coarse plagioclase phenocrysts or xenocrysts, commonly with a weak lamination and modal layering 
from decimetre to grain scale. Orthopyroxene to hornblende-spinel symplectites (see below) are 
commonly developed at olivine-plagioclase contacts. Clinopyroxene occurs in small proportions as 
a subordinate interstitial phase. Accessory phases such as apatite and ilmenite are present as rare 
scattered grains associated with interstititial clinopyroxene. The lower portion of the Savannah 
intrusion is characterised by metre to ten metres scale interlayering of troctolites and peridotites 
(see appendix for a compilation of XRF images). The troctolites within this interval are 
characteristically laminated with parallel alignment of both plagioclase and olivine. 
Cumulate textures in Dave Hill and Wilson’s intrusions are compared with Savannah and Savannah 
North in Figure 49. Mafic to ultramafic cumulates in the Dave Hill intrusion range from olivine-rich 
troctolites to olivine leuco-norites with prominent orthopyroxene oikocrysts, relatively weak 
lamination, and trace interstitial disseminated sulfides in a few samples. Wilson’s cumulates are 
primarily melatroctolites to plagioclase peridotites with coarse cumulus olivine. The peridotites 
grade continuously into troctolites with primarily interstitial plagioclase, and generally lack 
prominent poikilitic textures. The rocks from both Wilsons and Dave Hill tend to be orthocumulate, 
show zoning of plagioclase, and a higher abundance of accessory interstitial ilmenite and apatite. Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  87 Figure 49. Phase maps (from XRF mapping) of typical peridotites from Dave Hill and Wilson’s intrusions compared 
with Savannah and Savannah North examples. 
Copernicus is dominated by pyroxene-rich peridotites, where orthopyroxene oikocrysts commonly 
make up much of the volume of the rock. Olivine is present as resorbed chadacrysts, in a texture 
very similar to those reported from “oikocrystites” in the Natka Hill intrusion, Tanzania (Barnes et 
al., 2016b). Sulfides are present in the form of typical patchy disseminated to net textured ore 
(Barnes et al., 2017) 
Subchamber D rocks are strongly orthocumulate hornblende gabbronorites to hornblende olivine 
gabbronorites with abundant large hornblende oikocrysts (Figure 50). Olivine is present in some 
samples as cores on orthopyroxene and hornblende grains. Clinopyroxene is relatively abundant as 
granular anhedral to interstitial grains.

Two distinct types of symplectite are present at olivine-plagioclase contacts in all the intrusions and 
are a distinctive feature of almost all samples containing both olivine and plagioclase. The first and 
most common occurs as part of a zoned progression ( 
Figure 51: Textures in hornblende troctolites, Savannah North Intrusion. A) Tornado three element 
map (Ca in red, Fe green, Al blue); b) enlarged area, optical photomicrograph showing olivine-
plagioclase contacts lined by a fine corona of orthopyroxene, and symplectite intergrowth of 
hornblende and MgAl spinel in the distinctive relationship summarised in (c) (after Kretz, 2014). d,e: 
troctolite in which olivine contains fine symplectite intergrowth of orthopyroxene (opx) and

from olivine to orthopyroxene, to a finely intergrown symplectite of hornblende plus aluminous 
spinel ([MgFe]Al 2O4) to plagioclase. Such coronas have been widely reported in mafic and ultramafic 
rocks in high pressure metamorphic terranes, and are generally regarded as being high- P 
metamorphic in origin (Kretz, 2014). The second type is clearly replacing olivine, and consists of a 
symplectic intergrowth of orthopyroxene and a magnetite rich spinel. So far these have only been 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  89 recognised in the Savannah intrusion where they occur in some cases in conjunction with the first 
(amphibole) type. These are undoubtedly formed by oxidation of olivine, probably but not 
necessarily at the late magmatic stage. Similar textures have been reported from the low-pressure, 
un-metamorphosed Skaergaard Intrusion in Greenland (Holness et al., 2011), and from the Black 
Hill gabbro in South Australia (Turner and Stuewe, 1992), implying that they are not necessarily 
indicative of high-P metamorphism. 90   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley

6.1 Geochemical drill hole profiles 
Three drill holes were sampled closely for whole rock geochemistry: Savannah North (SMD 160), 
Dave Hill (SMD165 ) and Wilson’s Intrusion (SMD 166). Profiles are shown in  Figure 52, Figure 53, 
Figure 54. 
6.1.1 Savannah North 
The profile down drill hole SMD 160 (Figure 52) also shows modal mineralogy determined by last-
squares calculation from the whole rock and mineral chemistry data (see below). The profile shows 
wide fluctuation in MgO and an exactly ant-correlative fluctuation in Al 2O3 reflecting changing 
modal mineralogy between peridotite and troctolite down the hole, between depths of between 
about 100 and 900 m.  This interval is overlain by a hornblende gabbronorite unit at the top, and 
interleaved hornblende gabbronorite and troctolite at the bottom. The gabbronorite units both 
correspond to an increase in Zr, reflecting the proportion of liquid to cumulus phases. Based on this 
indicator, the interval between about 300 and 1250 m represents olivine +/- plagioclase 
orthocumulate to mesocumulate rocks with the gabbronorites above and below representing 
solidified liquid compositions, or possibly later intrusions of non-cumulate gabbronorite liquid. 92   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley
