Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  97 6.2 Comparative geochemistry of the intrusions - Major elements  
Most of the samples selected for whole -rock geochemical study are cumulates, raising some 
important considerations for the interpretation of their compositions. Major element chemistry 
needs to be considered in terms of mixing between the cumulus minerals and the interstitial 
trapped liquid componen t, and caution has to be applied in the interpretation of all but the most 
incompatible trace elements. The details of the cumulus mineral chemistry are beyond the scope of 
this paper, but need to be considered to the degree they influence the interpretati on of trace 
element spectra.  
6.2.1 Savannah North, Savannah, Sub -chamber D  
Whole rock compositions from the various cumulate -bearing intrusions are compared with simple 
mixing lines between the major cumulus phases, using average compositions as measured for the  
Savannah and Savannah North intrusions. Mixing lines between orthopyroxene (En ~78) and 
plagioclase (An 81) and between olivine (Fo 81) and plagioclase (An 75) are shown on plots of SiO 2, 
MgO, Na 2O and Mg# vs Al 2O3 (Figure 55). 
 
 
Figure 55. Whole -rock ge ochemical data, major elements plotted against alumina, wt. percent values, no volatile -
free recalculation. Dashed lines are calculated mixing lines for observed average compositions of olivine, 
orthopyroxene and plagioclase in the Savannah and Savannah No rth intrusions. Colour indicates location (intrusion), 
shape indicates rock type . 
 
98   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East K imberley  Two distinct trends are defined by the Savannah North and Savannah cumulates corresponding to 
these two mixing lines, but slightly offset from them. The Savannah norites fro m the northern 
section of the intrusion fall parallel with but consistently to the low Mg, high Na side of the trend, 
while peridotites and olivine gabbros from the southern portion all fall parallel to the olivine mixing 
line. Savannah North peridotites a nd olivine gabbros/gabbronorites mostly fall slightly displaced 
from the olivine -plagioclase mixing line, with a smaller proportion of samples falling towards the 
plagioclase -opx line. Consistent with the petrographic observations, Savannah North and the 
southern portion of Savannah is dominated by olivine - or olivine -plagioclase cumulates, while the 
northern segment of Savannah is dominated by plagioclase -opx cumulates. The slight offset of both 
suites from the pure cumulus mixing line is due to the presen ce of a low but consistently abundant 
trapped intercumulus liquid component.  
The trend of peridotites, troctolites and olivine gabbros from Savannah North on the plot of Mg# vs 
Al2O3 (Figure 55d) is relatively flat. This is significant for the interpretati on of the origin of these 
cumulates. A flat trend implies that the extent of fractionation of the magma, as indicated by the 
Mg#, is relatively limited and independent of the modal mineralogy of the cumulates. The average 
value of Mg# for the peridotite -troctolite trend of around 80 for the Savannah North Intrusion 
olivine -rich cumulates agrees well with the observed distribution of olivine compositions having Fo 
contents mostly between 80 and 82 (see section on mineral chemistry).  This would be expected i f 
the intrusion was emplaced as a suspension of crystals that became mechanically sorted into 
plagioclase -rich and olivine -rich layers or domains. The contrasting scenario would be one where an 
initially olivine saturated magma evolves through in situ frac tional crystallisation into an olivine -
plagioclase saturated magma, whereby the Mg# of the gabbroic (plagioclase cumulus bearing rocks) 
would be systematically lower in Mg# than the olivine -only cumulates. This latter scenario is evident 
in the Savannah cu mulate suite, which does evolve towards lower Mg# (~65, compared with 75 in 
the peridotites) with increasing Al content (as a proxy for increasing plagioclase abundance): the 
norites have systematically lower Mg# values, and hence derived from less primiti ve magmas, than 
the peridotites. Whole rock Mg# values also agree reasonably well with measured olivine and 
orthopyroxene values, which are used to constrain the Mg -rich end member of the linear mixing 
trends plotted on Figure 55 a and c.  
A separate groupi ng of samples with distinctly lower Mg#, higher Na and higher incompatible trce 
elements and TiO 2 is represented by a few samples in all of the intrusions: these are found at the 
northern tip of Savannah, and in the far north west of Savannah North (Figure  55 b). This grouping 
is interpreted to represent non -cumulate gabbros, i.e. the product of more or less isochemical 
crystallisation of mafic liquids. The grouping also includes a few samples from Sub -Chamber D. The 
remaining Subchamber D samples have high  Al2O3 at consistently low Ti, Zr (Figure 55 e, f) and other 
incompatible element concentrations and are interpreted as heteradcumulate hornblende 
gabbronorites.  
An enigmatic group of samples was taken from a body of ultramafic cumulates immediately 
underl ying the Savannah North orebody, with a view to deciding if these were derived from the 
Savannah or Savannah North intrusions. These samples fall predominantly on the olivine (peridotite) 
trend, indicating a clear affinity with Savannah North on the basis of major elements. These samples 
are lumped with the Savannah North peridotites henceforth.   
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  99 Plots of Ni and Cr vs MgO  (Figure 56) show the expected coherent compatible trends, with some 
notable departures. The peridotites of the Savannah North intrusion, a nd the ultramafic cumulates 
beneath the Savannah North orebody, define a distinct group at low Cr and high MgO, an unusual 
feature attributed to inhibition of crystallisation of chromite. This is consistent with the absence of 
chromite from these rocks, bu t is not readily explained. A possible explanation is low oxygen 
fugacity, and consequent speciation of Cr as Cr2+ rather than Cr3+, due to assimilation of graphite 
from country rocks. Graphitic xenoliths have been noted in Savannah North core, and also in  some 
ore samples.  
Most samples fall along a silicate -controlled MgO -Ni trend, with the exception of the ultramafic 
cumulates below Savannah North (these rocks are included within the Savannah North grouping for 
the rest of this chapter). Their higher Ni c ontent is due to the presence of disseminated sulfides. 
Norites from Savannah also fall below the trend due to the lower content of Ni in opx compared 
with olivine. It is noteworthy that all of the samples collected away from the immediate vicinity of 
the orebodies in Savannah North and Savannah lack detectable Ni anomalism due to sulfide. 
 
Figure 56: Plots of Ni and Cr vs MgO in whole rock, no volatile -free recalculation. Colour indicates location (intrusion), 
shape indicates rock type.  
6.2.2 Dave Hill and Wils ons 
The Dave Hill sample suite plots primarily along the norite (plag -opx) trend  (Figure 55) and includes 
orthopyroxenites, olivine norites and norites. Much of the major element chemistry appears to be 
controlled by the abundance of poikilitic orthopyroxe ne forming from peritectic resorption of 
olivine, based on comparison with the observed mineralogy (Figure 49). The suite shares with 
Savannah North the attribute of having a wide range of modal proportions of plagioclase at roughly 
constant Mg#, implying mechanical sorting of crystals rather than fractional crystallisation (Figure 
55), a point further discussed below in the context of mineral chemistry.  The larger Wilson’s sample 
suite consists mainly of peridotites lying on the olivine -plagioclase trend,  although at distinctly lower 
Mg# than both the Savannah and Savannah North peridotites, and with generally higher contents 
of excluded minor and trace elements implying a more orthocumulate character.  
100   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  6.2.3 FeO-MgO relationship and olivine compositions  
Magma an d olivine compositions are investigated further in a plot of FeO vs MgO  (Figure 57). Data 
points are restricted to samples falling along the peridotite -troctolite (olivine -controlled) mixing 
trends from Savannah, Savannah North, Dave Hill and Wilsons. Cons istent with the major element 
trends noted above, data points fall primarily on well -defined linear trends between pure olivine 
and the origin, corresponding to (Mg and Fe -free) plagioclase. Extrapolating these trends to the pure 
olivine end member is cons istent with average olivine compositions as measured for Savannah and 
Savannah North samples, although the Savannah North data contains a component with somewhat 
higher MgO for given FeO implying original olivine compositions up to about Fo85, somewhat mor e 
magnesian that the most forsteritic compositions measured. This discrepancy can be explained by 
the effect of reaction of cumulus olivine with trapped liquid (Barnes, 1986a ), which cause the final 
composition to be slightly less magnesian than the origin al cumulus component that defines the 
intercept of the mixing line. This plot confirms that the Savannah North magmas and olivines are 
distinctly more primitive (more magnesian) than those of Savannah and Wilsons; Wilsons, Savannah 
and Dave Hill (probably,  given the small number of samples) are indistinguishable, with original 
cumulus olivine compositions within a narrow range from about Fo78 to Fo81.

Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  101 Also shown on the FeO-MgO plot are samples of non-cumulate gabbros as defined above. Two non-
cumulate gabbros from Savannah have FeO/MgO ratios (Figure 57) in equilibrium with olivine 
compositions around Fo75-Fo80, consistent with being close to parent liquid compositions for the 
more evolved end of the spectrum of Savannah olivine-plagioclase cumulates. These samples 
average 48% SiO2, 16.6% Al2O3, 12.2% FeO, 7.4% MgO and 10.5% CaO, compositions fairly typical 
of high-alumina tholeiites in island arc settings. 
6.3 Comparative geochemistry of the intrusions - Trace elements 
Rare earth element (REE) and other incompatible trace element spectra from all samples from the 
various intrusions are plotted in Figure 58 and Figure 59 , and summarised as averages by rock type 
for the intrusions in Figure 60 and Figure 61. Ro cks of the various intrusions are divided into multiple 
groupings based on lithology and host intrusion. 
Peridotite-troctolite group 
Comprising the lower part of the Savannah intrusion, most of Savannah North and the Dave Hill 
peridotites . Patterns for this suite from Savannah, Savannah North and Dave Hill are virtually 
indistinguishable (Figure 59) being marked by overall LREE enrichment, mild HREE depletion (Yb 
depleted over Gd), pronounced negative Nb anomalies, moderate negative Hf-Zr anomalies, and a 
distinctive zig-zag pattern for Pb, Rb, Ba, Th and K. This suite is most obviously related to 
mineralisation . Norites from Wilson’s share this pattern, althoug h the peridotites have distinctly 
different patterns lacking HFSE (Zr, Hf, Nb, Ta) anomalies. 
Noritic Group 
Noritic (plagioclase-opx) cumulates, comprising the upper (northern) portion of the Savannah 
intrusion, most of the Dave Hill cumulates, and the cumulate rocks from Sub-chamber D, with minor 
components also present in Savannah North and Wilson’s  (Figure 58, Figure 59, Figure 60). Savannah 
and Savannah North norite suite rocks have very similar trace element patterns to the peridotite-
troctolite suite in these intrusions, sharing their distinctive negative Nb and Zr-Hf anomalies (Figure 
58, Figure 60). The Turkey Creek Gabbro also appears to have affinities with this group (Figure 61) 
and has very similar trace element patterns to the more orthocumulate rocks in the Savannah 
intrusions and Dave Hill (Figure 61). This group has consistent smoothly sloping LREE-enriched 
patterns lacking pronounced positive Eu anomalies. Dave Hill differs somewhat from the others in 
having strongly positive Eu anomalies in the norites, but has otherwise broadly similar patterns to 
Savannah and Savannah North. 
Copernicus 
Predominantly olivine cumulates showing extensive peritectic reaction to orthopyroxene and 
having broadly similar trace element patterns to the Savannah Intrusions, Dave Hill and Turkey 
Creek, but differing in lacking the distinctive negative Zr- Hf anomaly. The Wilson’s peridotite -
troctolite suite bears a closer resemblance to Copernicus than to the other intrusions, 
notwithstanding the age relationships (Wils on’s being the same age as the Savannah intrusions and 102   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  Dave Hill but older than Copernicus). The Copernicus trace element patterns in particular show 
some distinctive similarities to those of the Tickalara Metamorphics  (Figure 61). 
Early mafic dykes  
Non cu mulate rocks having concave -up REE with mild LREE depletion and depletion of HREE over 
MREE similar the major intrusions, and notably depleted relative abundances of the most highly 
incompatible elements K, Th, Ba and Rb (Figure 61). 
 
Surprisingly, the oli vine -bearing cumulates from Savannah have more of a positive Eu anomaly 
(associated with the accumulation of plagioclase) than the norites, which actually no Eu anomaly 
despite clearly containing a large cumulus plagioclase component. The implication is th at the parent 
magmas of the two halves of the Savannah intrusion had divergent histories of plagioclase 
accumulation, but were derived from similar parent magmas. Significantly, norites from all of the 
major intrusions have very similar average REE pattern s aside from small variations in the Eu 
anomaly; none have pronounced positive Eu anomalies and all have more or less parallel patterns 
with mild LREE enrichment. The norites are best interpreted as a mixture of accumulated plagioclase 
with residual liquid  that had already lost Eu preferentially to fractionated plagioclase.  
The norite and peridotite -troctolite trends from the Savannah intrusions and from Dave Hill, 
SubChamber D, and the Turkey Creek gabbro  (Figure 61) have remarkably similar trace element 
spectra as described above for the peridotite -troctolite grouping, and all can reasonably be 
concluded to be essentially co -magmatic. The norite trend is evidently derived from more evolved 
products of essentially the same magmas as the peridotite trend sa mples.

Magmatic sulfide mineral potentia l in the East Kimberley   |  107 7 Extent of Contamination by Tickalara 
metamorphics ? 
An initial hypothesis to be tested by this data set is that the different suites have been contaminated 
to varying degrees by Tickalara paragneiss country rock. This hypot hesis is tested in a series of plots 
of incompatible trace element ratios (Figure 62). These ratios are of elements that are not present 
in any of the cumulus phases, so to a first approximation they are invariant to addition or subtraction 
of those phases ; contamination trends should show as more or less linear mixing lines between 
primitive magmas and Tickalara compositions, outlined on the various plots on Figure 62. 
A weak, broadly linear trend from primitive mantle ratios towards average Tickalara meta morphics 
is seen on the Pearce Element Ratio plot of Th/Yb vs Nb/Yb in Figure 62 (Pearce, 2008 ), a plot that 
is commonly used as a discriminant for continental crust contamination of mantle derived magmas. 
However, convincing trends are not evident on any of the other ratio plots (Figure 62). The analysis 
is not diagnostic, given the wide variability in the Tickalara samples, but the lack of clear linear 
mixing trends from the bulk of the Savannah and Savannah North samples towards Tickalara 
compositions (w ith the exception of a small number of outlying samples) does not provide 
convincing support for a contamination model. In fact the range of element ratios for most of the 
intrusion samples is remarkably narrow. The norite suite, which on the basis of its higher silica 
contents would be the obvious candidate for a contamination origin, shows no more indication of 
Tickalara input than any other group, confirming the interpretation from the major element data 
that it merely represents more evolved products of  the same parent magmas.  
Further indications are evident from the mantle -normalised trace element spidergrams (Figure 60, 
Figure 61); the Tickalara paragneisses have extremely high ratios of particular elements such as K 
and Th to Nb and Ta, and Hf to Ti and Yb, that are not apparent in the intrusions. Concentrations of 
K, Th and Hf are so high in the paragneisses that even small amounts of contamination should 
significantly perturb these ratios. The lack of a discernible positive Th anomaly in Savannah or  
Savannah North is evidence against a major component of contamination by the local Tickalara 
metasediments, insofar as they are represented by the small suite of samples for which we have 
data. The notable exception is Copernicus, which does show a very s ubstantial negative anomaly of 
Nb and Ta relative to K and Th, and lacks the negative Zr -Hf signal. Contamination with Tickalara 
rocks is consistent with this pattern.  
In conclusion, the overall patterns of trace element distribution, notwithstanding prev iously 
published data on Os isotopes in the Savannah ores (Sproule et al., 2000 ) do not appear to suggest 
a major component of crustal contamination from the immediately adjacent Tickalara paragneisses 
for the Savannah area intrusions with the exception of  Copernicus and probably Wilson’s. However, 
it must be stressed that the Tickalara suite contains an extremely diverse range of lithologies, and it 
is unlikely that our suite of samples effectively represents it. It therefore remains possible but 
untestabl e with our data set that much of the range within and between intrusions could be 
explicable by contamination of a relatively restricted range of mantle melts. Notwithstanding, we 
are confident that the distinctive trace element characteristics of the Sava nnah and Savannah North 
intrusions in particular is not greatly modified from their original mantle magmatic signature, and 108   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley hence we can use this signature to infer the tectonic setting and petrogenesis of these magmas. This 
topic is considered in the following section.  
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  109  
Figure 62: Trace element ratios, normalised to McDonough and Sun (1995) chondrite abundances compared with 
data density fields for continental arc mafic -intermediate magmas from the GeoROC online database.  
  
110   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  7.1 Summary: petrogenesis and mine ral potential of the Savannah area 
intrusions.  
Based on the trace element data, it is conclusive that the peridotite -troctolite group and the norite 
groups are derived from a common magma source, or at least from basically similar parental 
magmas. Possibly  the norite group experienced higher degrees of crustal contamination from a 
quartz -rich source that increased its SiO 2 content without appreciably affecting the REE pattern or 
other trace elements. The Tickalara paragneisses, or at least those that we hav e sampled, cannot 
be reliably identified as the contaminant, however. The coherence of the trace element patterns 
within and between the different intrusions is sufficient to say that contamination has not radically 
modified the magmas from their original magmatic compositions.  
The peridotite group shows the clearest association with mineralisation of the 1845 age intrusions. 
On this basis the Wilson’s Intrusion might be regarded as having higher potential than the Dave Hill 
intrusion. On the other hand, th e Dave Hill intrusion shows strong trace element evidence of being 
co-magmatic with the Savannah bodies. Hence notwithstanding its predominantly noritic character, 
it should be regarded as the highest priority target of the so -far ore -free intrusions. The strong 
possibility exists of mineralisation within a hidden peridotite component in Dave Hill.  
The petrogenetic affinity of the Savannah area intrusions is debatable, but the weight of evidence 
tends to favour a subduction connection. In several aspects of  their trace element chemistry, 
notably strong negative anomalies in Nb and Ta and in Zr, Hf and Ti (Figure 61), and in elevated Rb 
and Pb (elements that can be enriched in supra -arc mantle due to mobilisation within fluids driven 
off the descending slab),  the parent magmas show distinct indications of having subduction -related 
origins as island arc tholeiites. The distinctly geochemically different early mafic dykes could be 
interpreted in this framework as having a back -arc origin.  
Some of the geochemica l features of the Savannah group magmas could be explained by continental 
crustal contamination of mantle -plume derived magmas, but as we have seen the evidence for 
extensive continental crustal contamination is ambiguous. Based on the prolonged nature of the 
mafic magmatism in the Halls Creek orogen, there is no evidence for a major large igneous province 
related to a plume arrival event, and there is no indication of age -progressive magmatism that could 
be construed as plate movement over a plume tail. Th e presence of abundant poikilitic hornblende 
in the Sub  Chamber D intrusion implies that at least some of the parent magmas had high water 
contents. Taken together, these lines of evidence favour a supra -subduction zone rather than a 
plume origin for the m agmas. The pulse of magmatism at 1845 Ma could be related to a slab 
detachment event, as has been suggested for broadly similar Ni -related mafic magmatism in the 
Central Asian Orogenic Belt  (Li et al., 2012 ; Mao et al., 2017 ). 
7.2 Petrogenetic affinity of the Savannah area intrusions  
Bearing in mind the caveat noted above that most of the rocks under consideration are cumulates, 
we use ratios of incompatible element ratios that are relatively insensitive to fractionation or 
accumulation of the dominant cumulus phases in the intrusions, i.e. olivine, orthopyroxene and 
plagioclase. Since some of the elements used do have some degree of minor compatibility in 
pyroxenes, we first need to consider to what degree this might bias the conclusions. Plotting a Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimber ley  |  111 variety of trace element ratios, including ratios involving Yb which is the least incompatible of the 
REE in pyroxene (Bédard, 1994a), shows no significant correlation between the highly incompatible 
element Ba (an indicator of trapped liquid abundance) and any of the element ratios, and also shows 
no systematic relationship to cumulus mineral assemblages. On this basis, we can confidently use 
trace element patterns and ratios as indicators of petrogenetic affinity, and specifically to address 
the question of whether the magmas are plume derived, or whether there affinities are more 
characteristic of supra-subduction arc magmas. 
We address this question by comparing potentially diagnostic combinations of incompatible trace 
element ratios with data density contours from published data, drawn from the online GeoROC 
compilation (Sarbas, 2008), for three tectonic settings: continental arcs, intra-oceanic island arcs, 
and continental flood basalts interpreted as products of intraplate plumes, following a similar 
protocol to Barnes et al. ( 2012 ) (Figure 62, Figure 63, Figure 64). 
Based on comparison with the literature data, it is clear that most of the Savannah areas show the 
closest similarities with the continental arc suite, where they overlap closely on almost all of the 
diagnostic element ratios; somewhat less closely with oceanic arcs, and least closely with 
continental flood basalts. On the latter, the major differences are in ratios involving Nb, Yb and La, 
the Savannah data showing distinctly higher degrees of Nb depletion. There is an almost complete 
correspondence between the main array of Savannah data and the peak distributions on all the 
selected ratio plots with the continental arc population, the only difference being a tendency for 
the Savannah grouping to have slightly lower Gd/Yb ratios (i.e. less pronounced HREE depletion) 
than the GeoROC dataset. This may be explicable in part by slight HREE enrichment in the cumulus 
pyroxene component within the Savannah suite (see mineral chemistry chapter), although as noted 
above this effect is not detectable when comparing olivine and pyroxene cumulate lithologies from 
the same intrusion. The contrast is therefore likely to reflect parent magmas, and may reflect either 
relatively shallow levels of derivation (less extensive garnet stability in the source) or a less hydrated 
source (ditto for amphibole).  
Looking more closely at the different groupings within the Savannah suite, and considering overall 
average trace element patterns on the spidergrams in Figure 60 and Figure 61, demonstrates the 
existence of two distinguishable magma suites, along with a range of outliers: 
(1) Flat to slightly depleted N-MORB to E-MORB-like patterns, represented mainly by the
first-emplaced mafic granulite dykes, and also including some samples from Turkey Creek and 
Wilsons;  
(2) Continental arc-like characteristics (Sub-chamber-D, Savannah, Savannah North, Dave
Hill, most of Wilsons, Copernicus), having a relatively tight distribution with limited contamination 
for immediately adjacent Tickalara metasediments; 
More dispersed outlying composition include samples from SubChamber D, Turkey Creek Gabbro 
and Savannah (mainly norites and gabbros); these form a distinctive grouping on the plot of Ba/Th 
vs Nd/Hf, these ratios being indicative of high field strength element depletion. 
The first group, most clearly represented by the mafic granulite dykes, appears to represent 
relatively pristine mantle-derived magma, with primitive mantle (flat) to E-MORB characteristics, 
suggesting derivation from decompression of an ambient mantle source. The Turkey Creek Gabbro 
shows minor characteristics of arc or crustal rocks, suggesting either an origin related to an arc or 112   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  contamination by arc -related rocks, such as the Tickalara metasediments. The mafic granu lites have 
flat trace element profiles, slightly enriched LREE profiles, and fall around the MORB -OIB array on 
the Nb -Th-Yb plot of Pearce (2008) ( Figure 65), suggesting an uncontaminated, but slightly enriched, 
mantle source. The mafic granulites have sim ilar compositions to E -MORB (i.e. plume -enriched 
oceanic mantle).  
The second group encompasses the majority of intrusions, and the major Ni -Cu mineralized 
intrusions in the Savannah district. These intrusions have trace element characteristics with strong 
affinities to typical mafic magmas within continental arc suites, overprinted by a variable and largely 
unknown imprint of contamination from continental crust. There is no compelling evidence for the 
operation of a mantle plume; trace element characterist ics are much more similar to arc magmas 
of know affinity than to continental flood basalts typical of intraplate plume provinces.

118   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley 8.1.1 Nickel in olivine 
Nickel in olivine data (Figure 68) are particularly interesting in potentially indicating sulfide 
segregation and equilibration history. This plot shows sample averages for electron microprobe 
data, and also the fields for Ni content obtained from laser ablation analysis described in detail 
below. Compared with a global database of olivine compositions from volcanic rocks and layered 
intrusions (Maier et al., 2016), all of the four main intrusions plot either within or slightly to the low 
Fo, high- Ni side of the main global data array. In the case of the Savannah, Wilson’s and Dave Hill 
samples, the high Ni is probably attributable to the effects of reaction of olivine with intercumulus 
trapped liquid (Barnes, 1986b). This has the consequence of lowering the Fo content of the olivine 
by as much as 10%, depending on the olivine mode and trapped liquid content, with a relatively 
small change in the Ni content. If the rock contains disseminated sulfides, then this effect will 
produce an increase in the Ni content of olivine owing to the exchange of Fe and Ni between sulfide 
and olivine (Barnes and Naldrett, 1985 ; Li and Naldrett, 1999), accounting for the unusually high Ni 
content of the Savannah peridotite zone samples with over 3000 ppm Ni. Hence the Ni signal of the 
Ni-enriched Savannah intrusion olivines is indicative of equilibration with both olivine and 
intercumulus liquid, but the interpretation of the field of laser-analysed olivines is ambiguous. 
The data for the Savannah North samples is likewise ambiguous. The samples analysed contain no 
sulfides, but have probably been modified to at least some degree by trapped liquid reaction, hence 
being shifted to the left on the Ni-Fo plot. Hence it appears that a proportion of the Savannah North 
samples lie towards the low-Ni portion of the global array, and indeed overlap with olivines from 
the upper mafic-ultramafic intrusion overlying the Nova-Bollinger orebody (Maier et al., 2016 ). 
The Dave Hill and Wilson’s samples fall on the overlap between the Savannah North and Savannah 
intrusions. There is no clear indication from either sample set that they have equilibrated with 
sulfides, or record any systematic depletion of the parent magma in Ni due to prior sulfide 
extraction. Two of the Dave Hill samples fall within the Savannah North field, but given the likely 
extent of trapped liquid equilibration cannot be conclusively interpreted as being Ni-depleted. Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  119 8.2 Laser ablation data - Savannah and Savannah North

Mineral proportions (olivine, orthopyroxene, plagiocl ase, clinopyroxene, amphibole and biotite) 
were recalculated for each sample used for LA -ICP-MS analysis by using constrained least squares 
inverse method  (Albarede, 1995 ). The mineral proportions are calculated based on whole -rock 
geochemistry and major a nd trace element mineral chemistry obtained by SEM and LA -ICP-MS 
analysis (using SiO 2, TiO 2, Al 2O3, FeO Total, MnO, MgO, CaO, Na 2O, K 2O, P 2O5, NiO and Cr 2O3 
concentrations). In that case, the mineral proportions (F Min)are calculated using the following 
form ulas (Albarede, 1995 ). 
Fmin= Fmin0 +λ(ATA)−1J 
 
where:  
A is the mineral composition matrix (A 12 x 6 in this case)  
J is a vector unity (J 12) 
Fmin0 =(ATA)−1ATY 
 
Y is the whole -rock composition vector (Y 12 in this case)  
λ is the Lagrange multiplier  with 𝜆=(1−𝐽𝑇𝐹𝑚𝑖𝑛 0)

Magmatic sulfid e mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  129 8.4 Inferring resident magma compositions  
The trace element concentrations of the parental  (or resident)  liquid of an int rusion can be 
estimated using mineral analysis (e.g. Lambert and Simmons, 1988 ; Mathez, 1995 ). The 
concentration (CiLiq) of an element i in the liquid from which a cumulus mineral formed was 
calculated by assuming chemical equilibrium between the mineral (Min) and the liquid (Liq) by using 
the following equation.  
𝐶𝐿𝑖𝑞𝑖=𝐶𝑀𝑖𝑛𝑖
𝐷𝑀𝑖𝑛 /𝐿𝑖𝑞𝑖      (1) 
where 𝐶𝑀𝑖𝑛𝑖 is the concentration of the element i in a cumulus mineral and 𝐷𝑀𝑖𝑛 /𝐿𝑖𝑞𝑖 is the partition 
coefficient between the mineral and the liquid.  
The equation (1) assume s that the composition of the mineral has not changed after its 
accumulation. However, several studies (e.g. Bach and Fruh -Green, 2010 ; Barnes, 1986b ; Bédard, 
1994b , 2001 ; Cawthorn, 1996 ) have shown that the cumulus minerals may react with the trapped 
intercumulus liquid (“trapped liquid shift” effect, Barnes (1986b) ). The trapped liquid crystallizes 
interstitial phases and may produce overgrowths on cumulus crystals which are both enriched in 
incompatible elements. Similarly , the minerals may homogenize du ring sub -solidus processes. If 
these post -mineral accumulation modification occurs, the final concentration of an element in the 
“cumulus” minerals will be a weighted average of liquidus and post -cumulus composition and hence 
estimation of the parental liq uid composition from equation (1) will be biased. In order to take the 
above processes into account, two additional methods of calculation have been developed and uses 
minerals and whole -rocks trace element geochemistry (Methods 2 and 3 described in Godel et al. 
(2011) ). Results for the Savannah and NOG intrusions are provided in  Figure 78. 130   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   
Figure 78: Mantle normalized trace element patterns of the inferred resident magmas from which the cumulate rocks 
from Savannah and North Olivine Gabbro (NOG) intrusio ns. 
  
 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  131 9 Oxygen Isotopes  
All results presented in Supplementary Materials T able S 4. 
9.1 Results  
Oxygen isotopes (18O/16O, ‰) were collected on zircons from three Savannah, three Savannah 
North, two Turkey Creek Gabbro, two Tickalara metasediments, the microgranit e (900F) an d the 
mafic dyke (900F) samples.  
The detrital zircons of the Tickalara samples show a wide range in δ18O. Ignoring zircons >  1900 Ma, 
as these likely represent distal detritus, the zircons yield δ18O of 6.91 to 10.89  ‰. However, it is 
important to highlight that these data are representative of the magma in which the detrita l grain 
formed in, not necessarily the ‘whole -rock’ δ18O value of the Tickalara siliciclastics, which may range 
to > 20 ‰ (Eiler, 2001 ; Valley et al., 2005a ; Valley et al., 20 05b) in line with collated values (Eiler, 
2001 ). This may explain why some zircons from Savannah and Savannah North have δ18O values 
greater than that for the Tickalara detrital zircons  (Figure 79). 
The Savannah North data show a range from 5.62 to as high  as 12.81 ‰, although all data except 
one point (Figure 79) are < 10 ‰, with the median value of all data 6.93 ‰ ± 0.48 (average error) 
or ± 1.5 (standard deviation of dataset + median of individual errors). The NOG11 sample 
demonstrates a consistently hig her range of δ18O at 7.89 to 9.58 ‰, with no samples overlapping 
with the mantle field. This is in contrast to the two other Savannah North samples, NOG1 and 
NOG23, which have δ18O ranges 5.62 - 7.72 and 5.93 - 7.18, respectively, with the lighter end -
memb ers overlapping the mantle zircon field (Figure 79). 
Data from Savannah show a similar range in δ18O from 5.95 to 11.7  ‰, but again, only five zircons 
show >  8 ‰. The median value of all data is slightly higher than Savannah North at 7  ‰ ±  0.43 or ±  
1.45. As at Savannah North, two samples (SAV -21 and SAV -9) demonstrate lighter arrays at 6.27 -
7.23  ‰ and 5.94 -6.89  ‰, with SAV15 slightly higher at 7.18 -8.69  ‰ (excluding data >  9 ‰). 
The Turkey Creek Gabbro demonstrates a more restricted range of δ18O values, w ith none >  8 ‰, 
with TG7 at 5.36 -7.52  ‰ and TG -8 at 5.99 -7.97  ‰. The grains/rims interpreted as metamorphic 
show similar range  of δ18O values from 6.53 to 7.96  ‰, albeit with a s lightly higher median at 7.53  
‰, as opposed to 7.09  ‰. 
The ca. 1830 Ma microgr anite has a similar range of δ18O to that of the Turkey Creek Gabbro, at 5.62 
to 7.41  ‰, with one value much heavier at 10. 26 ‰. This sample has a median value of 6.87 ‰, 
with lighter values overlapping with the mantle zircon field (Figure 79). 132   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley

Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  133 9.2 Discussion on isotopic constr aints  
9.2.1 Oxygen isotope data  
The interpretation of the Savannah intrusion as having formed from moderately contaminated arc 
magmas is supported by the δ18O data. The majority of data falls below 10 ‰, and forms arrays 
from the mantle field towards heavier, low -temperature altered crustal material (sediments that 
have experience the hydrosphere), i.e. seafloor sediments. We suggest that the majority of δ18O 
data represents mantle -wedge generated magmas (mantle -like at δ18O ca. 5.3 ‰) that interacted 
with subducted ocean sediments ( δ18O ca. up to ca. 40 ‰ for cherts, 20  ‰ for siliciclastics; Eiler 
(2001) ). This mixture is dominated by the mantle -material du e to mass balance, resulting in 
continuous arrays from mantle values to ca. 8‰. Scattered, relatively rare zircons that plot above 
ca. 8 -10 ‰ may represent discrete localized incorporations of the Tickalara metasediments on 
emplacement, with the high value s preserved relative to source contamination as no magma 
transport process occurred to homogenise the magma. This homogenization process from source 
to emplacement may explain why the δ18O arrays of NOG -11 and SAV15 do not intersect the mantle 
field and form heavier arrays from 7.9 -9.6 ‰ and 7.2 -8.7 ‰, respectively. These samples may have 
been more greatly mixed and homogenized during magma transport. Alternatively, incorporation 
of Tic kalara metamorphics in an efficiently mixing or converting magma chamber may have added 
heavier material and also homogenized the signal. In any event, the heavier character of these δ18O 
data are suggestive of a subducted sedimentary component, supported by the lack of clear 
contamination from the country rocks. Clearly contamination would have taken place, but it appears 
that source signatures overwhelmed that of the contaminant, suggesting that this process was 
relatively minor.  
9.2.2 Other isotopic systems  
Sproule et al. (1999)  report Re -Os isotopic data on the ores from the Savannah sulfide orebody 
(known at the time as Sally Malay), and also Nd isotope data from the host intrusion. Massive sulfide 
ores yield exceptionally radiogenic initial Os isotopic compo sitions Os +950 to +1300, whereas 
disseminated sulfide mineralization and unmineralised troctolite and peridotite yield lower Os 
values +60 to +470, interpreted by Sproule et al as reflecting a large component of crustaly 
assimilated Os. In contrast, Nd isotopes show a limited range of mantle -like values of Nd from -0.2 
to +0.7, consistent with values obtained from other East Kimberley intrusions. Sproule et al. 
reconcile these results by crustal contamination of a magma derived from original mantle havi ng 
Nd less than +4, contaminated with crust having Nd of the order of -10. These conditions would 
be consistent with moderate degrees of contamination of a magma derived from metasomatised 
arc mantle. The highly radiogenic Os signature probably reflects an originally very low Os content of 
the primary magma; arc -derived mafic magmas are characteristically very poor in Os, so a small 
amount of crustal contamination, particularly from Re -enriched metasediments, would go a long 
way in shifting Os isotopes to  highly radiogenic compositions.  134   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  10 Geochronology Results

10.1 Mafic -ultramafic  intrusions  
Eight mafic -ultramafic intr usions were dated in this study ( Table  5).Three samples from the 
mineralized Savannah intrusion were dated at 1843 ± 6, 1846 ± 5 and 1844 ± 5 Ma, with three 
samples of the proximal Savannah North intrusion within error of these at 1839 ± 5, 1846 ± 6 and 
1842 ± 5 Ma.  
Two samples of the minerali zed Copernicus pyroxenite yielded crystallisation ages of 1823 ± 3 and 
1823 ± 3 Ma (identical).  
In terms of (currently) unmineralised intrusions, Dave Hill yielded an age of 1839 ± 6, Wilson’s 1847 
± 3 and 1848 ± 5 Ma, sub -chamber -D 1844 ± 3 Ma, and the Tu rkey Creek gabbro 1843 ± 5 and 1848 
± 5 Ma (magmatic zircon cores). It should be noted that zircons from the Turkey Creek gabbro display 
resorbed textures; hence these may be inherited grains from the Savannah/Savannah North 
intrusions that were resorbed i n the mafic Turkey Creek magma.  
Two samples of mafic granulite, interpreted as early mafic dykes based on cross -cutting 
relationships, have igneous crystallisation ages of 1842 ± 4 and 1838 ± 10 Ma (magmatic grains and 
cores).  
In an attempt to discriminate  the ages of the Savannah and Savannah North intrusions, high -
resolution thermal ionization mass spectrometry (TIMS) is currently underway on the same zircons 
as dated by SHRIMP.  
10.2 Cross -cutting (late) dykes  
Three cross -cutting dykes were dated in this study . Two (dolerite and a microgranite) were sampled 
from within the 900 Fault and cross -cut the Savannah and Savannah North intrusions. The third is a 
gabbro -dioritic dyke that cross -cuts the Dave Hill intrusion.  
The dolerite and microgranite yielded U -Pb ag es of 1842  ± 3 and 1829  ± 3 Ma, respectively, with the 
diorite dyke the same as the microgranite at 1829  ± 3 Ma.  
10.3 Metasedimentary  rocks  
Two samples of the Tickalara metasedimentary rocks, which make up the country rock of the region 
and the majority of the Central Zone, were dated to constrain the age of any xenocrysts that might 
occur in the mafic -ultramafic rocks, and to confirm that zircons dated in these intrusions were not 
inherited.

10.4 Metamorphic  ages  
While typical new -growth metamorphic zircons were rare -absent, recrystallized magmatic grains 
and metamorph ic rims were identified and dated within the mafic granulites, Tickalara 
metasediments, and the Turkey Creek Gabbro. In the metasedimentary rocks, discrete metamorphic 
zircons and rims yielded metamorphic ages at 1862  ± 6, 1864  ± 15 and 1838  ± 13 Ma (singl e rim) 
were identified. The two older ages are within error of the maximum depositional age, and hence 
may have formed shortly after sediment deposition, although burial and heating would have had to 136   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  be rapid, or more likely as detrital metamorphic zircons . The younger age is more likely to have 
formed in -situ within the sediment.  
Metamorphic ages from disturbed, recrystallized or new -growth zircon rims from the mafic 
granulites and Turkey Creek Gabbro, are 1837  ± 9, 1844  ± 5 and 1846  ± 9 Ma, respectively. This 
suggests that high -temperature metamorphism and mafic -ultramafic magmatism were coeval. The 
rims on zircons from the Turkey Creek Gabbro may be related to transport of inherited ca. 1845 Ma 
grains in a mafic magma, whereby the magma resorbed and reset  the outside of inherited zircons. 
If this is the case, then the rims yield the age of Turkey Creek magmatism, which is within error of 
speculated inherited grains. Hence the Turkey Creek gabbro is pa rt of the 1845 ma event, rather 
than the later 1830 Ma ‘ Halls Creek’ magmatism.  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  137 Figure 80: Combined results from the geochronology study of the Savannah magmatic camp.

Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  139 10.5 Discussion 
10.5.1  Timing of magmatism in the Savannah area, Central Zone, Halls Creek Orogen 
The oldest rocks in the study area comprise the Tickalara metamorphics, a complexly deformed and 
metamorphosed formation that includes granites (i.e. Rose Bore granite), basalts (pillowed, MORB 
geochemistry), volcaniclastics and siliciclastic (turbiditic) rocks (Tyler et al., 2012). These 
metasediments have a maximum depositional age of ca. 1870 Ma, and their geochemistry and 
oxygen isotopes in zircons (ca. 1870 Ma group) suggest an arc origin, based on the heavy character 
of the oxygen (up to ca. 10 ‰), this is more likely to be a continental rather than oceanic arc (see 
compilation in Hollis et al. (2017)). The MORB -like geochemistry of basalts (Page et al., 1994) from 
this formation indicates a more complex tectonic origin.  
These sediments have detrital zircons from 2810 to <1944 Ma, suggesting proximity to Archean 
continental crust during deposition, rather than formation in an isolated, expansive intra-oceanic 
environment, supporting a continental arc derivation. These sediments contain metamorphic 
zircons and discrete rims suggesting high-temperature metamorphism at ca. 1865-1860 Ma and ca. 
1840 Ma. It is possible that the former is on grains that were included as detrital zircons, and hence 
my not represent an ‘in -situ’ metamorphic event, but as the latter age is younger we suggest that 
this corresponds to metamorphism of the Tickalara metsediments. This is supported by 
metamorphic rims on Tickalara zircons from Bodorkos et al. (1999) at ca. 1845 Ma. The ca. 1860 Ma 
event correlates with the Hooper Orogeny constrained for the Western Zone (Bodorkos et al., 1999 ; 
Tyler et al., 1999), and suggests that the Tickalara either underwent this event, or received zircons 
from rocks that had. This suggests the Western and Central zones had collided, or were already 
together, by ca. 1860 Ma. 
This volcano-sedimentary sequence was then intruded by numerous large mafic-ultramafic 
intrusions ranging in age from 1848-1838 Ma, essentially synchronously (all data within error) 
forming an overall emplacement age of 1844±2 Ma (MSWD 1.6; 14 samples). This age correlates 
with the metamorphic ages acquired here and suggests that high-temperature (low pressure) 
metamorphism was coincident with mafic-ultramafic  magmatism.  
Geochronological data from the 1823 Ma Copernicus pyroxenite, 1829 Ma microgranite and 1829 
Ma mafic-diorite dyke indicate bimodal mafic-ultramafic to felsic magmatism continued after the 
ca. 1845 Ma ‘Savannah Event’. These age data correlate with the onset of the Halls Creek Orogen at 
ca. 1830 (to 1808 Ma; Phillips et al. (2016a)) As stated above, geochemistry from Copernicus and 
the late dyke (no data available on the microgranite) suggest an arc-like origin but if the Halls Creek 
Orogen initated at ca. 1830 Ma, as suggested by the age of Sally Downs Supersuite granitoids then 
subduction would have ceased. Subsequently, we suggest that the Ni-Cu mineralized Copernicus 
pyroxenites, and possibly the mafic dyke, formed during a period of voluminous, hydrous mantle-
wedge-like magmatism associated with slab break-off (van Hunen and Allen, 2011) related to final 
collision of the Western-Central Zones and the Eastern Zone (see tectonic evolution section, Part V 
–Section3). We interpret the microgranite as part of the Sally Downs Supersuite.140   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  10.5.2  Timing of metamorphism  
Discrete metamorphic zircons are rare to absent in rocks analysed in this study. Identification of 
metamorphic events are based upon disturbed, reset or recystallised grains/rims or growth of  
discrete homogenous zircon rims. Interpretation of these is based primarily on textural 
characteristics as well as Th/U ratios (typically, but not always, <0.2 in metamorphic zircon). 
Identification of these rims and rare grains constrained twp episodes o f metamorphism at ca. 1860 -
1860 Ma and 1840 -1845 Ma.  
The first event correlates with the 1870 -1850 Ma Hooper Orogeny (Tyler et al., 2012 ), which 
deformed the 1855 Ma Whitewater Volcanics in the Western Zone. In the Central Zone, the first 
deformation post -dates the ca. 1863 Ma Rose Bore Granite, but pre -dates the 1850 Ma Dougalls 
Suite, which can also be shown the pre -date the second deformation.  
The second metamorphic event is synchronous with the intrusion of mafic -ultramafic intrusions as 
documented here , and has been constrained as high temperature (low pressure) by Bodorkos et al. 
(1999) . This is also coincident with the Koongie Park volcanics (and associated VMS mineralization; 
Orth (2002) ) and ca. 1850 Ma Dougalls TTG -granite suite (Sheppard et al., 2 001) to the south of 
Savannah in the Central Zone. Together, this tectonothermal event is indicative of a syn -magmatic 
metamorphic events constrained in other areas such as the Fraser range, Australia (Clark et al., 
2014 ) and Hidaka Metamorphic Belt in Jap an (Kemp et al., 2007 ). These events are widely associated 
with back -arc environments, supporting the interpretation presented for the ca. 1845 Ma magmatic 
rocks. It is possible to infer that, although the magmatic age of the mafic granulites is within err or 
of the other intrusions, that these dykes are slightly older. This is because they have been 
metamorphosed to granulite facies (granular textures) whilst the other intrusions demonstrate 
primary magmatic textures, possibly suggesting peak metamorphism b etween the crystallisation of 
the early dykes and emplacement of the other mafic -ultramafic intrusions. A problem with this 
hypothesis is that the Turkey Creek Gabbro, which cross -cuts most other intrusions, particularly 
Savannah and Savannah North, and ha s primary magmatic textures. Zircons from this gabbro have 
metamorphic rims suggesting it also went through syn -magmatic metamorphism. This suggests that 
either magmatism was more protracted and did not affect the higher MgO rocks as much, or that 
the Turk ey Creek Gabbro zircons are inherited grains with reset, or recrystallized rims, as suggested 
by their embayed morphologies.  
Interestingly, no metamorphic zircon was dated corresponding to the 1835 -1800 Ma Halls Creek 
Orogeny, which is constrained by defo rmation and metamorphism of the entire Lamboo Province 
(Tyler et al., 2012 ). Granitic rocks constrain the Lamboo -wide nature of the event, with the Mabel 
Downs Tonalite at 1835 Ma and 1835 -1805 Ma Sally Downs Supersuite (crustally -derived adakites 
and syn -collisional potassic granites) constraining two deformation events, confirming final 
amalgamation of all zones into a shared geological history.  
 Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  141 11 TIMS Dating 
11.1 Savannah North 
Five zircon crystals were analyzed from sample NOG-11 (Figure 82, Table 6 ). Calculated weights are 
between 0.5 and 1.0 µg, with calculated U concentrations being highly variable, from ca 100-4000 
ppm. Th/U ratios are typical for magmatic zircon, around 0.5 . One analysis had a somewhat elevated 
laboratory blank, though this had minimal effect on the age calculation. 207Pb/206Pb ages, relatively 
insensitive to the effects of Pb-loss not entirely mitigated by the chemical-abrasion technique, are 
presented here. The agreement in 207Pb/206Pb ages between all five grains supports our 
interpretation of the analyses representing a magmatic crystallization age, and it falls within 
uncertainty of an unconstrained upper-intercept age calculation. The weighted mean 207Pb/206Pb 
age of the five single- crystal analyses is 1833.66 ± 0.66 Ma (2σ , MSWD = 0.90, N = 5). 
Figure 82: Concordia plots of zircon U –Pb CA –TIMS data for sample from the Savannah North intrusion.

Six zircon  crystals were analyzed from sample NOG -11 (Figure 83, Table 6). Calculated weights are 
between 0. 2 and 2.0  µg, with calc ulated U concentrations ranging from ca 200 -9000 ppm. Th/U 
ratios are, again, typical for magmatic zircon, around 0.75 . Laboratory blanks were consistently 
between 0.3 -0.5 pg of common Pb.  Again, some slight discordance was observed, so the 207Pb/206Pb 
ages are presented here. As with the NOG -11 sample, the agreement in 207Pb/206Pb ages between 
all six grains supports our interpretation of the analyses representing a magmatic crystallization age, 
and it also falls within uncertainty of an unconstrained upp er-intercept age calculation. The 
weighted mean 207Pb/206Pb age of the five single -crystal analyses is 1835 .64 ± 0.53 Ma (2σ , MSWD 
= 1.04, N = 6), older than NOG -11 (Figure 84).

Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  145 12 AMS and remanent magnetisation study  
12.1 Aim of the study  
The first phase of this project (geological study) made significant breakthroughs, but a number of 
significant scientific and exploration questions have also arisen from the 1st phase. Particularly 
regarding the relationship, both in time and spatially, between the Savannah and Savannah North 
intrusions.  
Both intrusi ons have been dated (See part IV section 10. Geochronology ) using SHRIMP U‐Pb 
geochronology on zircons. The results demonstrates that both the Savannah and the Savannah 
North intrusions crystallised at ca. 1845 Ma. This data is equivalent (within error) to the data 
collected by GA.  
The geochronological constraint s appear to be at odds with the deformation histories of the 
Savannah and Savannah North intrusions, i.e., they appear to have undergone different deformation 
histories despite their close similarity in age. This has important implications for the origin a nd 
expected geometry of the orebodies. It appears that one intrusion has been tectonically rotated 
while the other has not. The previous work noted that “This requires additional magnetic studies to 
test whether this [apparent] rotation hypothesis is corre ct, and more detailed high resolution 
geochronology using the TIMS technique to obtain better resolution (± 1 million years, as opposed 
to the current uncertainty of +/‐ 3 Ma)”.  
This chapter aims at providing a Palaeomagnetic study of the Savannah  and Sava nnah  North  
intrusions in order to better understand the original geometry of the system. However, since there 
are many potential causes of resetting of Palaeomagnetic signatures and furthermore a likelihood 
of variability in the coercivity of the palaeomag netic grains within the different intrusions, we have 
also utilised a technique called AMS (Anisotropy of Magnetic Susceptibility) in order to get additional 
insights into the deformation recorded within the various intrusions.  146   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potentia l in the East Kimberley

vannah North 
The samples from savannah north show a large spread in densities, from ~2.85 up to 3.3 g/cc. This 
is fairly typical of a layered intrusion, in which the more ultramafic units (e.g., pyroxenites, olivine 
rich lithologi es) tend to sit toward the base, and the more fractionated feldspar‐rich units (e.g., 
anorthosites) tend to occur toward the (Figure 92). However, there is also a cyclicity within 
fractionated layered intrusions, in which layers alternate between Fe‐rich (e.g., pyroxene‐rich) and 
sodium/aluminium‐rich phases due to the fractional crystallisation process. This results in 
alternating moderately high and moderately low densities as illustrated by Figure 93. 
Figure 92: Density values measured from sampled of the Savannah North intrusion, displayed as a histogram with bin 
sizes of 0.025g/cc. 
The samples from Savannah North show a narrow range of values for magnetic susceptibility, NRM 
and Koensigsberger ratio (Figure 91), compared to the other intrusions sampled. This is probably 
the level of exposure of the intrusion. Magnetite will tend to become concentrated in a layered with 
successive fractional crystallisation in a layered intrusion and is commonly precipitated in 
magnetite‐rich anorthosite/ leucogabbronorite layers toward to the top of the intrusion. Th e section 
sampled here appears to be toward the bottom of the intrusion, where only minimal magnetite 
present. Toward the base of the intrusions magnetite forms as inclusions of iron trapped within the 
crystal lattice of pyroxene and or feldspar. The lack of high Koenigsberger ratios indicates that there 
is no pyrrhotite present and this likely indicates that the sampled area was precipitated pre‐ sulfur 
saturation. 
152   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Figure 93: Plot of density downhole from SMD155, illustrating both the trend toward denser rocks at depth but also 
the cyclicity of density due to fractional crystallisation. 
12.2.2  Savannah 
The densities of the Savannah intrusion samples have a bi‐modal distribution (as illustrated in Figure 
93) which is comparable with the results obtained at both Savannah North and Wilsons. This is fairly
typical of alternating pyroxenite/gabronorite layering commonly observed toward the base of a
layered intrusion (e.g. Austin, 2014).
Some Savannah samples have moderate magnetic susceptibilities indicating some magnetite is 
present. Whilst the elevated susceptibilities are associated with significant NRMs in some cases, 
they are not associated with high Koenigsberger ratios. The low Koenigsberger ratios are a little 
misleading as will be explained in the palaeomagnetic results later. But regardless, it is reasonable 
to infer that pyrrhotite is not present, and magnetite is the main magnetic phase.

12.2.3 Dave Hill 
The Dave Hill samples typically have high density, ranging from 3.0 – 3.3 g/cc, and this seems to be 
related to the lithologies sampled, many of which tend toward more ultramafic compositions, e.g., 
pyroxenite, olivine norite, olivine gabbronorite. In the other 3 intrusions sampled there is a well
pronounced  bi‐modal  distribution  of density,  which  is typical  of the lower  portions  of a layered 
intrusion.  At Dave  Hill however,  the lower density,  relatively  plagioclase‐rich  lithologies  do not 
appear to be present, perhaps suggesting that Dave Hill formed as a sill of relatively homogeneous 
composition, rather than a layered intrusion. 
Figure 95: Densi ty value s measured from sampled o f the Dave Hill intrusion, displayed as a histogram with bin sizes 
of 0.25 g/cc. 
The samples from Dave Hill show a similar range of magnetic susceptibility to both Wilsons and 
Savannah, ranging from low to moderate (e.g., 0.001 to ~0.05 SI) but are generally <0.015
SI. Remanence is significant in most of the samples, and compared with the other intrusions 
sampled Dave Hill is by far the most strongly remanently magnetised. Koenigsberger ratios are 
commonly in the range of 10‐20, which may be indicative of single domain magnetite or 
monoclinic pyrrhotite, but more likely the former. Two samples have more extreme 
Koenigsberger Ratios of 25‐50 (SAV007) and 150‐175 (SAV001). In the former case this 
appears to be associated with a strong fabric within the rocks which may have caused extreme 
grain shape in single domain magnetite. The more moderate Koenigsberger ratios could also be 
associated with exsolution lamellae of magnetite and ilmenite. In the latter case, the remanence is 
also associated with a moderate fabric, but sulfides are also visible in this sample, and so the 
extreme remanence is most likely caused by monoclinic pyrrhotite. The fact that pyrrhotite is 
clearly present toward the top of the sampled sequence may suggest the sulfur saturation point 
occurs toward the top of the sill. 
12.2.4 Wilsons 
The densities of the Wilsons intrusion samples have a bi-modal distribution (as illustrated in Figure 
96)which is comparable with the results obtained at both Savannah and Savannah North. This
isfairly typical of alternating pyroxenite/gabbronorite layering commonly observed toward the
baseof a layered intrusion.
In general, Wilsons is the least magnetic of the intrusions sampled.  However, a group of samples 
with high density (i.e., ~ 3.3 g/cc) have slightly elevated magnetic susceptibilities indicating 
some magnetite is present. Whilst the slightly elevated susceptibilities are associated with 
slightly elevated NRMs, they are not associated with high Koenigsberger ratios. Hence, it is 
reasonable to infer that pyrrhotite is not present, and magnetite is the main magnetic phase. 
A sample with relatively low density (SAV022) has the highest susceptibility (e.g., ~0.05 SI),  but 
154   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  155 this sample is actually a host rock (garnet psammite) not part of the intrusion.
Figure 96: Density values measured from sampled of the Wilsons intrusion, displayed as a histogram with bin sizes of 
0.025 g/cc. 
12.3 AMS (Anisotropy of Magnetic Susceptibility )
12.3.1 Background 
To date,  all 67 samples  have  had  their  A, B and  C specimen  measured  for AMS  (Anisotropy  of 
Magnetic Susceptibility) using the default settings on the Agico MFK1 kappabridge magnetometer. 
The results are used here to infer possible deformation of the intrusions. Background is discussed 
below. 
In general, for  an in situ  undeformed  layered  intrusion  we would  expect  the K3 (short  axis  of the 
AMS  ellipsoid)  to be vertical,  reflecting  the  vector  of shortening  during  crystallisation  i.e. the 
gravitational field of the Earth. The K1 (long axis) and K2 (intermediate) would be expected to form 
a girdle  normal  to K3, i.e.,  horizontal,  and  parallel  to the compositional  layering  of the intrusion. 
Assuming  there  was  no far-field  stress  applied  to the intrusion  as it  cooled,  K1 and  K2 would  be 
expected  to be poorly  clustered.  However,  if the intrusion  was  crystallised  in the presence  of an 
external  stress  field,  some  clustering  would  be expected.  However,  the K1 and  K2 would  still  be 
expected  to form  a horizontal  girdle.  Any  deformation  subsequent  to crystallisation  would  likely 
cause folding and or shearing of the AMS fabric. So deformation may be reflected as complete tiltin g 
of the layering, to form a tilted sill for example, in which the clustering of the results is consistent 
with  an in situ  layered  intrusion  but  without  the  sub-horizontal  layering.  Folding  may also  be 
manifested as fine-scale folding of the layering which would result in a girdle of K3 orientations. If 
there were more than one deformations event there could be two or more overprinting folds, and 
the more  overprinting  events  present,  the more  likely  the result  are to be uninterpretable.  AMS 
fabric  may also  be acquired  through  shearing  e.g.,  in the vicinity  of a shear  zone.   In this  case  of 
156   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley shearing it is probably that an y primar y layerin g related t o crystallisat ion would b e entirely 
overprinted. 
12.3.2 Savannah North 
Savannah  North displays  a fabric typical  of a sill or  layered intrusion,  with  a sub-vertical  mean  K3 
(short  axis,  shortening  direction)  with  K1 and  K2 sitting  within  a sub-horizontal  girdle  on the 
stereonet. In this case we use the K3 as a pole to the layering fabric (pink circles), which is related 
to the  shortening  direction  during  crystallisation  (i.e.,  gravity).  The  close  clustering  at Savannah 
North (Fig 1a) indicates that the original layering appears to be a mostly undeformed AMS fabric. 
However,  there  is some  local variability  of the  K3 observed,  and  some  evidence  to suggest  some 
minor N-S, NE-SW and ESE-WNW oriented deformation may have taken place, based on a number 
of sub-horizontal  K3 directions  present.  In assessing  the  results  from  the  two  holes  sampled 
individually, it appears that the N-S oriented K1 is prevalent in SMD155  (Figure 98 a), whereas an 
ESE K1 is more  prevalent  in SMD160  (Figure  98 b) examine  the  results  in more  detail  below  by 
further isolating different sub-sets of the data. 
There are a number of ways to further isolate different aspects of the AMS. Here we trialled 
several approache s, including: clipping results to only include lineation dominant fabrics; Clipping 
results to only include foliation dominant fabrics; Clipping low susceptibility results and low 
anisotropy results which tend to have more variable/ higher error AMS orientations, these all 
yielded similar results.  
However, results may also be clipped to reflect certain K1 or K3 orientations, and these cases have 
yielded better isolated clusters each representing a different variation in magnetic fabric, including 
those with:  
Su
b-vertical K3 (e.g.  Figure 98 c)
Steep SW-oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 98 d)
Moderate south-oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 98 e)
Sub-horizontal SE-oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 98 f).
These isolated clusters of data can be used to infer several structural interpretations.  The majority 
of results are consistent with a sub-horizontal magmatic fabric; however, examination of the 
cleaned data (i.e., restricted to the main cluster of sub-vertical K3s; Figure 98 c), indicates that the 
main magmatic fabric has been tilted slightly toward the north and/ or NE, post crystallisation. 
Furthermore, there is evidence in Figure 98 d and e to suggest that the main magmatic fabric has 
been rotated more substantially (i.e., folded) in some instances resulting in moderate NE- and north-
dipping fabrics being present. This implies that the main magmatic/magnetic fabric has been folded 
by both N-S and NE-SW directed shortening likely associated with relatively ductile conditions. There 
is another fabric present which appears to be sub-vertical NNE-oriented, and likely associated with 
WNW-directed shortening (Figure 98 f). In this case the fabric appears to be a shear fabric rather 
than simple folding tilting of the magmatic fabric, and it is consistent with reverse shearing within 
discreet zone of the intrusion probably associated with ductile-brittle conditions. Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  157 Figure 97: AMS results for Savannah North, showing: A. All results on a stereonet. The convention used for this and 
all subsequent AMS data is the blue squares=K1, green triangles=K2 and pink circle=K3; B. plot of L (lineation) vs F 
(foliation) which indicates that the fabric present is dominantly a foliation.

h 
The results for savannah are more difficult to interpret, and do not show a clear pattern, when 
viewed all together (Figure 99) or even when clipped to remove near isotropic results. This likely 
indicates that the intrusion has been significantly deformed, syn- to post- intrusion. 
Figure 99: Preliminary AMS results for Savannah, showing: A. Stereonet displaying K1, K2 and K3 for all samples, plus 
mean directions and confidence ellipses; B. plot of L (lineation) vs F (foliation) which indicates that the fabric present 
is dominantly a foliation. 
The results from the different drill holes sampled are quite different, as shown in Figure 100 a and 
b. The samples from drill hole KUD1503 appear to preserve a mostly sub-horizontal fabric (as
indicated by the sub-vertical K3), and in a limited number of cases also show evidence of some SE-
NW oriented shortening (as indicated by the sub horizontal K3s). Within this group of sub-vertical
K3 results there is a general N-S trend suggesting some modification of the magmatic fabric as will
be discussed further below. In general the K2 and K3 orientations are mixed, but the K1 orientations
are strongly clustered with a sub-horizontal NE-SW fabric, which is typical of a strong lineation. Such
a lineation could be the result of a stress field being present at the time of intrusion (syn-intrusion),
or possibly related to strike-slip shearing of the intrusion (forming an intersection lineation).
The results from KUD1526 do not show evidence for a sub-horizontal foliation being the dominant 
fabric, as would be expected for a sill or layered intrusion. The results broadly indicate that a sub-
vertical NW-SE oriented foliation, as indicated by the girdle of K1 orientations, is the dominant 
fabric. This could be interpreted as being caused by NE-SW directed shortening, associated with a 
post-crystallisation deformation event. However, such results are also consistent with a NW-SE-
oriented dyke. The results are further investigation below (Figure 100 c to f), using different data 
isolation techniques. The basis for clipping out different datasets for Savannah was based purely on 
the K3 orientations.

E. F.Sub-horizontal N WDrill Hole KUD1526 Drill Hole KUD1503Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  161 In the case of the savannah results data were clipped to better isolated clusters each representing 
a different variation in magnetic fabric, including those with: 
Sub-vertical K3 (e.g., Figure 100 c)
Sub-horizontal NW- SE-oriented K3 (e.g Figure 100 d)
Sub-horizontal N-S-oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 100 e)
Sub-horizontal NE- SW-oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 100 f)
The first clip (Figure 100 c) which isolates sub-vertical K3 results indicates a sub-horizontal fabric, 
which is likely to be a magmatic fabric formed via settling within the magma chamber. The results 
with such fabrics come mainly from drill hole KUD1503, suggesting that the fabric present in the 
part of the intrusion intersected by this drill hole has not been significantly overprinted by tectonic 
fabrics. Conversely, the results from drill hole KUD1526 do appear to be mostly overprinted, so we 
may infer that it has been significantly more deformed. A notable feature of the K3 data in Figure 
100c is that they sit along a N-S girdle, which indicates that the primary magmatic fabric has been 
folded, post-crystallisation. In this case the data indicate that upright E-W -oriented folding of the 
magmatic fabric took place and this style of deformation is consistent with N-S shortening under 
ductile conditions. 
The other fabrics interpreted appear to be ductile-brittle fabrics, or in other words tectonic fabrics 
that have overprinted the magmatic fabric. These fabrics are all upright (sub-vertical) have a range 
of orientations from NW to NE-striking. Here we have clipped the data to three clusters indicating 
NW-SE, N-S and NE-SW directed shortening (pure strain). However, it is highly likely that the fabrics 
formed with some component of shearing. It is likely that strain was partitioned into the different 
fabrics. For example in Figure 100 e there is a dominant K1 orientation indicating a dominant throw 
within the inferred shear zone and hence dextral transpression is implied. However, in Figure 100f , 
the spread of both K1 and K2 within the NW-SE girdle indicate more mixed kinematics, i.e., more 
evenly distributed strain. The more evenly distributed K1 and K2 vectors imply that the shortening 
direction was approximately normal to the fabric, i.e., that the shortening direction was NE-SW for 
the case shown in Figure 100f. Hence, it would appear that NW-SE and NE-SW shortening was likely 
associated with approximately normal tectonic fabrics, whereas, the E-W fabric shown in Figure 
100e is more likely associated with transpression related to NE-SW shortening. 
12.3.4  Dave Hill 
The results for Dave Hill (Figure 101) are well clustered, and provide a clear indication of the fabric 
present. The data are moderately well clustered, but overall do provide a clear indication of the 
mean fabric. The mean K3 (pink) is oriented moderate toward the NW, and the K1 and K2 form a 
girdle indicating a moderate SW -dipping fabric. This could be interpreted as an original magmatic 
settling fabric that has been tilted, or a tectonic fabric. The mean K1 is sub-horizontal NW oriented 
and the K2 is moderately to shallowly south-plunging, which is consistent with NE-SW shortening 
and broad-scale folding. Hence, the results are consistent with an original settling or sill fabric that 
has been tilted toward the SW by approximately 40°. However, this is not consistent with the 
magnetic modelling conducted as part of this study which indicates that Dave Hill is comprised of 
two sub-bodies separated by a SW-dipping shear zone. This interpretation suggests that the AMS 
fabric at Dave Hill could be related to deformation. Nevertheless, there are two fairly distinct fabrics 162   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley present in some samples, and the general fabric does display some variability in orientation, which 
we discuss below. 
Figure 101: AMS results for Dave Hill, showing: A. Stereonet displaying K1, K2 and K3 for all samples, plus mean 
directions and confidence ellipses; B. plot of L (lineation) vs F (foliation) which indicates that the fabric present is 
dominantly a foliation. 
There are a number of ways to further isolate different aspects of the AMS data as discussed 
previously. Here we trialled several approached, including: Clipping results to only include lineation 
dominant fabrics; Clipping results to only include foliation dominant fabrics; Clipping low 
susceptibility results and low anisotropy results which tend to have more variable/ higher error AMS 
orientations. In the case of the Dave Hill data, none of these approaches yielded better clustered 
data. 
However, results may also be clipped to reflect certain K1 or K3 orientations, and in this case we 
have isolated several distinct clusters each representing a different variation in magnetic fabric, 
including those with: 
Moderate NW-oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 102 a)
Shallow west oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 102 b)
Moderate NE-oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 102c) which were further sub-divided into:
oSub-vertical/ steep NE-oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 102d)
oShallow NNE-oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 102 e)
oShallow ENE-oriented K3 (e.g., Figure 102 f)
Based on these sub-divisions it seems reasonably clear that the data broadly reflect a structural 
fabric associated with either a sill or shear zone, dipping moderately to the SW. However, the 
variability of the data suggest that the fabric may locally vary from sub-horizontal (e.g., Figure 102d) 
to moderately SW-dipping fabrics (e.g., Figure 102d) which probably reflects variability due to the 
broad -scale NE-SW shortening as discussed previously. Furthermore, within the moderately SE-
dipping fabric there is evidence of 3 distinct magnetic fabrics: SE-dipping (e.g., Figure 102a); SSW-

12.3.5 Wilsons 
The results for Wilsons (Figure 104 ) also reasonably well clustered. However, in this case the K3 (the 
pole to the fabric) sits within a sub-vertical E-W girdle, implying that the original sill fabric has been 
modified by E-W shortening to form N-S oriented folds. The K1s are well clustered, and it is possible 
that K1 traces the plunge of the folding. There is some evidence in the data to suggest an additional 
generation  of folds  with  shallow  SW-plunges.  To examine  these  possibilities  the  data  is further 
subdivided and assessed below. 
166   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Figure 104: All AMS results for Wilsons, showing: A. Stereonet displaying K1, K2 and K3 for all samples, plus mean 
directions and confidence ellipses; B. plot of L (lineation) vs F (foliation) which indicates that the fabric present are 
dominated by foliations rather than lineations. 
There are a number of ways to further isolate different aspects of the AMS data as discussed 
previously. Here we use several approaches, including: 
Clipping results to only sub-vertical K3, these would be typical of typical crystallisations
fabrics in a layered intrusion (e.g., Figure 105a) .
Clipping results to only include lineation dominant fabrics. Such fabrics would be more likely
to reflect crystallisation within an external strain field (e.g., Figure 105b).
Clipping low susceptibility results which tend to have more variable/ higher error AMS
orientations (e.g., Figure 105c).
Clipping results to only include foliation dominant fabrics. Such fabrics are likely to reflect
both crystallisation fabrics and post-crystallisation deformations. However, these can be
discriminated, whereby clustering of K3 indicates a magmatic fabric, whereas clustering of
K1 indicated a tectonic fabric (e.g., Figure 105d).
Results may also be clipped to reflect certain K1 orientations, and in this case we have
isolated the two main K1 orientations present, those that are oriented NE-SW (e.g., Figure
105e). N-S K1 fabrics were also isolated (e.g.,Figure 105f).
