E. F.168   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley From these data we can make several observations about the tectonic history of the Wilsons 
intrusion. The sub-vertical mean K3 (Figure 105 a, e) does imply that there is a primary magmatic 
fabric present. However, within the groups of results that have a dominant sub-vertical mean K3 
(e.g., Figure 105 a, b, e) there is an obvious clustering of the K1 data in sub-horizontal SW 
orientations. In this case the strong clustering of K1 suggests that in addition to the main sub-vertical 
shortening imposed by gravity, and additional, probably weak NW-SE oriented stress field, or at 
least NE-SW oriented tension, was present during cooling of the intrusion. The combination of these 
two stress fields would cause a sub-horizontal NE-SW oriented lineation. This interpretation is 
consistent with the general structural fabric of the area in which the Wilsons intrusion occurs. 
The other main observation is that the overall K1 is strongly clustered with sub-vertical N- S 
orientations, associated with a spread of K2 and K3 orientations in an E-W girdle (e.g Figure 104 , 
Figure 105c, d and f). This interpretation is most applicable to data that have high relative magnetic 
susceptibility (K) and high relative anisotropy (P; e.g., Figure 105c) and with foliation dominant 
fabrics (Figure 105d). If the data are clipped so as to only include samples with N-S sub-vertical K1, 
we find that the dominant K3 associated with this dataset is sub-horizontal E-W. This style of AMS 
fabric (the K3 and K1 orientations in particular) is consistent with E-W shortening, whereby the 
spread of K2 and K3 orientation implies folding of the magmatic fabric, and the K1 traces the fold 
axis. 
So the most probably interpretation is that the Wilsons intrusion acquired a magmatic fabric in a 
NW-SE oriented stress field. The magmatic fabric which was tectonically overprinted by E-W 
shortening causing a sub-horizontal, N-S folds to be superimposed on the original fabric. 
12.3.6  AMS Summary 
Table 7 below provides a summary of the interred fabrics, and an interpretation of the relative 
timing. The inferred superposition of events based on whether the interpreted AMS fabrics appear 
to be, magmatic (i.e., formed due to gravitation forces during cooling of the intrusion), ductile (i.e., 
folding of the magmatic fabric), or ductile-brittle (i.e., tectonic fabrics which formed as discretised 
zones of shearing within the intrusion). Whilst this interpretation is consistent with the data, there 
are uncertainties in relation to the timing of various events, and the shortening directions inferred. 
In particular, the shortening direction inferred for the shearing events are uncertain, because it is 
difficult to ascertain the relative contributions of pure vs shear strain.  
There is a fundamental difference between the style of deformation present at Savannah and 
Savannah North versus Dave Hill and Wilsons. Savannah and Savannah North both appear to record 
an early N-S oriented folding event, followed by inferred NE and NW oriented shortening events 
which formed tectonic fabrics. We can be fairly certain of the shortening directions inferred from 
the ductile folding events. A variety of tectonic fabrics are present, and whilst they are broadly NE 
or NW oriented, some are also north oriented. If these fabrics are interpreted as being mainly 
caused by pure strain, then the inferred shortening directions are valid. However, it is also feasible 
to evoke a strain partitioning model in which either N-S or E-W shortening formed both NW and NE-
striking foliations. The clustering of the data at Savannah North is consistent with pure strain in 
different orientations. Conversely, the wide distribution of K1 and K2 within a girdle at Savannah is 
consistent with portioning of strain within the foliation. So in both cases the data suggest that the 
shortening directions are probably valid. Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  169 Dave Hill and Wilsons appear to have a much simpler history. Dave Hill appears to have been folded 
by a NE-SW event whereas the evidence at hand suggests that Wilsons may have cooled during the 
NW-SE event. They  both  record  an E-W  folding  event, and  at Dave  Hill  this  is manifested  as 
superposed  folding,  whereas  at Wilson  it is manifested  as broad-scale  folding  of the  magmatic 
fabric. 
So it appears that Savannah and Savannah North intruded synchronously, which is consistent with 
their close spatial relationship. Both affected by the same deformation events. However, based on 
the inference that Savannah North exhibits a ductile style of fabric for the NE-SW shortening event, 
we interpret that it remained hotter for longer than Savannah. This interpretation is consistent given 
the Savannah North intrusion is significantly larger than Savannah. Both Savannah and Savannah 
North preserve evidence of a N-S folding event which is not observed for Dave Hill. The 
relative petrophysical homogeneity of Dave Hill suggests it is a sill rather than a layered 
intrusion sensu stricto . The evidence suggests it intruded after the N-S shortening event, or was 
not affected by it. The main fabric at Dave Hill indicates that the first deformation event was NE-
SW oriented, causing the magmatic fabric to be tilted to the SE. Wilsons does not seem to have 
been affected by this deformation, but may have intruded during the NW-SE shortening event. 
Wilson really only records gentle E-W folding and hence we interpret that it intruded last. The E-
W deformation event only affects Dave Hill and Wilsons, and hence we interpret that this tectonic 
event post-dates cooling of the Wilsons intrusion. 
One of the  main  aims of  this  research  was to deduce  whether  the  intrusions  were  affected  by 
significant deformation and whether they had a shared or different deformation history. The AMS 
results show clearly that each intrusion has been affected by deformation, and furthermore the 
results suggest that the style of deformation is different for each intrusion. Savannah North 
appears to be the least deformed, and this is probably due to its relatively large stock-like 
architecture, which would be kinematically resistant to overprinting deformation. Conversely, 
Savannah appears to have undergone complex deformation. The elongate shape of the 
intrusion suggests that a fault zone may have acted as a conduit to the intruding magma, and 
the juxtaposition of the intrusion relative to the fault may have causes significantly more intense 
deformation within the intrusion. Both Dave Hill and Wilson exhibit fairly simple deformation, 
which is possibly related to their position in a completely different rock package, with different 
kinematic properties. 170   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Table 7 : Summary of deformation events based on AMS interpretations. 
12.4 Remanent Magnetisation 
12.4.1  Savannah North 
Savannah North typically has weak remanent magnetisation, and the palaeomagnetic results are 
very mixed and do not give much of an indication of a dominant remanent direction. There are 
numerous samples which contain relatively stable remanence, which can persist up to high 
alternating fields. In many cases there is some evidence to suggest that dual polarity remanence is 
present, which indicates that the magnetisations are genuine. However, these samples were 
typically not demagnetised sufficiently for us to be certain of the directions. Furthermore, there 
does not seem to be much of a correlation between the magnetisations measured from sample to 
sample. Principal component analyses were completed on all sample except where no magnetite 
was present (e.g., SAV032, 039). The results were sorted into magnetisations with low, moderate 
and high grain coercivities, and the results were plotted on stereonets (Figure 106, Figure 107, 
Figure 108).  
The low coercivity (i.e., magnetically soft) components were mostly upward oriented, and typically 
steeply inclined (Figure 106), and in general, there is a close relationship between the magnetisation 
directions and the upward projected drill orientation. This indicates that the soft components are 
dominated by a drilling-induced magnetisation.  
The moderate coercivity magnetisations (Figure 107a) do not cluster at all and hence we will not 
provide any speculation on their meaning. The high coercivity (i.e., hard) magnetisations (Figure 
107b) do not cluster particularly well either.

As indicated previously, there are results in which dual polarity are noted, and the magnetisation

showing dual polarities and those that do not. We found that removing the results with dual polarity 
reduced the spread of results substantially, providing more confidence in the results. With the dual 
polarity results removed, a mean direction oriented steeply down to the SW can be obtained from 
the data (Figure 109a), albeit with very h igh α95 of 36.8° (low confidence). Overall we really cannot 
make any useful conclusions other than there appears to be a steep downward magnetisation

The results with dual polarities are typically sub-horizontal and ENE- WSW oriented. It is not at all 
clear what the cause of the magnetisation might be, but such magnetisations are consistent with 
relatively late (i.e., Cambrian to Ordovician overprints). Given that these magnetisations are 
commonly oriented 90° to the drill hole orientation, it is possible that the magnetisations are caused 
by pencil magnets (e.g., Figure 109b).  
12.4.2  Savannah 
We conducted a principal component analysis (PCA) on all the Savannah samples. The results were 
mixed with some samples displaying soft up to very stable remanence, often comprised of 2- 4 
individual components. Many samples showed only weak-moderate coercivity components, other 
samples essentially recorded palaeomagnetic noise, which indicates a lack of magnetic minerals, or 
very soft (low coercivity) magnetic grains.  
In most cases these highly stable, remanently magnetised, lithologies retained two high coercivity 
palaeomagnetic components which have opposite polarity. In most cases these orientations 
account for the majority of the palaeomagnetic signal, which is not common. Usually opposite 
polarity results are only present in the most stable magnetisations, which commonly account for 
only the last ~10% of total magnetisation. These samples are very different, because in a number of 
cases the NRM values are initially very low, for example sample SAV062A (Figure 110 ) has an initial 
NRM of ~25 mA/m, as the sample is progressively degaussed, the intensity increases up to 
~200mA/m (8x higher than the NRM). This demagnetisation behaviour is indicative of one 
component of approximately apposite (i.e., self-cancelling) palaeomagnetic components being 
removed whilst the other remains strong. 
In many cases the magnetisations are so stable that the highest remanent magnetisation intensity 
occurs on the last step. Hence the most stable component is almost totally unresolved, and 
furthermore the remanence is extremely stable. In such cases we have just assumed the directions 
based on the last two degaussing stages (i.e. 130 mT and 140 mT), but thermal demagnetisation will 
need to be used to clarify the directions properly.  
Many of the samples retain two strong magnetisations with opposite polarity, and approximately 
equal intensity, which effectively cancel each other out. As a consequence the Koenigsberger ratios 
of many of the Savannah samples are misleading in terms of providing a measure of the stability 
and strength of the remanence, because the Koenigsberger ratio is calculated using NRM (which is 
the sum of the remanent magnetisation vectors) not the total intensity (de-vectorised). When the 
Koenigsberger ratio is calculated for SAV062A based on the latter preposition, the total intensity of 
remanence would be ~375 mA/m (15x higher than the NRM), and the associated Koenigsberger 
ratio would be 4.2. These results are more consistent with a results from magnetically stable 
remanence preserving lithologies. Savannah is a rare case in which the lithologies locally have strong 
remanence and relatively weak susceptibility, but because the remanence is largely self-cancelling, 
the anomaly at Savannah is very subtle. 174   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Figure 110: Plot of remanent magnetisation intensity with increasing demagnetisation for sample SAV062A. 
The PCA results were subdivided into weak, moderate and high coercivity components and plotted 
and analysed separately. The low coercivity components (Figure 111) typically had upward 
inclination, but did not cluster at all, not even around the drill-hole orientation. The fact that there 
was not a dominant drilling induced component probably suggests that the soft components 
measured were acquired post-drilling, whilst sitting in core trays in the core yard. 
Figur e 111: Stereonet displaying all the low coercivity magnetic components extracted from principal component 
analyses. The upward projection of the drill hole orientations are plotted for reference. 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  175 Th
e moderate to high coercivity components, plotted in Figure 112a, all sit on a N-S oriented girdle. 
This is not common for palaeomagnetic data of approximately comparable coercivity from a single 
intrusion. The results are widely spread, from sub-vertical to sub-horizontal, which is not consistent 
with a folding model. However, because the data represent two polarities, the data were converted 
so as to represent one polarity (Figure 110b).The converted results provided a more tightly clustered 
dataset in which the results spread from sub-horizontal south, to moderately downward inclined 
north –plunging magnetisations. The mean direction of these magnetisations has declination: 176.5 ° 
and inclination: 59.1°, which is consistent with the data from Dave Hill, Wilsons, and to a lesser 
degree Savannah North.  
Figur e 112:  Stereonets  displaying:  A. all medium  to high  coercivity  magnetic  components  extracted  from  principal 
component  analyses.  B. All dual  polarity  pair  of magnetisations  have  been  converted  to a single  polarity.  This 
conversion reduces the spread of data and may also be used to provide a better estimate of the likely palaeomagnetic 
pole. The data have a pronounced north-south trend, possibly indicating modification of the primary palaeomagnetic 
vector by folding. 
There are three main clusters, as illustrated by the 1% contour provided in Figure 113 and the spread 
of the palaeomagnetic vectors along a N-S girdle is consistent with the spread of AMS data 
for Savannah. Hence, it is reasonable to infer that N-S-oriented shortening may have rotated both 
the palaeomagnetic  vectors and the AMS fabrics. The clustering of the data interpreted as a 
folding model implies that the main palaeomagnetic vector was rotated by 40-50° both north 
and south resulting in three clusters of palaeomagnetic results; the original mod-steep south 
magnetisations, a shallow-sub-horizontal south magnetisation and a steep to moderate north 
magnetisation. 
There is some evidence to suggest that the palaeomagnetic vectors could also have been rotated 
to the NW and SE which is not inconsistent with the deformation interpreted from the AMS, but it 
is less well substantiated by the palaeomagnetic  data. Alternatively, it could be that there are two 
main paleaomagnetic clusters, as is observed at Dave Hill. There is a moderate south plunging 
cluster,  which is undeformed at Dave Hill but is interpreted to have been folded at Savannah to 
form a N-S  girdle of magnetisation vectors. The second, a moderately SSE-plunging cluster 
has not been 
12
3
12
3
A 
 B modified at either. If this is the case then the SSE-plunging magnetisation must post-date the N- S 
folding. Furthermore, at Savannah, the magnetisations that plot in the SSE-cluster are of moderate 
coercivity, and are typically removed in alternating fields of ~40 mT. This is consistent with the SSE-
magnetisation being a secondary metamorphic overprint, rather than a magnetisation formed 
during cooling/exsolution.  
An implication of these observations, is that Dave Hill may have intruded at approximately the same 
time as Savannah, but it was not deformed during the earlier N-S shortening event. This 
interpretation is consistent with the AMS results, and suggests that the geological history of the 
Savannah/Savannah North area may have been very different to that of the Dave Hill/Wilson’s area. 
There are a number of scenarios that may explain why, but the most obvious explanations could 
include: very different rheology of the host rocks; different kinematics, e.g., the placement of the 
intrusions in relation to major faults and shear zones, the temporal relationships between stress 
fields and emplacement. The major difference between the two sub-areas is the host lithology. 
Savannah and Savannah North are hosted by migmatitic pelites which will be incompetent during 
deformation, and likely instigate rotation of competent bodies hosted within. Conversely, the mafic 
granulites which host the Dave Hill and Wilsons intrusions would be rheologically more similar to 
the intrusions they host, and hence there would be far less differential stress during deformation, 
and hence less or no rotation.

along the N-S  trend. These could be interpreted as due to tilting of the primary palaeomagnetic vector (moderate 
south plunging) both north and south by 40-50°.

Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  177 12.4.3  Dave Hill 
Results 
The results from Dave Hill fall into two broad categories. The majority of samples have fairly strong 
remanent magnetisation that is persistent up to very strong demagnetising (degaussing) fields. In 
many cases these samples did not begin to demagnetise until 20-30 mT step, and furthermore, in 
many cases the samples were not fully demagnetised in a field of 140 mT (the maximum field 
achievable using the 2G magnetometer). This indicates that the magnetic grains are of high 
coercivity (i.e., they are resistant to overprinting). These samples all have a major component of 
magnetisation that is oriented approximately in a southerly declination, with steep to moderate 
downward inclinations. The results of the principal component analysis (PCA), summarised in Figure 
114a provides a mean remanent magnetisation direction of Dec: 182.5° and Inc: 61.3°. In calculating 
a mean magnetisation orientation for Dave Hill, the drilling induced magnetisations were excluded, 
but the results were not weighted more heavily toward the stronger magnetisations which should 
technically provide a more accurate result. 
As shown by the 1% contour applied to the data (Figure 114b) there are two sub-parallel clusters of 
data. However, both of these are recorded in at least one sample and it is therefore likely that these 
represent two distinct, but temporally close periods of acquisition of remanence, rather than 
modification of the remanence direction by subsequent folding. This interpretation is consistent 
with the AMS results, since the plunge of the folding interpreted is sub-parallel to the remanence, 
and as such would not significantly modify its declination or inclination during folding. 
Figure 114: Summary of Palaeomagnetic results from Dave Hill, showing: A. remanent magnetisation directions for 
the majority of samples with a calculated mean; B. remanent magnetisation directions for the majority of samples 
with a 1% contour underlain. 
These results are consistent with the pronounced negative magnetic anomaly at Dave Hill. There 
are several different overprints observed, but these for the most part only constitute a very minor 
A 
 B 178   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley component of the total magnetisation, and hence for the purposes of magnetic modelling are 
insignificant.  
SAV009, 010 and 014 all behaved differently during the AF demagnetisation experiments as 
illustrated in Fig 42. In each case, the majority of the magnetisation >80% was removed in the first 
few demagnetisation steps. This is clear evidence that the bulk of magnetic grains present are low 
coercivity (i.e., easily overprinted). In the case of SAV009 and SAV014, there is only a drilling induced 
component present, and the remainder of the measurements are simply instrument induced noise. 
In the case of SAV010 there is a strong drilling induced component which accounts for ~80% of the 
total magnetisation. However there are two other magnetisation present, oriented moderately 
down to the SSE and moderately up toward the NNW. These likely represent polar opposites due to 
a polarity switch of the magnetic field, and are consistent with magnetisation within the main group 
of results. 
Figure 115: Samples SAV009 (A), SAV010 (B) and SAV014 (C) all display a strong drilling induced magnetisation, as 
shown by the parallelism between the upward drilling orientation (blue triangles) and the main magnetisation 
deduced (orange squares) 
Magnetic Modelling 
Based on the AMS and palaeomagnetic results for Dave Hill, a simple modelling exercise was 
undertaken, to confirm the results. A model was generated in ModelvisionProTM, whereby the 
orientation of the body was constrained using the implied magmatic fabric from the AMS 
interpretation. The petrophysical properties were constrained using the mean measured 
susceptibility, and estimations of the NRM, based on the most highly magnetised samples. Using 
this approach it was possible to match the anomaly to ~6% r.m.s. (Fig 113). However, several aspects 
of the morphology of the modelled anomaly were not adequately similar to the actual magnetic 
anomaly, suggesting either the body shape or magnetisation used were not appropriate. 
To match the low amplitude high on the southern side of the main negative anomaly, it is necessary 
for the body to be sub-vertical to north plunging. Furthermore, the main anomaly is comprised of 
two adjacent overlapping anomalies rather than a single anomaly. This suggests that either two 
bodies, or a body that is cut by a WNW-NW trending fault, are appropriate. The latter explanation 
is consistent with the final model produced and also is consistent with the AMS results. However 
we would need to adopt a shear related model for the generation of the AMS fabric, rather than a 
primary magmatic fabric. Significant shearing of the body could easily cause the truncation 
observed, and furthermore recrystallisation of the magnetite during shearing may have led to 
acquisition of a new palaeomagnetic signature. 
A 
 B 
 c Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  179 Figure 116: An Initial magnetic model for Dave Hill, shown with TMI grid. The model was constrained by the AMS 
fabric for the body dip, the measured remanence and magnetic susceptibility was assumed to be negligible. 
A second model was generated using the analytic signal as the basis for the architecture of the 
starting model (Figure 117, Figure 118). The body is comprised of one body, which thins substantially 
in the middle along an approximately NW-tending zone of weak magnetisation. Such a model i s 
consistent with a fault or shear zone running through the high remanence zone as inferred from the 
AMS data.  
Figure 117: A second, more detailed magnetic model for Dave Hill, shown with the analytic signal grid. The constraints 
applied are the same as for the initial model. 
180   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Figure 118: 3D perspective view of the second 3D magnetic model illustrating the spatial relationship between the 
magnetic model and the shear zone (inferred from AMS). The two nodes of the magnetic anomaly correspond to the

is 1:1; scale varies in this perspective view. 
The model in which the truncation occurs between the two highly remanent zones is a better matc h 
to the anomaly. However, it is important to note that the line data from which these bodies were 
modelled was flown east-west, which is parallel, not perpendicular to the anomaly, and furthermore 
the E-W orientation is not optimal for modelling because t he Earth’s magnetisation is oriented N -S. 
Furthermore, the line spacing is 400 m so although we can create synthetic N-S lines and re-sample 
the field to those lines for the purpose of modelling, the resolution and precision of the N- S 
components of the magnetic field are poorly constrained.  Hence, although the petrophysical 
properties of the Dave Hill intrusion are consistent and well constrained, the magnetic data from 
which the models are derived substantially under-sample the true magnetic field, and therefore 
both models are imprecise. 
12.4.4  Wilsons 
The Wilsons samples can also be categorised in three main groups. Many of the samples appear to 
contain very soft magnetisations, which are consistent with fairly coarse (i.e., multi-domain) 
magnetite. In the other cases, the remanence is comprised of several components, including a soft 
component and one or more other relatively stable components. Several samples, generally toward 
the top of the holes (SAV015, 017, 018, 019, 020) display genuine remanent magnetisation. In some 
cases the magnetisation is dominated by a relatively stable (i.e., high coercivity) remanence, that is 
not fully demagnetised in a field of 140 mT (e.g., SAV015, 017, 018).  
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  181 There is very little correlation between the directions obtained in general, and many of the 
directions obtained are merely instrument induced noise, or related to the in situ or drilling induced 
magnetisations. The main exception are a group of steep to moderate downward inclined, southerly 
oriented magnetisation, which are present in the high coercivity samples. The principal component 
analyses of the AF demag data from these samples is plotted in Figure 119. The mean direction 
obtained is sub-parallel to the results from Dave Hill and hence we interpret that the acquisition of 
the remanent magnetisation occurred at approximately the same time.  
The Wilsons magnetisations are however, much weaker, and more inconsistent than those 
measured from the Dave Hill intrusion. So whilst remanence is present it does not dominate th e 
magnetic signal of the intrusion, firstly because it is weak, and secondly because much of the 
magnetisation is also induced and or soft remanence. The induced magnetisation and soft 
remanence will be oriented in the opposite direction to the hard remanence, and hence the two 
components likely cancel each other out to some degree. Nevertheless, the sub-parallelism of the 
dominant magnetisations at Wilsons and Dave Hill imply that they have a shared history.  
Figure 119: Summary of Palaeomagnetic results from Wilsons, showing: A. remanent magnetisation directions for the 
majority of samples with a calculated mean; B. remanent magnetisation directions for the majority of samples with 
a 1% contour underlain. 
12.4.5  Palaeomagnetism Summary 
It is clear that the primary (high coercivity) palaeomagnetic components at both Savannah and 
Savannah North have been modified by deformation. In the case of Savannah it is difficult to resolve 
the likely primary and secondary components. However in the case of Savannah, it is apparent that 
the primary component has been tilted north and south by ~40-50° during a N-S folding event that 
was also inferred from the AMS results. The secondary component appears to be unmodified, and 
is consistent with the palaeomagnetic results from Dave Hill. The results from Wilsons are of low 
quality compared with Savannah and Dave Hill, but the mean palaeomagnetic vector deduced is 
consistent with those from Savannah and Dave Hill. Hence it is reasonable to take the PCA data from

The resultant apparent pole was plotted against a modified APWP of Idnurm and Giddings (1988) 
with revised data from Schmidt (2014) in Figure 120. Note that the Savannah Combined pole plots

Figure 120: Plot of all medium to high coercivity components for Savannah, Dave Hill and Wilsons. The Kamb contour 
of the data (underlain), and the mean vector (overlain) are consistent with each other, and hence suggest that the 
data identify a significant palaeomagnetic component. 
It is important to note that while there are sufficient data for parts of the APWP, there are major 
gaps particularly from 1880 Ma to 1650 Ma, and from ~1500 Ma to 1200 Ma. Furthermore, although 
the 1200 Ma and 1650-1500 Ma poles are plotted in opposite parts of the globe, they are in fact 
very close but with different polarity. So there is some uncertainty as to whether there was 
significant plate movement relative to the magnetic pole during these intervals, or whether the 
north Australian plate remained approximately stationary relative to the pole from ~1850- 1200 Ma. 
Given that any earlier magnetisations would have been deformed by later orogenic events, it seems 
most plausible that the bulk of the magnetisations formed at ~1200 Ma during the Yampi orogeny. 
This corresponds to the cluster of un-rotated magnetisations. However there is sufficient doubt to 
warrant more detailed investigation particularly given that some samples (e.g., SAV060-066) contain 
pyrrhotite, which will be more easily reset due to its lower Curie point (~320°C). We have addressed 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  183 this to some extent by conducting thermal demagnetisation of a selection of 30 samples which 
showed stable and or complex magnetisation. However, this study is still in progress. 
The Savannah Apparent Pole also sits along the Palaeozoic APWP of Li et al. (1990), at approximately 
390 Ma (Figure 121). Whilst this seems highly implausible, previous workers do attribute 
deformation within the area to the Alice Springs Orogeny so it cannot be fully discounted.

184   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley 12.5 Discussion 
A review of prior work on the deformation and metamorphism in the Savannah area was undertaken 
and the results are summarised in Table 8. This allows us to place the results of the palaeomagnetic 
and AMS study into a regional tectonic context. In terms of the AMS results, it seems clear that NE 
and NW- striking shear fabrics observed at Savannah and Savannah North are related to high 
temperature shearing during the Halls Creek Orogeny and possibly also the Stafford Tectonic Event. 
The un-sheared (i.e., original magmatic) fabrics at Savannah appear to be tilted north and south, 
which we attribute to N-S shortening during the Stafford Tectonic Event. This is also consistent with 
the palaeomagnetic results, since we interpret that there was an early high coercivity magnetisation 
that was tilted north and south. Provided peak metamorphic conditions during the Halls Creek 
Orogeny did reach approximately ~565° the early magnetisation probably represents a high 
temperature metamorphic overprint that was subsequently rotated by folded in the Stafford 
Tectonic Event.

(1998)  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  185 Although Savannah North is less deformed in general than Savannah, it also retains what appears 
to be an early  magnetisation  that  is spread  across  a NE-SW  trending  girdle.  Again,  based  on 
information tabulated in Table 8,  this might also be consistent with folding  during the Stafford event, 
albeit  in a different  orientation  to what  is recorded  in the  AMS  and  palaeomagnetic  data  at 
Savannah. Furthermore, the AMS data for Savannah North, show evidence of both N-S and NE-SW 
tilting. This interpretation is consistent with that of Orth (2002) who noted that the more northern
areas of the central zone were more affected by the Halls Creek Orogen (which included the Stafford 
Tectonic Event). 
So it is fairly clear that the palaeomagnetic vectors and the magnetic fabrics display evidence of both 
N-S and  NE-SW  folding,  which  is consistent  with  both  being  affected  mainly  by the  Halls  Creek
Orogen, particularly the latter Stafford Tectonic Event. This interpretation is consistent with that of
Orth (2002) who noted that the more northerly areas of the central zone were more affected by the
Halls Creek Orogen (which includes the Stafford Tectonic Event).
The palaeomagnetic  vectors  and  AMS  fabrics  at both  Dave  Hill and  Wilsons  do not  seem  to be 
affected  by the  earlier  tectonism  or metamorphism.  They  both  preserve  relatively  unmodified 
palaeomagnetic  directions  that  are consistent  with  poles  aged  ~1200  Ma  on an apparent  polar 
wander path (APWP) for  Australia (Schmidt, 2014). Because they appear to be well clustered, it is 
reasonable to assume they are reasonably undeformed. This suggests that the magnetisation may 
have  been  acquired  during  the Yampi  Orogeny.  The  inferred  NE-SW  tilting  of the Dave  Hill AMS 
fabric is consistent with inferred shortening directions for the Stafford Tectonic Event, but also with 
the inferred shortening during the Yampi Orogeny. The approximately S-plunging superposed folds 
inferred  from  the  AMS  data  for Dave  Hill,  and  the  sub-horizontally  SSW-plunging  folding  of the 
magmatic  fabric  at Wilsons  is consistent  with  relatively  late  folding,  possibly  during  Alice  Springs 
Oroge
ny. The palaeomagnetic directions obtained are also consistent with the 410-390 Ma section 
of the apparent polar wander path of Li et al. (1990). In fact the mean palaeomagnetic vectors for 
Savannah, Dave Hill and Wilsons, could feasibly be related to ages of ~1650-1500 Ma, ~1200 Ma or 
410-410 ma, because they all plot in approximately the same position on an apparent polar wander
path.
12.6  Conclusions 
In general, the northern two intrusions were subjected to similar conditions, and the dual polarity 
palaeomagnetic signatures are consistent with a shared cooling history. Both N-S, NE-SW and NW-
SE shortening  was  recorded  in both  intrusions.  These  shortening  directions  suggest  that  the two 
northerly intrusions were mainly affected by the early tectonic events, the Halls Creek Orogen and 
the Stafford Tectonic Event (which is essentially the latter part of the Halls Creek event). Whilst the 
two intrusions  experienced  similar conditions,  the  inferred  syn-  to post-  cooling  rotation  of the 
bodies  (during  which  the palaeomagnetic  signature  was  acquired)  was  in a different  orientations 
(i.e.,  N-S  at Savannah;  NE-SW  at Savannah  North).  Both  are consistent  with  the Stafford  Tectonic 
Event, but the evidence presented in this study suggests that the N-S deformation was the earlier 
of the  two.  It is equally  likely  that  the differences  in inferred  shortening  /rotation  direction  were 
caused by strain partitioning. 
The southern two intrusions were also subjected to similar conditions, but those conditions appear 
to be very different  from  those  recorded  further  north.  The evidence  presented  in this  study 186   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley suggests that Dave Hill was affected mainly by the Yampi Orogeny, during which the magmatic fabric 
was tilted to the SW. The well clustered single polarity remanent magnetisation at Dave Hill is 
consistent with an age of ~1200 Ma, which is concurrent with the latter stages of the Yampi 
Orogeny. E-W shortening appears to be preserved at both Dave Hill and Wilson. At Dave Hill the E-
W shortening causes superposed N-S folds to overprint the SW-dipping fabric. At Wilsons the E- W 
shortening causes folding of the previously sub-horizontal magmatic fabric. Based on our review of 
the previous structural studies in the area, the E-W shortening seems to be consistent with the Alice 
Springs Orogeny. The palaeomagnetic vectors are also consistent with Li et al. (1990) APWP at ca 
390 Ma. Whilst the latter explanation might seem implausible, it is important to note that prior work 
in central Australia (e.g. Austin, 2014 , 2015) suggests that there was substantial uplift of both the 
Musgrave and Arunta blocks during the Palaeozoic, which caused the bulk of the magnetisation 
recorded in those rocks. We certainly do not have a thorough enough understanding of the thermal 
and tectonic history of the eastern Kimberley at present to infer any more than: either two options 
(1200 Ma or ~390 Ma) are possible for the southern area. Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  187 Part V Conclusion Chapter 188   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley 1 Discussion on the parental magma composition 
Do the intrusion have the same parental magma or not?
Savannah, Savannah North and Dave Hill have distinctive and essentially identical parent magma 
compositions, shared with Turkey Creek and Subchamber D. Savannah North contains the most 
magnesian olivines and pyroxenes and hence records the most primitive magmas. In the Wilson’s 
intrusion, the noritic component is very similar to Savannah, Savannah North and Dave Hill, while 
the peridotitic rocks appear to be a distinct magma. The early mafic dykes are different from all the 
others. 
Do we see crustal contamination or not (prospectivity indicator)
It is very likely that the magmas have undergone some crustal contamination to account for the S 
isotope data and the Re-Os isotope data previously reported by Sproule et al. (1999), but in order 
to account for the lack of variability between the intrusions, and the lack of a contamination signal 
attributable to the immediately adjacent Tickalara paragneisses, it is likely that this contamination 
occurred at depth in a well homogenised magma chamber. Hence the parent magmas to the various 
intrusions were all affected to the same degree, rather than showing variable signatures due to 
contamination at the level of emplacement.  On this basis, both Dave Hill and Wilson’s can be 
regarded as prospective. The higher abundance of olivine- rich cumulates at Wilson’s relative to 
Dave Hill implies it should have higher priority on the information available so far, although the 
presence of disseminated magmatic sulfides within at least one Dave Hill sample. 
What is the magmatic signature of the magma (plume vs arc-subduction related
etc…)  very short discussion on the Tectonic setting – history, see extended
version in part 3 of this final part of the report.
The balance of evidence suggests that the Savannah intrusions were formed from subduction-
related island arc tholeiites with a possible component of back-arc magmatism, on the basis o f 
certain distinctive trace element geochemical characteristics. The pulse of magmatism responsible 
for the Savannah suite at ~1845 Ma may be related to a particular episode such as a slab detachment 
event. There is no compelling evidence for the involvement of a mantle plume. Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  189 2 Relationship between the mafic-ultramafic 
intrusions in the Savannah area

Savannah intrusion dated at 1844.3 ± 5 Ma  with the SHRIMP, and 1835.6  ± 0.5 Ma with
TIMS dating.
Savannah North intrusion dated at 1842.6 ± 5 Ma with the SHRIMP , and 1833.6 ± 0.6
Ma with TIMS dating.
Tickalara Metamorphics dated at 1869 ± 5 Ma (SHRIMP)
Wilson’s intrusion dated at 1847.5 ± 5 Ma (SHRIMP)
Dave Hill intrusion dated at 1839 ± 5 Ma  (SHRIMP)
Crossing-cutting mafic dyke at Dave Hill dated at 1829 ± 5 Ma (SHRIMP)
Sub-chamber D intrusion dated at 1843 ± 5 Ma (SHRIMP)
Copernicus intrusion dated at 1823 ± 5 Ma (SHRIMP)
Early mafic dyke samples dated at 1845 ± 5 Ma (SHRIMP)
Aplite dyke located within the 500 and 900 Faults dated at 1853 ±5 Ma (SHRIMP)
Turkey creek gabbro 1843 ±5 Ma  with metamorphic rimbs dated at 1846 ± 5 Ma
(SHRIMP)
The Tickalara metamorphics represent the oldest of the dated lithology (1870 Ma), with few grains 
showing evidences of metamorphism dated around 1846 Ma. Savannah, Savannah North, Sub-
Chamber D, the Early mafic dykes, Wilson’s, and Dave Hill, all seem to be intruding at the same age, 
within error (± 5 Ma): 1845 Ma. Finally, the Copernicus intrusion seems to have intruding later (20 
Ma later –  1825 Ma). 
2.2 Geological relationships 
These dates can be combined with geological observations (cross cutting relationships, and signs of 
deformation and metamorphism) in order to confirm and/or slightly modify our interpretation of 
the sequence of event that shaped the Savannah district: 
The early mafic dykes seem to have undergone higher metamorphic grades, with
evidence of granulite facies, with the rocks showing equigranular characteristics.
The Aplite and mafic dykes are located within cross cutting shear zones (500 and 900
faults) that cross-cut and displace both the Savannah and Savannah North intrusions.
The Savannah intrusion shows evidences of sub-vertical magmatic layering whereas the
Savannah North intrusion show evidences of sub-horizontal magmatic layering.2.3 Insights fr om AMS an d Remanent magnetism study 
The AMS and Remanent magnetism study conducted on some of the intrusions, such as Savannah, 
Savannah North, but also Wilson’s and Dave Hill , brought to light new evidence to use in order to 
understand the timing of events: 
 Savannah and Savannah North have both been affected by the same deformation
events, they therefor intruded synchronously.
 Even though Savannah and Savannah North have undergone the same deformation
events, they still show evidence of fundamentally different styles of deformation (pure
strain in various orientations for Savannah, as opposed to positioning strain within the
foliation for Savannah North). Savannah North being a larger body of magma might
have been more resistant to deformation than the thin elongated Savannah intrusion.
 Savannah North shows evidence of a ductile style of NE-SW shortening, implying that it
remained hotter 
longer than Savannah.
 Savannah’s e longated shape, combined with evidences of important deformation, may
suggest that a fault zone may have acted as a conduit for the intrusion of Savannah
 The difference in the orientation of shortening and rotation between Savannah and
Savannah North might be due to strain partitioning
 Dave Hill and Wilson’s both present a simpler deformation history:
o Wilson’s seems to record 2 of the deformation events observed at Savannah
and Savannah North: a NW-SE shortening during which it cooled, and an E-W
folding.
o Dave Hill only preserved evidence  of the E-W folding event.
Using results from the AMS and remanent magnetism studies, the order of intrusion between these 
four intrusions seems to be as follow: Savannah and Savannah North, followed by Wilson’s, and 
Dave Hill last. 
2.4 Geometrical and geochronological relationship between Savannah 
and Savannah North 
Combining  all the acquired  information  summarized  above  and  in part  IV of this  report,  we think 
that the Savannah and Savannah North intrusions are part of the same magmatic event. However, 
Savannah would have intruded slightly before Savannah North (0.9 to 3.1 Ma earlier) according to 
TIMS dates. From the results of the AMS and palaeomagnetic  study, we can infer that Savannah
has undergone more deformation than Savannah North. There are two reasons for this 
difference in deformation, 1) the difference of geometry of the two intrusions; Savannah North 
being a large solid bathtub like intrusion, it would have been more resistant to deformation than 
the elongated and narrow Savannah intrusion, and 2) the deformation event could have 
happened while or just after Savannah intruded the Tickalara Matamorphics, but Before the 
intrusion of Savannah North. These two intrusions might also have been intruding in a slighty 
different setting, with a different strain partitioning.  Figure 123 shows a model of a ‘flower’ 
structure. We think that the Savannah intrusion 
190   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  191 might have intruded within an active ‘flower’ type fault system which would have engendered the 
90° rotation of the intrusion that we now observe.  Savannah would have intruded along one of 
these strik e slip fault and therefore be affected by the rotation.

192   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  3 Tectonic setting of the East Kimberley Ni -Cu 
systems  
3.1 ca. 1910 -1870 Ma  – A Paleoproterozoic ocean basin  
The tectonic evolution of the Lamboo Province starts at ca. 1910 -1860 Ma. The ca. 1912 -1904 Ma 
Ding Dong Downs Volcanics and Sophie Downs Supersuite (basement of the Eastern Zone), that 
initiated a continental rift zone th at developed into an ocean basin between ca. 1910 and 1870 Ma 
(Ogasawara, 1988 ), although more recent work by Phillips et al. (2016b)  attribute this magmatism 
to a passive margin setting, possibly more likely to be magmatic in the Paleoproterozoic due to 
elevated mantle temperatures (Herzberg et al., 2010 ). 
Similar to Griffin et al. (2000) , we infer a micro -continent / ribbon continent (Central Zone) 
surrounded by oceanic crust with the Kimberley and North Australian continental blocks to the west 
and east (Figure 5a), respectively, with their respective marginal terranes (Western and Eastern 
Zones). At ca. 1910 -1860 Ma, passive margin volcanism in the Eastern Zone formed the Ding Dong 
Downs Volcanics and Sophie Downs Supersuite at ca. 1910 Ma, followed by e ruption of felsic 
volcanics at ca. 1880 Ma in the Brim Rockhole Formation.  
3.2 ca. 1870 -1850 Ma – Initiation of subduction and the Hooper Orogeny  
In the Western Zone, the <1872 Ma Marboo Formation was deposited into either a passive margin 
or continental arc setting. The Ruins Dolerite occurs at 1872 -1861, shortly followed by the ca. 1865 
Ma Paperbark Supersuite, suggesting west -dipping subduction initiated around 1870 Ma, forming 
the Whitewater Volcanics at ca. 1860 -1850 Ma. The contemporaneous nature and str ike length of 
this unit is suggestive of a continental volcanic arc. This subduction transported the exotic Central 
Zone towards the margin of the Eastern Zone. The 1870 -1860 Ma Hooper Orogeny marks the 
collision of the Western Zone with the micro -continen t of the Central Zone. Presumably subduction 
stepped back under the Central Zone, and slab -breakoff occurred where the original trench had 
been (Figure 5bi). However, magmatism in the Western Zone continued until ca. 1855 Ma 
(Whitewater Volcanics). This, c oupled with a lack of constrained felsic -intermediate volcanism in the 
Central Zone at this time, suggests that either this magmatism was driven by slab break -off, or that 
subduction continued at an angle that prolonged arc -related magmatism in the Western  Zone.  
From ca. 1855 -1845 Ma there is a progressive transition in magmatism from the Western Zone to 
the Central Zone (Figure 5bii). We suggest that the roll -back of the subduction zone led to arc 
extension and eventually back -arc basin formation. This tr ansported the slab east, turning off 
magmatism in the Western Zone at ca. 1855 Ma. This corresponds with the deposition of the 
Tickalara metasediments at ca. <1865 Ma, emplacement of MORB basalts and mafic volcaniclastics. 
An alternate hypothesis is that t he subduction stepped back at ca, 1865 Ma, forming a ‘Tickalara 
arc’ which formed the zircons deposited in the Tickalara metasediments, leaving slab break -off to 
drive magmatism in the Western Zone from 1865 -1855 Ma. However, current lack of confirmed arc  
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in th e East Kimberley   |  193 volcanics in the Central Zone at this time shed doubt on this hypothesis, and hence it si not our 
preferred interpretation.  
3.3 ca. 1850 -1830 Ma – Tickalara back -arc basin and the Savannah Event  
As the back arc continued to open, large volumes of mafic -ultrama fic magmatism were emplaced 
at 1860 and 1845 Ma, the vast majority in the latter event (Figure 5c). The 1845 Ma intrusions 
correlate with the Dougalls Suite TTG, suggesting felsic arc magmatism in the Central Zone at this 
time. Data presented here demonstr ate the mafic -ultramafic intrusions were sourced from a 
combination of ambient mantle (E -MORB to primitive mantle) and arc magmas in a back -arc 
environment. The Koongie Park Formation of felsic volcanic and volcaniclastic rocks, together with 
associated Zn -Pb VMS mineralization were emplaced at ca. 1843 Ma, synchronous with these 
intrusions. Underplating of the back -arc rift with voluminous mafic magmatism led to a major 
tectono -thermal event recorded as syn -magmatic granulite facies metamorphism at ca. 184 4 Ma. 
We term this the Savannah Event, corresponding to a relatively short period of voluminous 
magmatism, high -temperature metamorphism and the formation of Ni -Cu and VMS mineralization 
in the back -arc basin. The exact driver behind this event is unclear.  It may have been the 
combination of Paleoproterozoic subduction of potentially young oceanic crust in an ambient 
mantle ca. 100 -200°C hotter than present. Slab -break off would be predicted to occur more readily 
in a warmer mantle due to the hotter, weaker  slab and presence of greater sub -lithospheric melt. 
Other mechanisms such as a slab window or delamination cannot be completely ruled out at this 
stage.  
3.4 ca. 1830 -1800 Ma – The Halls Creek Orogeny  
Mafic -ultramafic intrusions and associated TTG and felsic v olcanic rocks of the Dougalls Suite and 
Koongie Park Formation appear to wane by 1840 -1835 Ma, possibly due to closure of the back -arc 
basin as subduction advanced with the approach of the Eastern Zone and North Australian Craton 
(Figure 5d). Collision of the two blocks amalgamated all zones as they are found today, and led to 
the Halls Creek Orogen marked by the occurrence of the Sally Downs crustal adakites and post -
collisional granites in all zones.  
The 1829 Ma microgranite and mafic dyke presented here are likely members of the Sally Downs 
Supersuite presented in the Savannah district. The Central Zone has the vast majority of these 
granitoid rocks, and this may be due to a reduced crustal thickness compared to the other zones 
from its pre -history as a b ack-arc basin.  
The 1823 Ma Ni -Cu mineralized Copernicus pyroxenite is the first confirmed occurrence of a 
mineralized mafic -ultramafic intrusion outside the 1845 Ma event. The geochemistry of this 
intrusion demonstrates a strong arc character, with little to no evidence of mixing with a more 
mantle -derived material. As a result, we suggest this represents a new, completely different 
intrusive suite which formed in relation to slab break -off associated with continental collision.  
 194   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley Figure 124: Schematic diagram showing our interpretation of the tectonic history of the East Kimberley. 
 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  195 4 The sulfur isotope budget across the margin of 
the Kimberley block  
The MIF -S is a tracer unique to the Archean sedimentary rock record (Farquhar et al., 2000 ), which 
has been us ed to fingerprint input of this sulfur-bearing reservoir to hydrothermal and magmatic 
deposits in the Archean rock record (Agangi et al., 2016 ; Bekker et al., 2009 ; Fiorentini et al., 2012b ; 
LaFlamme et al., 2016 ; Penniston -Dorland et al., 2012 ; Selvaraja et al., in revision ) as well as in 
younger terrains (LaFlamme et al., In Revision ; Selvaraja et al., 2017 ). The characterisation of the 
multiple sulfur isotope signature of mineral systems located along craton margins has the potential 
to pro vide key insights into the processes that occur during the evolution of very ancient lithospheric 
blocks during supercontinent cycles (LaFlamme et al., In Revision ). In this context, the presence of 
∆33S anomalous values in the Proterozoic record along cra ton margins may be utilised as a robust 
and indelible tracer (Farquhar et al., 2000 ) to track the pathway of volatile exchange between an 
Archean craton and its reworked margins (LaFlamme et al., In Revision ; Selvaraja et al., 2017 ), where 
it is empiricall y know that mineral systems are preferentially located (Begg et al., 2010 ). 
The lack of any detectable ∆33S anomaly in the Savannah and Speewah systems is not only in 
agreement with the lack of exposed Archean sedimentary rocks in the vicinity of the deposits, but 
also points to a more fundamental lack of interaction between the m agmas emplaced at 1700 -1800 
Ma and the inferred presence of an unexposed Archean core in the Kimberley Block. On the basis 
of radiogenic isotopes (Re -Os and Sm -Nd), Graham et al. (1999)  inferred the presence of Archean 
lithospheric mantle beneath the Kimbe rley block but also suggested that this mantle was largely 
unaffected during Proterozoic orogenesis. On the basis of the multiple sulfur isotope dataset 
presented here, we argue that if any interaction between Proterozoic magmas and Archean 
lithospheric ma ntle had occurred, evidence of that process would be at least partially recorded in 
the relatively low R factor Savannah system. But this is not the case. More data will have to be 
collected in the future to ascertain whether this is a regional signature o f the entire Proterozoic belt 
in the Kimberley or rather more complexity exists, with the goal to identify areas of endowed 
mineral potential on the basis of the presence of ∆33S anomalous signatures (Selvaraja et al., 2017 ). 
 196   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  5 Application of findings to targeting models for 
intrusion -hosted  Ni-Cu-PGE deposits applicable to 
other craton margin orogenic settings in Western 
Australia, specifically Musgrave and Albany -Fraser 
terranes  
One of the important large -scale finding of this study is the constraint and recognition that 
Paleoproterozo ic arc and collisional environments are prospective and conducive to the formation 
of intrusion -hosted orthomagmatic Ni -Cu deposits. The identification of a major tectono -thermal 
event at ca. 1845 Ma, with syn -magmatic high -temperature metamorphism, and Ni -Cu 
mineralization, is similar to that found in the Fraser range (Clark et al., 2014 ), where the Nova -
Bollinger deposit occurs (Maier et al., 2016 ), suggesting this is a prospective environment which 
should be included in global exploration models. Plume m agmatism is therefore not essential for Ni 
sulfide mineral potential. Whether this arc and collisional setting is perspective only because of the 
relative high temperature of the Paleoproterozoic mantle, is something that needs to be confirmed 
with further  research, but that would be valuable in regards to reducing search space based on age.  
The major driver behind the prospectivity of these intrusions appears to be their large -scale tectono -
magmatic setting, and as a result the entire Central Zone is prosp ective for Savannah and Savannah 
North –style systems. It is unclear whether the occurrence of the Koongie Park Formation and its 
felsic volcanics indicates that rifting was less extensive, or magmas were less focused, or more 
contaminated, in the southern  region of the zone. This region may represent a shallower crustal 
level than that in the Savannah area.  
Further to this 1845 Ma event, is the identification of Copernicus as much younger than Savannah 
at 1823 Ma. This appears to indicate that Copernicus f ormed at the beginning of the Halls Creek 
Orogen, in a post -collisional setting, during the emplacement of the Sally Downs Supersuite. This 
suggests a whole new generation of intrusions synchronous with the Halls Creek Orogen can now 
be considered as poten tially prospective for intrusion -hosted Ni -Cu. On a broader viewpoint, this 
suggests post - to syn -collisional processes, such as slab break -off, may be important and have the 
potential to generate chonolith -style Ni -Cu systems.  
From a more localized standp oint, all mineralized intrusions currently mined (Savannah and 
Savannah North) have a particular geochemistry suggesting a mixed, arc -related origin. The Dave 
Hill and Sub -chamber -D intrusions have similar geochemistry, suggesting that these too have the 
potential to host mineralization. The Turkey Creek Gabbro shows similar geochemistry, to a lesser 
extent, as these intrusions emphasizing its possible prospectivity. However, the age and 
geochemistry of the Copernicus pyroxenite show that Ni -Cu mineralizati on in the Central Zone is 
not restricted to the 1845 Ma event, or a particular geochemistry. As a result, the 1845 Ma mantle -
derived Wilsons and mafic granulites should not be discounted based on their different 
geochemistry.    
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  197 The mineralised intrusions at  Savannah are deep -seated syn -tectonic intrusions dominated by 
cumulate rocks, implying extensive flow through of magma, emplaced at or soon after the peak of 
regional metamorphism. Emplacement depth and, to some extent, intrusion geometry are poor 
indicat ors of prospectivity at initial prediction stage. As exploration advances, particular geometries 
such as keels of blade shaped dykes and boat -shaped or funnel shaped intrusions, and ribbon -like 
or tubular chonoliths, are the most favourable targets, but in  many cases these geometries ( Figure 
125) would only be recognised after extensive drilling, which is unlikely to be carried out unless 
mineralisation is intersected.  
Presence of olivine cumulates, regardless of liquid composition, in suites of small synt ectonic 
intrusions within paragneiss sequences, are attractive exploration targets. Empirically, favourable 
indictors are poikilitic textures, presence of disseminated cumulus chromite, and (obviously) traces 
of interstitial magmatic sulfide. 198   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley    
Figure 125: Typical prospective shapes for intrusion hosted magmatic Ni -Cu-(PGE) deposits, from (Barnes et al., 
2015 ). 
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  199 6 Further Work 
The following studies would allow to further our understanding of the geology and the genetic 
processes at play in the East Kimberley: 
In order to gain a better understanding of timing relationships between the Savannah
and Savannah North mineralised intrusions, and other mafic-ultramafic intrusions
present in their vicinity, such as Dave Hill, Wilsons, Sub-Chamber D, and Copernicus,
it would be very interesting to carry out high precision TIMs dating on the already
available and prepared zircons.
Oxygen isotope analyses could also be carried out on these already made zircon
mounts, which would give us insights on crustal assimilation by the magma, as well
as on the origin (tectonic setting) of the magmas.
Further geochemical, mineralogical and isotopic characterisation (mainly S isotopes
on sulfides when present) of these other intrusions would also allow us to better
assess their prospectivity by gaining knowledge on some of the genetic processes at
play: possible crustal sulfur assimilation, sulfide segregation, and dynamics of the
magmatic systems.
A better characterisation of the Tickalara metamorphic (lithostratigraphy,
geochemistry,  isotopic composition (S, O)) would allow us to better understand
crustal assimilation and better assess its importance or not as a substrate to produce
mineralised mafic-ultramafic intrusions.
A thermochronology study of the Savannah magmatic system would allow us to
identify tectonic domains based on pressure-temperature time paths.
Finally, additional AMS and palaeomagnetic work nearby mafic intrusions, e.g.,
MacIntosh Layered intrusion and the Speewah Dome, would provide a more
comprehensive understanding of the deformation history and remanent magnetism
in the east Kimberley, thus providing constraints for structural and geophysical
exploration.200   |  Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley  Appendix   
Magmatic sulfide mineral potential in the East Kimberley   |  201 Appendix 1: Description of the methodology and the 
samples used for in -situ S isotope analyses a t 
Savannah.  
  Savannah  Work 
Samples were chosen from drillcore  to get a representative  sulfides (blebby, disseminated,  massive) from various phases (pyrrhotite,  pentlandite,  
chalcopyrite).  Sample  locations  (pucks) can be seen in drill core photos. Drilled  out pucked  were mounted  in epoxy.  Mounts were imaged in reflected  light 
and in backscatter  electron.  EDS was acquired from all phases in eac h puck (sample).  Every  phase in each puck was analysed for multiple sulfur isotopes by 
ion probe.
